10 min
The Generality of Muqtada: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i Debate on Multiple Suppositions
Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on multiple suppositions in muqtada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on multiple possible implied meanings, state the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When multiple implied meanings can correct a statement, Hanafis suppose only one; Shafi'is suppose all — leading to differences in whether the statement is ambiguous.

In this lesson, we delve into the disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding muqtada when multiple suppositions (taqdirat) are possible. The Hanafi school holds that only one supposition is necessary to correct the speech, while the Shafi'is argue that all possibilities must be considered. This dispute has significant implications for how we interpret texts like the hadith on error and forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
When a statement requires an implied element and multiple possibilities are equally valid, should we suppose all (Shafi'i) or only one (Hanafi)?
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis say: only one supposition is needed; necessity is measured by its extent. Supposing extra is unnecessary.
Principle: 'Necessity is estimated by its extent' (الضرورات تقدر بقدرها).
3
State the Shafi'i argument
Shafi'is argue that supposing all possibilities is closer to reality; e.g., removing all aspects of error is closer to negating error itself. They prefer the 'closer metaphor' (المجاز الأقرب).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis deny that supposing all is always closer. It may lead to many metaphors, so ambiguity is preferable to forced generalization.
The principle of avoiding excessive metaphor (تقليل المجاز أولى) is invoked.
5
Understand the practical difference
If rulings differ among suppositions, Hanafis consider the speech ambiguous (mujmal); Shafi'is attempt to apply all or seek further evidence. This affects legal interpretation.
Example: The hadith on error — Hanafi: only one ruling (e.g., sin removal) is implied; Shafi'i: all possibilities, making it ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقتضى ما استدعاه صدق الكلام أو صحته من غير أن يكون مذكوراً في اللفظ، أي الأمر الغير المذكور اعتبر لأجل صدق الكلام أو صحته، ولولاه لاختل أحدهما (وعلى هذا فالمحذوف منه فإنه غير مذكور اعتبر لتوقف الصدق أو الصحة عليه وهذا اصطلاح الشافعية والقاضي الإمام أبي زيد منا ( ويمثل بقوله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان فإن صدقه لا يتصوّر إلا بتقدير شيء فإن كان خاصاً أو عاماً بعينه) من دون احتمال آخر (لزم ذلك الخاص أو العام فيقدّر ومنع عمومه لعدم كونه لفظاً) بعد إدخال المحذوف فيه كما وقع عن القاضي الإمام كما وهم ليس بشيء، لأن المقدر كالملفوظ) في الاتصاف بالعموم والخصوص، وأما من فسره بمعنى يفهم التزاماً لأجل تصحيح الكلام أو صدقه، مثل المأكول في لا آكل والبيع في أعتق عبدك عني بألف لا مثل الحديث المذكور، ادعى عدم العموم بناء على كونه غير ملفوظ لا حقيقة ولا تقديراً كما فعل الإمامان فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة وعشيرته الكرام فلا يتوجه إليه هذا الرد ثم مقصودهم من نفي العموم عموم تترتب عليه الأحكام من صحة التخصيص وغيره لا نفي الاستغراق مطلقاً، كيف وقد أجمعوا هم على الحنث بأكل كل مأكول وسيبين المصنف هذا المقتضى ويصرح بأنه لا عموم له، فليس المقصود الرد عليهم، بل على من نفى العموم عنه مع إدخال المحذوف فيه، وأشار في «التحرير» حيث قال : ومنع عمومه هنا لعدم كونه لفظاً ليس بشيء، كقوله ههنا إلى أن المقتضى لا عموم له في غير صورة الحذف هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام (وإن كان ثم تقديرات يصح كل بدلا من حيث إنه مصحح للكلام، يعني يكون بحيث يصح هذا الكلام بواحد أياً كان وبعد اعتبار واحد لا يحتاج إلى آخر فلا يضمر الكل عندنا معاً (خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى فإنه يضمر الكل عنده بل إن اختلفت أحكامها بأن يكون مع تقدير مفيد الحكم، ومع آخر لحكم آخر، ولا معين فمجمل فيتوقف إلى أن يتبين المراد (وإن لم تختلف أحكامها (فالمقدر المنتشر ) يعني الخيار إلى المخاطب في التقدير، ولا يتوقف في العمل، قال في الكشف»، ورأيت في بعض كتب أصحاب الشافعي دل العقل أو الشرع على إضمار شيء في كلام صيانة له عن التكذيب ونحوه، وثمة تقديرات يصح الكلام بأيها كان لا يجوز إضمار الكل، وهو المراد من قوله : المقتضى لا عموم له، أما إذا تعين التقديرات بدليل فيقدر كظهوره في العموم والخصوص حتى لو كان مظهره عاماً كان مقدره كذلك، وكذا لو كان خاصاً، وعلى هذا فلا نزاع فافهم لنا في تقدير (الواحد كفاية) لأجل التصحيح بالفرض فتقدير الزائد من غير ضرورة والضرورة تقدر بقدرها) إذ التقدير إنما كان لضرورة التصحيح ، فلا يقدر الزائد الشافعية قالوا :أولاً : إضمار الكل كرفع أحكام الخطأ أقرب إلى الحقيقة، كرفع ذات الخطأ فإن انتفاء جميع أوصاف الذات أقرب إلى نفي الذات والمجاز الأقرب أولى من الأبعد أقول كليته أي كلية أقربية إضمار الكل ممنوعة، لجواز أن يكون المقتضى في الإثبات نحو : إنما الأعمال بالنيات على أن إضمار الكل كأنه مجازات) كما أن الإضمار والمجاز في مرتبة وقلة المجاز (أولى فههنا مانع عن الحمل على الأقرب، ويحتمل أن يكون معارضة ومن (ههنا أي ومن أجل لزوم كثرة المجاز (قلنا: الإجمال) وإن كان كان خلاف الأصل أولى من التعميم) أي تعميم التقديرات (وقد يجاب تارة كما في «التحرير» بأن الحمل على المجاز الأقرب إنما هو إذا لم ينفه الدليل كون الموجب لإضمار البعض ينفي إضمار الكل لأنه بلا مقتض، أقول) موجب إضمار البعض لا ينفي إضمار الكل مر أنه متى أحد
Al-muqtada is what the truthfulness or correctness of speech demands without being mentioned in the wording, i.e., the unmentioned thing considered for the sake of the truthfulness or correctness of the speech, without which one of them would be defective. According to this, the deleted (element) from it is unmentioned, considered because truthfulness or correctness depends on it. This is the terminology of the Shafi'is and the chief Imam Abu Zayd among us. And it is exemplified by his saying ﷺ: 'The ummah is excused for error and forgetfulness.' For its truthfulness cannot be conceived except by supposing something. If it is specific or general with certainty, without other possibility, that specific or general is necessitated and is supposed. And its generality is prevented because it is not a wording, after including the deleted in it, as occurred from the chief Imam, which is a mistake and is nothing, because the supposed is like the uttered in being described by generality and specificity. As for those who interpreted it as a meaning understood by implication for the sake of correcting speech or its truthfulness, like 'the eaten' in 'I do not eat' and 'the sale' in 'Free your slave from me for a thousand' – not like the mentioned hadith – they claimed lack of generality based on it being not uttered, neither in reality nor by supposition, as did the two imams Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and their noble group. So this refutation does not apply to them. Then their purpose in negating generality is a generality upon which rulings like validity of specification etc. are built, not negation of all-inclusiveness absolutely; how could they all agree on the oath being violated by eating every edible? The author will explain this muqtada and clarify that it has no generality. So the intended refutation is not against them, but against those who negated generality from it while including the deleted in it. He indicated in al-Tahrir where he said: 'And preventing its generality here because it is not a wording is nothing,' like his statement here that the muqtada has no generality in other than the case of deletion. This is how this position should be understood. (And if there are suppositions, each of which is valid as a substitute insofar as it corrects the speech, meaning that this speech is correct by any one, and after considering one, no other is needed, then we do not suppose all together. Contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he supposes all according to him. Rather, if their rulings differ – such that with one supposition a certain legal ruling is useful, and with another a different ruling, and there is no specific determinant – then it is ambiguous and one must wait until the intended meaning is clarified. And if their rulings do not differ, then the supposed is widespread, meaning the choice is left to the addressee in supposition, and action is not suspended. It is stated in al-Kashf: 'I saw in some books of the Shafi'i followers: if reason or revelation indicates the suppression of something in speech to protect it from falsification and the like, and there are suppositions with any of which the speech is correct, it is not permissible to suppose all.' This is what is meant by the statement: 'the muqtada has no generality.' However, if the suppositions are specified by evidence, then it is supposed according to its appearance in generality and specificity, so that if its manifest form is general, its supposed is likewise, and similarly if it is specific. On this, there is no dispute, so understand. For us, supposing one is sufficient for correction by hypothesis; supposing extra without necessity is not done, and necessity is measured by its extent. The Shafi'is said: first, supposing all, like removing the rulings of error, is closer to reality, like removing the essence of error, because the negation of all attributes of the essence is closer to negating the essence, and the closer metaphor is preferable to the farther. I say: the totality of the claim that supposing all is closer is denied, because it is possible that the muqtada in affirmative contexts like 'Actions are by intentions' – the supposing all is like multiple metaphors. Just as suppression and metaphor are of the same rank, and the sparsity of metaphor is preferable, so here there is an obstacle to the closer interpretation. It is possible that there is an objection. And from here, due to the necessity of many metaphors, we say: ambiguity (ijmal), although it is contrary to the original principle, is preferable to generalization (ta'mim). It may also be answered, as in al-Tahrir, that the application of the closer metaphor is only when evidence does not negate it, because the factor that necessitates supposing some negates supposing all since it is without a need. I say: the factor that necessitates supposing some does not negate supposing all; it was mentioned that whenever one...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of al-muqtada (the implied meaning) in usul al-fiqh. It defines muqtada as the unstated element required for the truthfulness (sidq) or correctness (sihha) of a statement. The example given is the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (The ummah is excused for error and forgetfulness). For this statement to be true, something must be implied, such as 'رفع أحكام الخطأ والنسيان' (the rulings of error and forgetfulness are lifted) or 'رفع ذات الخطأ' (the essence of error is lifted). The debate centers on whether such implied elements carry generality. According to the Shafi'is and some Hanafis like Imam Abu Zayd, the implied element is considered like an uttered word in terms of generality/specificity if the deletion is specified. However, other Hanafis (like Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) argue that since the muqtada is not uttered, it has no generality, meaning it does not allow specification (takhsis) or other operations that apply to general words. The text clarifies that the disagreement is only about cases where the deleted element is considered part of the wording; if not, the muqtada indeed has no generality. When multiple suppositions are possible and equally correct, the Hanafis (contrary to al-Shafi'i) do not suppose all simultaneously but only one sufficient for correctness. If the rulings differ according to different suppositions and no evidence determines which, the statement is considered ambiguous (mujmal) until clarification. The Shafi'is argue for supposing all possibilities as more faithful to reality, but the Hanafis respond that this would lead to excessive metaphor, so ambiguity is preferable to forced generalization.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): The unmentioned element required for the truthfulness or correctness of speech.
تقدير (taqdir): The act of mentally supplying the implied element in a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Shafi'i argument for supposing all possible implied meanings (taqdirat) in a statement?
A) It is the only way to avoid ambiguity.
B) It is closer to reality and the closer metaphor is preferable.
C) It is required by the principle of necessity.
D) It is the consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view on multiple taqdirat is based on the principle of necessity; if multiple possibilities have different rulings, the text becomes ambiguous, requiring further evidence.

The Generality of the Muqtada (Implied Meaning)
Clarifying the scope of generality for implied meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether the muqtada has generality and distinguish between linguistic and legal generality.

Core Idea
The muqtada is like the spoken word in language but its legal generality is denied because necessity limits it to one estimation.

This passage delves into a subtle yet crucial concept in Hanafi usul: the muqtada (implied meaning). When a statement requires an estimated element (taqdir) to be true, such as the hadith on error and forgetfulness, scholars debate whether that estimated element carries generality (umum). The commentator clarifies the Hanafi position that the muqtada has linguistic generality but not legal generality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept of muqtada
Understand that muqtada refers to the implied element that must be estimated for speech to be true or valid.
Example: 'I do not eat' implies 'I do not eat meat' – 'meat' is the muqtada.
2
Distinguish two scholarly positions
Some scholars (like al-Qadi Imam) deny any generality because it is not a word. The Hanafi response: the estimated is like the spoken word in being characterized by umum and khusus.
The commentator rejects the absolute denial but acknowledges a valid nuance.
3
Analyze the key phrase 'al-muqaddar ka al-malfuz'
The estimated element is treated like the spoken word in linguistic attributes, so it can be general or specific linguistically.
However, this does not automatically grant it legal generality.
4
Differentiate linguistic umum from legal umum
Linguistic umum is the potential for comprehensive meaning; legal umum is the capacity to be specified (takhsis) and produce rulings over all individuals.
Hanafis affirm linguistic umum but deny legal umum for muqtada because necessity fixes only one estimation.
5
Apply to the oath violation example
If someone swears 'I do not eat' and means 'meat', the oath covers all edibles linguistically, but for legal ruling (ḥanith) they only need to evaluate one specific estimation.
Consensus: he is liable if he eats any food, showing linguistic umum in effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية: أمتي الخطأ والنسيان فإن صدقه لا يتصوّر إلا بتقدير شيء، فإن كان خاصاً أو عاماً بعينه من دون احتمال آخر لزم ذلك الخاص أو العام فيقدّر، ومنع عمومه لعدم كونه لفظاً بعد إدخال المحذوف فيه كما وقع عن القاضي الإمام وقد وهم ليس بشيء، لأن المقدر كالملفوظ في الاتصاف بالعموم والخصوص. وأما من فسره بمعنى يفهم التزاماً لأجل تصحيح الكلام أو صدقه، مثل المأكول في لا آكل والبيع في أعتق عبدك عني بألف لا مثل الحديث المذكور، ادعى عدم العموم بناء على كونه غير ملفوظ لا حقيقة ولا تقديراً كما فعل الإمامان فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة وعشيرته الكرام فلا يتوجه إليه هذا الرد. ثم مقصودهم من نفي العموم عموم تترتب عليه الأحكام من صحة التخصيص وغيره لا نفي الاستغراق مطلقاً، كيف وقد أجمعوا هم على الحنث بأكل كل مأكول. وسيبين المصنف هذا المقتضى ويصرح بأنه لا عموم له، فليس المقصود الرد عليهم، بل على من نفى العموم عنه مع إدخال المحذوف فيه. وأشار في «التحرير» حيث قال: ومنع عمومه هنا لعدم كونه لفظاً ليس بشيء، كقوله ههنا إلى أن المقتضى لا عموم له في غير صورة الحذف. هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام.
The third article on linguistic principles: "My community: error and forgetfulness" – its truth cannot be conceived except by estimating something. If that something is specific or general without another possibility, then that specific or general becomes necessary and is estimated. And its generality is denied because it is not a word after the deletion is included, as occurred from al-Qadi Imam – and it is an error, not valid – because the estimated is like the spoken word in being characterized by generality and particularity. As for one who interpreted it as a meaning understood implicitly for the sake of correcting speech or its truthfulness, like 'the eaten' in 'I do not eat' and 'the sale' in 'I freed your slave for you for a thousand' – not like the mentioned hadith – claiming no generality based on it not being a word, neither in reality nor by estimation, as the two imams Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and their noble associates did, then this rebuttal does not apply to him. Then their purpose in denying generality is the generality upon which legal rulings depend, like validity of specification etc., not absolute universality, how could they when they unanimously agree on the oath violation by eating every eatable. And the author will explain this muqtada and declare that it has no generality, so the purpose is not to rebut them, but rather one who denied its generality while including the deletion in it. He pointed out in al-Tahrir where he said: Denying its generality here because it is not a word is not valid, as his saying here indicates that the muqtada has no generality in cases other than deletion. This is how this context should be understood.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and clarification from the commentator regarding the concept of 'muqtada' (the implied meaning). The discussion centers on statements that require an estimated element (taqdir) to be true, such as the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (Error and forgetfulness are forgiven for my community). The commentator clarifies that the estimated element (al-muqaddar) is treated like the spoken word in terms of possessing generality (umum) and particularity (khhusus). He then distinguishes between two groups of scholars: those who include the omitted element in the text and then deny its generality (this is rejected), and those who interpret the statement as an implicit (iltizami) meaning without considering it as an estimated word – these scholars (like Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) are not subject to this rebuttal. Their denial of generality specifically concerns legal rulings (ahkam) like the validity of specification (takhsis), not absolute universality (istighraq). The commentator concludes by noting that the author (of the matn, likely al-Musallam al-Thubut) will later clarify that the muqtada has no generality except in non-deletion cases.

Key Terms
المقتضى (al-muqtada): The implied or required element in speech that must be estimated (taqdir) for the speech to be true or valid.
التقدير (al-taqdir): The act of mentally estimating an omitted element in speech to make it meaningful or true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the estimated element (al-muqaddar) regarding generality?
A) It is not considered a word, so it has no generality at all.
B) It is like the spoken word in being able to be general or specific.
C) It is general only if it is explicitly spoken.
D) It has generality only for legal rulings, not linguistically.

Key Takeaway
The muqtada has linguistic generality but not legal generality; this distinction is essential for correct usul application.

The Hanafi Rule on Multiple Estimated Elements
Stating the Hanafi rule on estimating multiple possible omissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for estimating omitted elements when multiple possibilities exist, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When multiple estimated elements are possible, Hanafis estimate only one—the addressee chooses if rulings are the same.

In Hanafi usul, when a statement is incomplete and requires an estimated element, the question arises: how many estimates should we make? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi answer: only one estimation is needed unless the rulings differ, in which case the statement becomes ambiguous. We also contrast the Shafi'i view that all possibilities must be estimated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Situation
Recognize when a statement requires an implied element (muqtada) for correctness. The text is incomplete without it.
2
Determine Multiple Possibilities
Check if there are multiple valid estimations that could each complete the statement. If only one, no dispute.
3
Apply Hanafi Rule
If the rulings of the estimated elements are different, the text becomes mujmal (ambiguous) and clarification is needed. If rulings are the same, the muqaddar muntashir applies—choice to addressee.
This avoids unnecessary assumption.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i Position
Al-Shafi'i holds that all possible estimations must be made (idmar al-kull) to preserve the full meaning and avoid falsehood.
Hanafi response: necessity is limited.
5
Understand the Talil
The Hanafi proof: estimation is for necessity, and necessity is measured by its extent. One estimation suffices, so extra is unnecessary.
Principle: al-ḍarūratu tuqaddaru bi-qadarihā.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان ثم تقديرات يصح كل بدلا من حيث إنه مصحح للكلام، يعني يكون بحيث يصح هذا الكلام بواحد أياً كان وبعد اعتبار واحد لا يحتاج إلى آخر فلا يضمر الكل عندنا معاً خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى فإنه يضمر الكل عنده. بل إن اختلفت أحكامها بأن يكون مع تقدير مفيد الحكم، ومع آخر لحكم آخر، ولا معين فمجمل فيتوقف إلى أن يتبين المراد. وإن لم تختلف أحكامها فالمقدر المنتشر يعني الخيار إلى المخاطب في التقدير، ولا يتوقف في العمل. قال في «الكشف»: ورأيت في بعض كتب أصحاب الشافعي دل العقل أو الشرع على إضمار شيء في كلام صيانة له عن التكذيب ونحوه، وثمة تقديرات يصح الكلام بأيها كان لا يجوز إضمار الكل، وهو المراد من قوله: المقتضى لا عموم له. أما إذا تعين التقديرات بدليل فيقدر كظهوره في العموم والخصوص حتى لو كان مظهره عاماً كان مقدره كذلك، وكذا لو كان خاصاً، وعلى هذا فلا نزاع فافهم. لنا في تقدير الواحد كفاية لأجل التصحيح بالفرض فتقدير الزائد من غير ضرورة، والضرورة تقدر بقدرها، إذ التقدير إنما كان لضرورة التصحيح، فلا يقدر الزائد.
And if there are estimations each of which is valid as an alternative, in the sense that the speech is made correct by any one, and after considering one there is no need for another, then we do not estimate all together, contrary to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) who estimates all according to him. Rather, if their rulings differ—such that with one estimation a ruling is beneficial and with another a different ruling, and there is no specific one—then it is ambiguous (mujmal) and one must wait until clarification is given. If their rulings do not differ, then the estimated is indefinite (muntashir), meaning the choice is up to the addressee in estimation, and action is not delayed. He said in al-Kashshaf: I saw in some books of the Shafi'is that reason or law indicates the insertion of something in speech to preserve it from being false and the like, and there are estimations by any of which the speech is valid, but it is not permissible to insert all. This is the meaning of the statement: the muqtada has no generality. If the estimations are determined by a proof, then it is estimated according to its appearance in generality and particularity, such that if its manifest meaning is general, then its estimated meaning is likewise general, and likewise if it is specific. In this there is no dispute—understand. For us, a single estimation suffices for the sake of correction by assumption, so estimating extra is unnecessary, and necessity is estimated to the extent needed. Since the estimation is only due to the necessity of correction, one does not estimate extra.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents a key rule in Hanafi usul regarding iqtida' (implication). When a statement is incomplete and requires an estimated element to be correct, the Hanafi school holds that if multiple possibilities exist, only one estimation is needed—the choice of which is left to the addressee if the rulings are the same. If the rulings differ, the text becomes ambiguous (mujmal) and clarification is needed. This contrasts with the Shafi'i view, which requires that all possible estimations be taken into account. The commentator uses the example from al-Kashshaf to show that even Shafi'is agree that not all estimations are permissible in certain cases. The Hanafi position is based on the principle of necessity: estimation is only for correction, so only the minimum required is done. This avoids unnecessary complication.

Key Terms
المقدر المنتشر (al-muqaddar al-muntashir): An estimated element that is not fixed to a specific word but is left to the choice of the addressee.
إضمار الكل (idmar al-kull): Estimating all possible omitted elements in speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, what happens if there are multiple possible estimations with the same ruling?
A) All are estimated together.
B) The text becomes ambiguous (mujmal).
C) Only one is estimated, chosen by the addressee.
D) The statement is considered invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on multiple estimates balances necessity and practicality, avoiding unnecessary complexity.

Hanafi Preference for Minimal Estimation Over Generalization
Hanafi rebuttals to Shafi'i proofs for universal estimation in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis reject universal estimation (ta'mim) in speech interpretation and apply the principle of necessity to determine the minimal estimated element.

Core Idea
Estimating all omitted elements multiplies metaphors; Hanafis choose ambiguity over generalization.

The Shafi'i school argues that when speech has an implicit omission (idmar), we should estimate all possible omitted elements to stay closest to literal truth. The Hanafi response, presented in this passage, challenges this on multiple grounds: it would lead to an excessive number of metaphors (which are rare in legal language), and the necessity to preserve truthful speech only requires estimating the minimum. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi refutation and the principle that ambiguity (ijmal) is preferable to generalization (ta'mim).

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i argument for universal estimation
Identify the Shafi'i claim that estimating all omissions is closer to literal truth (haqiqah), analogous to negating all attributes of an essence being closer to negating the essence itself. Note their example: 'There is no sultan in the city' implies negation of all sultanic qualities.
This argument relies on the principle that the closest metaphor should be chosen.
2
Identify the Hanafi counter: multiple metaphors
The commentator argues that if we estimate all omissions, we end up with multiple metaphors for a single statement, which violates the principle that metaphor is rare (qillat al-majaz). In affirmative contexts like 'Actions are but by intentions', estimating all would require multiple incompatible metaphors.
The Hanafi position is that estimation should only be as much as necessary to avoid falsehood, not more.
3
Explain the preference for ijmal over ta'mim
The Hanafi response concludes that ambiguity (ijmal) is preferable to generalizing estimates (ta'mim). Even though ijmal is contrary to the default (asl) of clarity, it is chosen to avoid excessive metaphors. This is supported by rebuttals from al-Tahrir and al-Mukhtasar, emphasizing that the cause requiring estimation of some elements does not necessitate estimating all.
The Hanafis hold that the default of non-estimation conflicts with universal estimation, but not with minimal estimation.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i example and Hanafi reinterpretation
The Shafi'i example 'There is no sultan in the city' is dismissed as a particular case based on custom, not a universal proof. The Hanafi commentator reinterprets it as a metaphor: 'sultan' is used to refer to a person who combines all sultanic attributes, so only one estimation (the person) is needed, not multiple estimations of attributes.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of preferring metaphorical interpretation over proliferating estimates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشافعية قالوا: أولاً: إضمار الكل كرفع أحكام الخطأ أقرب إلى الحقيقة، كرفع ذات الخطأ فإن انتفاء جميع أوصاف الذات أقرب إلى نفي الذات والمجاز الأقرب أولى من الأبعد. أقول: كليته أي كلية أقربية إضمار الكل ممنوعة، لجواز أن يكون المقتضى في الإثبات نحو: إنما الأعمال بالنيات على أن إضمار الكل كأنه مجازات كما أن الإضمار والمجاز في مرتبة وقلة المجاز أولى فههنا مانع عن الحمل على الأقرب، ويحتمل أن يكون معارضة ومن ههنا أي ومن أجل لزوم كثرة المجاز قلنا: الإجمال وإن كان خلاف الأصل أولى من التعميم أي تعميم التقديرات. وقد يجاب تارة كما في «التحرير» بأن الحمل على المجاز الأقرب إنما هو إذا لم ينفه الدليل كون الموجب لإضمار البعض ينفي إضمار الكل لأنه بلا مقتض. أقول: موجب إضمار البعض لا ينفي إضمار الكل. وقد يجاب تارة أخرى كما في «المختصر» بأن باب غير الإضمار أكثر. وهذا يقتضي أن لا يضمر شيء، ودليلكم يقتضي أن يضمر الكل فوقع التعارض بينهما وبقي دليل إضمار البعض سالماً فيعمل به. فإن قلت: كثرة باب غير الإضمار كما تعارض دليل إضمار الكل كذلك تعارض دليل إضمار البعض، قلت: لعله بناء على الترجيح بكثرة الأدلة، وأيضاً هذا الدليل إنما يعارض دليل تقدير الكل معاً وأما تقدير البعض فمقطوع غير قابل بأن يعارضه شيء، قال في «الحاشية»: وبهذا يندفع ما أشار إليه بقوله: (ورد) هذا الجواب بأن الكلام على تقدير لزوم الإضمار صوناً عن الكذب في كلام الشارع فلزوم الإضمار مقطوع فلا يعارضه أصالة عدم الإضمار. وجه الدفع أن الصون عن الكذب إنما يقتضي تقدير البعض أياً كان وأما تقدير الزائد فلا يقتضيه الصون، فالأصالة تعارضه، بل تعارض تقدير الكل معاً فيتساقطان ويبقى تقدير البعض فقط سالماً، أي بعض كان فتدبر. وأما ما أجاب هذا الراد بأن التقابل التام بين الإيجاب الكلي والسلب الكلي لا بينه وبين الإيجاب الجزئي فليس بشيء كما لا يخفى. وقالوا: ثانياً: إذا قيل ليس في البلد سلطان فهم نفي جميع الصفات السلطانية من العدل والسياسة وإنفاذ الحكم وغيرها فيقدر الكل. قلنا: هذا مثال جزئي لا يثبت حكماً كلياً، بل ذلك بعرف خاص فيه فلا يقاس عليه غيره من الصور على أن يجوز أن يكون من عموم المقدر أي صفة السلطان لا من قبيل عموم التقديرات فلا يدل على جواز عموم التقديرات مع أنه يحتمل أن يراد بالسلطان صفاته مجازاً إطلاقاً للمحل على الحال فلا يكون من باب التقدير حتى يفيدكم. أقول: ولك أن تمنع الملازمة وهي فهم نفي جميع الصفات عند هذا القول بل المفهوم منه نفي من يجمع هذه الصفات على طريق استعمال السلطان فيه مجازاً من قبيل الاستعارة تشبيهاً للجامع بين الصفات بالسلطان، لا أن ههنا تقديراً لمن الصفات حتى يرد عليه إنه قد لزم حينئذ أيضاً يجمع كثرة التقديرات، فإن المقدر حينئذ من يجمع صفة السلطان وصفة أخرى له وهكذا فافهم. هذه
The Shafi'is said: Firstly: Estimating all (idmar al-kull) is closer to the literal truth, like removing the rulings of error being closer to removing the essence of error, for the negation of all attributes of an essence is closer to negating the essence itself, and the closer metaphor is preferable to the more distant. I say: This whole claim—that estimating all is closer—is denied, for it is possible that the muqtada in affirmative contexts like 'Actions are but by intentions' is that estimating all would be multiple metaphors, and metaphor is less frequent; so there is a preventer here from taking the closer, and there may be a counterargument. From here—i.e., due to the necessity of many metaphors—we say: Ambiguity (ijmal) is preferable to generalization (ta'mim) of estimations, even though ambiguity is contrary to the default. And a response is sometimes given as in al-Tahrir: The closer metaphor is taken only if the proof does not negate it; the cause requiring estimation of some negates estimation of all because it is without need. I say: The cause requiring estimation of some does not negate estimation of all. And another response is given as in al-Mukhtasar: The category of non-estimation is larger. This would require that nothing be estimated, while your proof requires that all be estimated, so they conflict, and the proof for estimating some remains sound and is acted upon. If you say: The larger category of non-estimation conflicts with the proof for estimating some just as it conflicts with estimating all, I say: Perhaps it is based on preponderance by multiplicity of proofs; also, this proof only conflicts with the proof for estimating all together, while estimation of some is definitive and cannot be opposed by anything. He said in al-Hashiya: This repels what was indicated by his saying: The rebuttal of this answer is that the speech is considered on the assumption that estimation is necessary to preserve from falsehood in the speech of the Lawgiver, so the necessity of estimation is definite and is not opposed by the default of non-estimation. The repelling basis is that the preservation from falsehood only requires estimation of some, any some, while estimation of extra is not required by preservation, so the default opposes it—rather, it opposes estimating all together, so they cancel out, and only the estimation of some remains valid, whichever some. Reflect. As for this rebutter's answer that complete opposition exists between universal affirmation and universal negation, not between it and partial affirmation, it is nothing, as is clear. And they said: Secondly: If it is said 'There is no sultan in the city', it is understood as the negation of all sultanic attributes, like justice, governance, execution of judgments, etc., so all are estimated. We say: This is a particular example that does not establish a universal ruling; rather, it is based on a specific custom, so it should not be analogized to other cases. Moreover, it may be from the generality of the estimated (i.e., the sultan's attributes) and not from the generality of estimations, so it does not prove the permissibility of universal estimation. And it is possible that by 'sultan' his attributes are meant metaphorically, as place is used for occupant, so it is not from the category of estimation at all. I say: You may deny the implication—that is, understanding the negation of all attributes from this statement—rather, what is understood is the negation of one who combines these attributes, by way of using 'sultan' metaphorically as a simile for the combiner of attributes. There is no estimation of the attributes here, otherwise it would lead again to multiple estimations. Understand. This is the end.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage continues the usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the estimation of omitted elements in speech. The Shafi'is present two arguments: first, that estimating all possible omissions is closer to the literal truth (haqiqah), analogous to negating all attributes being closer to negating the essence; second, an example from common speech: 'There is no sultan in the city' implies negation of all sultanic qualities. The Hanafi commentator refutes both. For the first, he argues that often the implied meaning (muqtada) in affirmations (like 'Actions are by intentions') would require multiple metaphors if all were estimated, and metaphor is less frequent—so ambiguity (ijmal) is preferred over generalization (ta'mim). He also supplies alternative rebuttals from al-Tahrir and al-Mukhtasar, emphasizing that estimation is only to the extent of necessity. For the second example, he denies that universal estimation follows, interpreting 'sultan' metaphorically as a person who embodies all qualities, so only one estimation suffices. The exposition demonstrates the Hanafi preference for minimal estimation and cautious use of metaphor.

Key Terms
الإجمال (al-ijmal): Ambiguity in meaning that requires clarification; here it is preferred over generalization of estimations.
التعميم (al-ta'mim): Generalization; here it means applying all possible estimations universally.
الإضمار (al-idmar): Estimation or implication of an omitted element in speech; a key concept in usul al-fiqh for interpreting elliptical expressions.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical or figurative language; its rarity (qillat al-majaz) is a Hanafi principle used to limit interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the primary reason Hanafis prefer ambiguity (ijmal) over generalization (ta'mim) in estimation?
A) They believe ambiguity is always clearer.
B) Universal estimation leads to multiple metaphors, which is rare in legal language.
C) They reject estimation entirely.
D) The Shafi'i example is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
Hanafus limit estimation to the minimum needed to preserve truthful meaning, preferring ambiguity over excessive metaphor.

Does the Concept of Opposition Have Generality?
Debate on whether the concept of opposition implies general application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key points of the dispute regarding the generality of mafhum al-mukhalafah and evaluate the Hanafi position that it lacks general application.

Core Idea
Mafhum al-mukhalafah lacks generality because it is derived from silence, not from a word with uniform indication.

This passage delves into a subtle usul dispute about whether the concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafah) has general application. The author navigates between the Hanafi position and that of Imam al-Ghazali, resolving that the dispute is substantive, centering on the uniformity of indication. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to distinguish between textual implications and logical necessities in legal derivation.

Guided Path
1
Define the concept of opposition
Recall that mafhum al-mukhalafah is the implied meaning from a statement that the ruling applies only to the mentioned case, and the opposite applies to the unmentioned. For example, 'In grazing sheep there is zakat' implies no zakat on non-grazing sheep.
This is a contested source of rulings; Hanafis reject it as a valid indicator.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents: (1) The Hanafi position: the concept has no generality because it is not indicated by a word. (2) Al-Ghazali's view: attributes the negation of the ruling on the unmentioned to the original principle (asl), not to the concept itself. (3) The majority: affirm generality for the concept.
The author argues that al-Ghazali's position is essentially the same as the Hanafi one.
3
Analyze the nature of the dispute
Some claim the dispute is merely verbal (al-niza' al-lafzi), i.e., whether to call the concept 'general'. The author refutes this, showing it is substantive because it affects legal reasoning.
The substantive issue is whether the concept's indication is uniform (tasabuh al-dalalah).
4
Examine the criterion of uniform indication
The author concludes that the real question is whether the concept indicates all unmentioned cases uniformly. If uniform, it is general; if varied, it is not. He illustrates with qisas: the negation of qisas in erroneous killing is clearer than in quasi-intentional killing, showing variation.
Hanafis argue that since the concept is not a word, its indication cannot be uniform.
5
Apply to a concrete example
Take the statement 'On grazing sheep there is zakat.' The concept implies no zakat on non-grazing sheep. Hanafis say this negation is simply due to the absence of any evidence for zakat, not a general principle. Proponents of the concept treat it as a general rule applicable to all non-grazing livestock.
This difference affects how rulings are derived for new cases (e.g., goats, camels).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة لمفهوم المخالفة عند قائليه عموم لجميع ما وراء المنطوق (خلافاً للغزالي) الإمام حجة الإسلام ( فقيل) : النزاع (لفظي يعود إلى أن العام هل هو ما استغرق في محل النطق) وبه يقول الإمام حجة الإسلام، فنفي العموم عنه (أو) ما استغرق في الجملة سواء كان في محل النطق أو غيره، كما يقول به الجمهور فأثبتوا العموم إذ لا خلاف لأحد من قائلي المفهوم (في ثبوت نقيض الحكم لا في محل النطق عموماً بل الخلاف إنما هو في إطلاق لفظ العام عليه، هذا بأن كلامه لا يساعده والظاهر من كلامه أن يبنى على عدم كونه لفظاً (و) قال (في «التحرير»: جاز أن يقول الإمام الغزالي بثبوت النقيض للمسكوت على العموم، وينسبه إلى الأصل لا إلى المفهوم) بأن لا يكون للفظ دلالة على ثبوت الحكم فيما وراء المنطوق لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً، فيبقى المسكوت على ما كان قبل، فينتفي الحكم لعدم مقتضيه فلا يكون من العموم في شيء إذ لا بد فيه من دلالة وهذا كطريق (الحنفية) النافين للمفهوم بعينه (أقول أولاً : الكلام بعد تسليم المفهوم وهذا بالحقيقة إنكار له (و) أقول (ثانياً النسبة) أي نسبة ثبوت النقيض في المسكوت (عموماً إلى الأصل لا يصح، إذ ربما يكون المفهوم وجودياً) فلا يمكن إسناده إلى الأصل وهذا أيضاً لا يصح عموماً، فإن بعض الوجوديات أيضاً ينسب إلى الأصل، لكن لا يضر المورود والإيرادان لا يتوجهان إليه أصلاً، فإنه من أين علم أن هذا الحبر الإمام تكلم بعد تسليم المفهوم وعبارته المنقولة في التحرير من المستصفى النزاع عائد إلى أن العموم من عوارض الألفاظ خاصة أم لا، فإن من يقول بالمفهوم قد يظن للمفهوم عموماً ويتمسك به وفيه نظر، لأن العام لفظ تتشابه دلالته بالإضافة إلى المسميات والتمسك بالمفهوم ليس تمسكاً بلفظ، بل بسكوت فإذا قال في سائمة الغنم زكاة فنفي الزكاة عن المعلوفة ليس بلفظ حتى يعم أو يخص، ويجوز أن يكون حاصله أن القائل بالمفهوم يظنه عاماً ويتمسك به وفيه نظر فإنه يصح عندنا إذ ليس اللفظ هناك دالاً عليه كما زعموا بل هو تمسك بالسكوت، فإن المسكوت يبقى على ما كان والأصل في الأحكام العدم، فيلزم انتفاء الحكم، فلا يكون عاماً ولا يصلح للتمسك به، ثم ليس المقصود أن لكل كلام مفهوماً عاماً ينسبه إلى الأصل، بل لا المقصود أن المفهوم لو كان ثبت بالسكوت لا بدلالة اللفظ فاندفع الثاني أيضاً، وعلى هذا فالنزاع معنوي مبني على خلاف آخر معنوي فتدبر (وقيل) ليس النزاع لفظياً (بل النزاع في أن العموم ملحوظ المتكلم فيقبل التجزي) والخصوص في الإرادة (أولاً) ملحوظ للمتكلم (بل) هو (لازم) عقلي) كالمأكول في لا آكل عند الحنفية فلا يقبله وهو مراد) الإمام (الغزالي) قدس سره، فالنزاع في العموم القابل للتجزي فأثبته الجمهور، وأنكره هذا البحر القمقام قدس سره، وإن تذكرت تحقيق ما قد سلف يعينك على فهم هذا (وأورد) عليه أن كلامه لا يتحمل هذا التوجيه حيث قال في ردّهم أي رد القائلين بعموم المفهوم لأن العام لفظ تتشابه دلالته، والتمسك بالمفهوم ليس تمسكاً باللفظ بل بسكوت فإن ظاهره أن المناط أن المعاني لا تتصف بالعموم لا كونه ملحوظ ،المتكلم وأيضاً يرد عليه أن كون المفهوم غير ملحوظ للمتكلم غير معقول على تقدير القول به، فإذا كان دلالة اللفظ عليه بالوضع كان المتكلم ملاحظاً له مستعملاً للفظ فيه فالعموم فيه لو كان قابلاً للتجزي والخصوص كما في سائر الألفاظ العامة وأيضاً الحكم على الشيء من غير اتصاف ما يغايره بنقيضه معقول، فلا يكون المفهوم لازماً عقلياً، ولو حرر كلام القائل بأن العموم استغراق يقصد من اللفظ ولا دلالة هنا للفظ، بل قد يفهم بالسكوت عن الحكم عليه انتفاء الحكم كما تفهم اللوازم العقلية لآل إلى ما في «التحرير»، ولا يرد عليه شيء (أقول) ليس النزاع كما زعموا بل النزاع في أن المفهوم هل تتشابه (دلالته على الأفراد فيكون عاماً فإن تشابه الدلالة معتبر فيه أو تتفاوت) الدلالة عليها (فلا يكون) عاماً والفحوى يجوز أن يتفاوت) في الانفهام فإن قولك في القتل العمد قود دلالته على عدمه في الخطأ تفاوت دلالته على عدمه في شبه (العمد فإنها في الأول أظهر دون الثاني (فافهم) وفيه نظر فإن الدلالة على المفهوم وضعي، ولا شك أن تساوي نسبة الأفراد إليه من لوازمه، فلا يمكن كون الدلالة على أفراد المسكوت متفاوتة، وإن كان التفاوت من خارج فلا يضر العموم، كما أن دلالة العام على سبب نزوله أقوى منها على ما سواه، فإن قيل : المقصود أن ليس دلالته عليه بالوضع فلا يتشابه قلت: هذا بالحقيقة إنكار للمفهوم، وقد كان على زعمه الكلام بعد التسليم فتدبر .
Question: For the concept of opposition according to its proponents, it has generality for all that is beyond the spoken expression, contrary to al-Ghazali, the Imam Hujjat al-Islam. It was said: The dispute is verbal, returning to whether the general is what encompasses only in the place of utterance, as the Imam Hujjat al-Islam holds, thus negating generality from it; or what encompasses in general, whether in the place of utterance or other, as the majority hold, thus affirming generality, since there is no disagreement among proponents of the concept regarding the affirmation of the opposite of the ruling, not generally in the place of utterance, but rather the disagreement is only in applying the term 'general' to it. He said in al-Tahrir: It is possible that Imam al-Ghazali affirms the opposite for the unmentioned generally and attributes it to the principle, not to the concept, such that the word has no indication of the ruling beyond the spoken, neither negation nor affirmation, so the unmentioned remains as it was before, thus the ruling is negated due to the absence of its requirement, and it is no part of generality at all, since generality requires indication, and this is exactly the way of the Hanafis who negate the concept. I say: First, this speech is after conceding the concept, and this is in reality a denial of it. Second, the attribution [of the negation] generally to the principle is invalid, because sometimes the concept is existential, so it cannot be attributed to the principle, and this also is not valid generally, for some existential things are also attributed to the principle, but that does not harm. The two objections do not apply to him at all, for how does he know that this learned Imam spoke after conceding the concept? His transmitted statement in al-Tahrir from al-Mustasfa: The dispute returns to whether generality is an accident of words specifically or not. For one who holds the concept may imagine that the concept has generality and adheres to it, but there is consideration, because the general is a word whose indication is uniform regarding the named things, and adherence to the concept is not adherence to a word, but to silence. So when he says, 'On grazing sheep there is zakat,' the negation of zakat for the non-grazing is not by a word that can be general or specific. It is possible that its outcome is that the proponent of the concept imagines it is general and adheres to it, but there is consideration, for it is valid according to us, since the word is not indicative of it as they claim, but rather it is adherence to silence. For the silent remains as it was, and the principle in rulings is negation, so the negation of the ruling follows, hence it is not general nor suitable for adherence. Then the intention is not that every speech has a concept generally attributed to the principle; rather the intention is that if the concept were established by silence and not by word indication, then the second [objection] is also repelled. Upon this, the dispute is substantive, built upon another substantive difference, so reflect. And it was said: The dispute is not verbal, but rather the dispute is about whether generality is intended by the speaker and thus admits division and particularity in the will, or it is not intended by the speaker but is a rational necessity, like 'I do not eat' which for the Hanafis does not admit division, and that is the intent of Imam al-Ghazali. So the dispute is about generality that admits division; the majority affirmed it, and this great sea of knowledge denied it. If you recall the investigation that has preceded, it will help you understand this. And an objection was raised against him that his speech cannot bear this interpretation, for he said in refuting those who affirm generality for the concept: 'Because the general is a word whose indication is uniform, and adherence to the concept is not adherence to a word but to silence.' For its apparent meaning is that the criterion is that meanings are not described with generality, not whether it is intended by the speaker. Also, it is objected that the concept not being intended by the speaker is unintelligible on the assumption of holding to it; for if the word's indication of it is by convention, then the speaker intends it and uses the word for it, so generality in it would be subject to division and particularity as in other general words. Also, the judgment of a thing without attributing its opposite to what differs from it is rational, so the concept is not a rational necessity. If one corrects the statement that generality is a comprehensiveness intended from the word, and there is no indication here from the word, but rather it is understood from silence about the ruling the negation of the ruling as rational necessities are understood, it would lead to what is in al-Tahrir, and nothing is objected against it. I say: The dispute is not as they claimed, but rather the dispute is about whether the concept's indication of individuals is uniform, thus it is general, because uniformity of indication is considered in it, or it varies, thus it is not general. And the implication may vary in understanding. For your saying in premeditated murder, 'Qisas,' its indication of its negation in erroneous killing varies from its indication of negation in quasi-intentional killing, for the former is clearer but not the latter. So understand. There is consideration: for the indication of the concept is conventional, and there is no doubt that equal proportion of individuals to it is among its necessities, so it is impossible for the indication of the individuals of the silent to vary, and if the variation is external, it does not harm generality, just as the indication of the general regarding its cause of revelation is stronger than for other than it. If it is said: The intention is that its indication is not by convention, so it is not uniform—I say: This is in reality a denial of the concept, and according to his assumption, the speech was after conceding it, so reflect.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute concerning the generality (عموم) of the concept of opposition (مفهوم المخالفة). The author presents the view of Imam al-Ghazali, who denies that the concept of opposition has general application, and the view of the majority who affirm it. Initially, the dispute is described as verbal: is the term 'general' applicable to the concept? The author quotes from al-Tahrir to show that Ghazali attributes the negation of the ruling on the unmentioned to the original principle (عدم المقتضي) rather than to a general indication from the word. This aligns with the Hanafi position that the concept is not indicated by the word but by silence, and thus cannot be considered general. The author then argues that the dispute is actually substantive, not verbal, because it concerns whether the generality is intended by the speaker and admits division, or is a rational necessity like the implication of 'I do not eat' for the Hanafis. He refutes objections against his interpretation and concludes that the real issue is whether the concept's indication of its individuals is uniform (تَشَابُه الدَّلالَة) – if uniform, it is general; if varied, it is not. He provides an example from qisas to illustrate variation in understanding. Finally, he notes that if one denies conventional indication for the concept, it amounts to denying the concept altogether, contradicting the assumption that it is conceded. This discussion is essential for understanding how Hanafi usul treats logical implications versus textual indications.

Key Terms
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhūm al-mukhālafah): The implied meaning derived from a statement that the ruling applies to the mentioned case only, understood from silence about other cases.
عموم (ʿumūm): Generality, i.e., the inclusion of all individuals under a ruling indicated by a word with uniform indication.
النزاع اللفظي (al-nizāʿ al-lafẓī): A verbal dispute that reduces to a disagreement about terminology, not about substantive legal effect.
تشابه الدلالة (tasābuh al-dalālah): Uniformity of indication, meaning that the word or expression indicates all its referents equally without variation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position described in the passage, why does the concept of opposition not have generality?
A) Because the concept is indicated by a word that is not general.
B) Because the concept is understood from silence, and silence does not have the uniform indication required for generality.
C) Because Imam al-Ghazali denied it.
D) Because the majority of scholars affirm it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school denies generality to mafhum al-mukhalafah because it is derived from silence, not from a word with uniform indication.

Rule of Conjunction and Restriction in Defective Sentences
Debate on conjunction and restriction for defective sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a conjoined defective sentence takes on the restriction of the preceding sentence, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of tashrik.

Core Idea
Conjunction implies partnership – a restricted ruling must be shared with a conjoined defective sentence.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is: when a defective sentence (jumlah naqisa) is conjoined to a preceding qualified sentence, does it automatically take on that qualification? The Hanafis say yes, based on the principle that conjunction (ataf) implies full partnership (tashrik) in the ruling, including its restrictions. The Shafi'is object using grammatical examples and the consensus of grammarians. We will work through the arguments and see how this usul difference leads to divergent fiqh rulings on qisas for a dhimmi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed rule
What exactly is the usul principle in question? It concerns a defective sentence (jumlah naqisa) conjoined to a preceding sentence that has a restriction (qayd). Does the restriction carry over to the conjoined sentence?
The passage focuses on the phrase 'wa la dhu 'ahdin fi 'ahdihi' as the conjoined defective sentence.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi position: yes, the restriction carries over. The proof is 'al-'ataf li al-tashrik' – conjunction is for partnership; therefore, to preserve partnership in the restricted ruling, the restriction must apply to the conjoined sentence.
The Hanafis derive this from the wider context of the hadith on killing a dhimmi.
3
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'is object using the example 'I hit Zaid on Friday and Amr' – they argue that the time restriction does not automatically apply to 'Amr' because grammarians universally reject such carryover. Also, the conjoined phrase may not be defective.
The Shafi'is argue that the conjoined sentence 'Amr' is complete, not defective, so the rule does not apply.
4
Analyse the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes: the rule applies only when the conjoined sentence is defective (cannot stand alone without the restriction). In the example, 'Amr' can stand alone, so it is not analogous. Moreover, the grammarians' authority does not override mujtahid derivation.
The passage notes that the conjoined sentence 'wa la dhu 'ahdin fi 'ahdihi' is defective because without 'fi 'ahdihi' the sentence is incomplete.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is the ruling on qisas: Hanafis use the general qisas verses (2:178) to permit killing a Muslim for killing a dhimmi, while Shafi'is restrict based on the hadith 'la yuqtalu mu'minun bi kafirin' interpreting that the restriction 'fi 'ahdihi' does not extend to the first part.
The Hanafi position leads to a broader application of qisas.

Source Text

Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses a usul dispute regarding the rule of conjunction (ataf) for defective sentences. The Hanafis argue that when a defective sentence is conjoined to a preceding qualified sentence, it takes on the same qualification to maintain partnership in the ruling. The Shafi'is object that this conflicts with the practice of grammarians and provide counterexamples. The commentator defends the Hanafi position, noting that the rule is derived from a subsidiary issue about killing a dhimmi. He concludes that the correct view is that the conjoined sentence only takes the restriction if it cannot stand without it, and that the Shafi'i objections are not valid.

Key Terms
العطف (al-'ataf): Grammatical conjunction, combining two elements with a sharing of ruling.
التشريك (al-tashrik): Partnership or sharing in the ruling between the conjoined and the base.
التقييد (al-taqyid): Restriction or qualification of a ruling by a condition or circumstance.
الجملة الناقصة (al-jumlah al-naqisa): A defective sentence that cannot stand alone without a complement to complete its meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The conjoined sentence does not take the restriction.
B) The conjoined sentence takes the restriction only if it is general (amm).
C) The conjoined sentence takes the restriction because conjunction implies partnership.
D) The conjoined sentence takes the restriction only if the context demands it.

Key Takeaway
A defective sentence (jumlah naqisa) when conjoined to a preceding restricted sentence takes on that restriction because conjunction implies full partnership in the ruling.

Definition of Takhsis and Distinction from Naskh
Defining takhsis and addressing objections about naskh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define takhsis, distinguish it from naskh, and resolve the objection about sequential specifications.

Core Idea
Takhsis restricts intention from the start; naskh lifts a ruling after full intention.

This passage defines the usul term takhsis (specification) and addresses a key confusion with naskh (abrogation). The author also tackles a subtle objection about multiple sequential specifications, showing how intention operates in singular vs multiple usages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the formal definition of takhsis in the text: 'qasr al-'amm 'ala ba'd musammayatih fi al-irada' and note the variation that includes mutlaq.
The key phrase is 'fi al-irada' (in intention), which distinguishes it from naskh.
2
Distinguish takhsis from naskh
Analyze the objection that the definition does not exclude naskh of some individuals. Understand the response: in naskh, the whole was originally intended then part removed; in takhsis, only part was intended from the beginning.
The difference lies in the initial intention, not just the resulting scope.
3
Analyze the objection about second specification
Consider the scenario where a second specification removes an individual that was included after the first. The objection claims this is not 'restriction from the beginning'.
This tests whether the definition handles sequential specifications.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author argues that if usage is singular, intention is singular: only the final set is meant from the start. If usages are multiple, each usage has its own intention, making the second a specification relative to that usage.
This preserves the definition by linking restriction to the intention at the time of each usage.

(Specifications) Including it general with specific. (It) i.e. specification is restricting the general to some of its individuals in intent. (And it may be said) specification is restricting the word absolutely, general or non-general, to some of its individuals, thus including restriction of the unqualified. (It was said regarding the restriction) in the definition that it is deficient because it does not exclude abrogation of some (individuals) for that is restriction to some individuals of the general. (And it was answered) that there it is not restriction to some, because it is intending some from the beginning rather the whole was intended there, then some (i.e. its ruling) was lifted, unlike specification, for it is restriction in the aforementioned sense. Then it was objected that the refutation regarding the second state remains, i.e. the second specification, for the individual excluded by the second specification was included at the time of the first specification, then excluded, so it is not restriction, thus it exits from the defined. (I say: the usage) of the general is only one, so the intention is not multiple such that first what remains from the first specification is intended then some of it, i.e. what remains from the second, but from the beginning of the whole matter what remains after the two specifications is intended, so restriction applies there. And if usage is multiple, intention becomes multiple, so in one usage what remains after the first specification is intended, and in another what remains after the two specifications, so it is specification relative to this usage and not the first. (And then it is permissible that the abrogator be in a usage) that it is from the definition.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defines the usul term 'takhsis' (specification). The author explains that takhsis is restricting a general term (amm) to some of its individuals in the intention of the speaker. Some define it more broadly to include restriction of unqualified (mutlaq) terms. The key issue is distinguishing takhsis from naskh (abrogation). In naskh, part of the general is intended from the beginning but later removed; in takhsis, only some individuals were ever intended. The passage also addresses an objection about sequential specifications: if a second specification removes something that was included after the first, it appears to be a new restriction. The author resolves this by arguing that if the usage is singular, the intention is singular and only the final set of individuals is meant from the start. If usages are multiple, each usage has its own intention, making it specification relative to that usage.

Key Terms
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Restricting a general term to some of its individuals in the intention.
النسخ (al-naskh): Lifting a previously established ruling, which may involve removing part of a general term's application.
القصر (al-qasr): The act of restricting the scope of a term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how is takhsis different from naskh (abrogation)?
A) Takhsis is broader than naskh.
B) In naskh, the whole was initially intended then part is removed; in takhsis, only some were intended from the start.
C) Takhsis applies only to commands, naskh applies to prohibitions.
D) Takhsis requires a dalil, naskh does not.

Key Takeaway
Takhsis is an initial restriction of intention, not a later removal; the distinction from naskh hinges on the intention at the time of revelation.

The Hanafi Definition of Takhsis: Mustaqill Muqarin Only
Explaining the Hanafi condition for takhsis via independent and concurrent mukhassis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define takhsis according to the Hanafi school, identify the conditions of mustaqill and muqarin, and distinguish non-independent mukhassises from those that effect takhsis.

Core Idea
Takhsis is only by an independent concurrent mukhassis; non-independent qualifiers do not restrict the general.

The Hanafi school uniquely restricts the concept of takhsis (specification) to cases where a general term is limited by an independent and concurrent evidence. Non-independent qualifiers such as exception, condition, and attribute are not considered mukhassises. This passage clarifies the school's position and refutes the view that the dispute with the Shafi'is is merely terminological.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition of takhsis
Define takhsis as restricting the general (العام) to a part (بعض) by an independent concurrent mukhassis (المستقل المقارن). Note the two conditions: independence (mustaqill) and concurrence (muqarin).
Independence means the mukhassis has a separate meaning opposing the generality; concurrence means it is mentioned together with the general utterance.
2
Identify the five non-independent mukhassises
List istithna (exception), sifat (attribute), shart (condition), badal al-ba'd (substitute of some), and ghayah (limit). According to the text, these do not effect takhsis according to Hanafis.
These are called ghayr mustaqill because they are part of the same sentence and do not have an independent existence.
3
Refute the Shafi'i view that the dispute is verbal
Explain the author's argument that it is substantive (ma'nawi) by showing how each non-independent mukhassis leads to logical absurdity if considered a restrictor. Use the example of 'Honor the men if they are Hashimites' – if it restricted, it would mean 'Honor the Hashimite men if they are Hashimites' which is redundant.
The author asserts that custom ('urf) understands these constructions differently, preserving the generality of the original term.
4
Distinguish Hanafi vs Shafi'i interpretation
Contrast that Hanafis keep the general term general and suspend the ruling for all individuals, only executing it for those meeting the condition. Shafi'is consider the general restricted from the start. This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
Example: 'Honor the scholarly men' – Hanafis: command applies to all men, but only executed for scholars; Shafi'is: command addresses only scholars.
5
Summarize the epistemological weight
Note that the Hanafi position is based on linguistic custom and logical consistency, and the text claims it is 'the truth'. Only independent mukhassises (e.g., another general statement, rational proof, or specific text) truly restrict the general.
Delayed mukhassis is considered naskh (abrogation), not takhsis.

As for the independent, it conveys a meaning opposing the general ruling in some, so it is known that the intended meaning is the part from the beginning, so there is restriction, and nothing of what was mentioned in non-independent is necessary, as is not hidden. Commentary
This passage is an extended argument by the commentator (likely from a sharh of Muslim al-Thubut) defending the Hanafi position on the definition of takhsis (specification). The Hanafi school holds that takhsis (restricting a general term to some of its individuals) only occurs through a 'mustaqill muqarin' — an independent and concurrent mukhassis. Independent means the mukhassis has a separate meaning that opposes the generality, and concurrent means it is mentioned together with the general at the time of utterance, not delayed. The text explains that non-independent mukhassises like istithna (exception), sifat (attribute), shart (condition), badl al-ba'd (substitute of some), and ghaya (limit) do not effect takhsis in the Hanafi view. The author then refutes the Shafi'i claim that the dispute is merely verbal (lafzi), arguing it is substantive (ma'nawi). He provides examples: if a condition restricted the general, saying 'Honor the men if they are Hashimites' would imply the intended men are only the Hashimites, leading to redundancy. Instead, the Hanafi interpretation is that the condition suspends the ruling for all individuals, but the ruling is only executed for those meeting the condition; the general remains general in scope. Similarly for attributes, the general term is first taken broadly, then restricted, and then its generality applies only to the restricted class, not a restriction of the original general. The conclusion is that only when the mukhassis is independent (like another general statement, a specific text, or a rational proof) does it truly specify the general from the beginning, and this is valid only if concurrent. The later part mentions that a delayed mukhassis is actually naskh (abrogation), not takhsis. The passage ends with a note that the author's view is what he has explained.

Key Terms
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Restricting a general term (العام) to some of its individuals by an independent concurrent mukhassis.
المستقل المقارن (al-mustaqill al-muqarin): A mukhassis that is independent in meaning (separate from the general) and concurrent in time with the general utterance.
غير المستقل (ghayr al-mustaqill): A mukhassis that is not independent, such as exception, attribute, condition, substitute of some, or limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Exception (istithna)
B) An independent textual evidence (dalil mustaqill)
C) Rational proof ('aql)
D) Another general statement (amm) that specifies

Key Takeaway
Takhsis in the Hanafi school is exclusively through a mustaqill muqarin; non-independent qualifiers like exception, condition, and attribute do not restrict the general term.

The Rational and Linguistic Permissibility of Takhsis
Arguing that takhsis is rationally possible and linguistically attested

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on takhsis (specification) and evaluate the objection that takhsis entails lying or a change of mind.

Core Idea
Takhsis is rationally permissible and linguistically attested; specified statements are metaphorically true.

This passage addresses a foundational usul question: can a general statement be specified without contradicting its truth? The Hanafi school affirms that specification is rationally possible and occurs in language, proven by induction. It then defends this position against the objection that specification would make the original statement a lie or imply a change of mind, ultimately showing that specified speech is true metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
The passage opens with the ruling: takhsis is permissible rationally and occurs in language. This is the core position being defended.
2
Understand the opponents' objection
Opponents argue that takhsis would make the statement a lie (kadhib) or imply a change of mind (bada'), which is unfitting for a rational speaker.
These objections stem from a literalist reading: if you say 'every human is mortal' then later exclude Socrates, it seems you lied.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The rebuttal distinguishes between reports (khabar) and constructs (insha'). The dispute only concerns reports. For constructs, every construct entails a report, but that is not the focus. The key counter is that specified speech is true metaphorically (majaz), even if not literally true.
Metaphorical truth does not require literal correspondence; the statement is true in a transferred sense.
4
Recognize the inductive proof
The Hanafi proof for occurrence of takhsis is induction (istiqra'). By surveying language, we find many examples of specification, proving its possibility.
Induction is a valid source of certainty (qat'i) in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(التخصيص جائز عقلاً) أي لا يحيل العقل وقوع التخصيص بمخصصات من الكلام وغيره (وواقع) في اللغة (استقراء خلافاً لشذوذ لا يعبأ بخلافهم (قالوا) في الاستدلال (إنه كذب فلا يليق أن يتفوه به عاقل وفي شرح الشرح» إنه كذب (أو بداء) وإنما زدا هذا ليشمل الإنشاء) ويثبت المدعي بتمامه ولم يكن شاملاً إياه من قبل هذه الزيادة لأن الكذب لا يكون في الإنشاء بل مختص بالخبر ودفع بأن الخلاف ليس إلا في الخبر على ما صرح به الآمدي وغيره كما في التيسير فهذه الزيادة ضارّة له فلا تصح (أقول : من ههنا أي من أجل أن الخلاف في الخبر فقط تبين ضعف ما قيل ويمكن الجواب عن عدم شمول الدليل للإنشاء بأن كل إنشاء يلزمه (خبر) فلو وقع التخصيص فيه يلزم الكذب في الخبر اللازم له (أو أنه لا قائل بالفصل بين الخبر والإنشاء، أي يمكن الجواب بأنه كذب، فلا يصح في الخبر، وإذا لم فيه لم يصح في الإنشاء، وإلا يلزم الفصل بينهما ولم يقل به أحد، وجه ضعف الجوابين أن مبناهما أن الخلاف في الإنشاء أيضاً، وليس كذلك قلنا يصدق) الكلام الذي وقع فيه التخصيص حال كونه (مجازاً) وإن لم يصدق حقيقة (فإنه لا يلزم من النفي حقيقة النفي
يصح
مجازاً) .
ISSUE: Specification is permissible rationally, meaning the intellect does not deem impossible the occurrence of specification by specificators from speech and other things. And it occurs in language, by induction, contrary to a deviant group whose opposition is not considered. They said in argument: It is a lie, so it is not fitting for a rational person to utter it. And in Sharh al-Sharh: It is a lie or a change of mind. They added this to include construct speech, so that the claim is fully proven, and it was not including it before this addition because lying does not occur in constructs but is specific to reports. It was rebutted that the dispute is only about reports, as stated by al-Amidi and others as in al-Taysir, so this addition is harmful to him and invalid. I say: From here, i.e., because the dispute is only about reports, the weakness of what was said becomes clear. And it is possible to respond to the lack of inclusion of the proof for constructs by saying that every construct entails a report, so if specification occurs in it, a lie occurs in the report entailed by it. Or that there is no one who distinguishes between report and construct, i.e., it is possible to respond that it is a lie, so it is not valid in reports, and if it is not valid there, it is not valid in constructs, otherwise a distinction between them would be necessary and no one has said it. The weakness of the two responses is that they are based on the dispute being in constructs as well, which is not the case. We say: The speech in which specification occurred is true metaphorically, even if not true literally, for it does not follow from the negation of literal truth the negation of metaphorical truth.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of takhsis (specification). It first states that takhsis is rationally possible and occurs in language, proven by induction, contrary to an insignificant fringe group. Then it addresses the opponents' objection that takhsis would entail lying (kadhib) or a change of mind (bada'). The author explains that the objection only applies to reports (khabar), not to constructs (insha'), and that the dispute is only about reports. The author then gives two possible responses to the objection, but notes their weakness because they assume the dispute includes constructs. The correct response is that the specified speech is true metaphorically (majaz), even if not literally true, because metaphorical truth does not require literal truth.

Key Terms
تخصيص (takhsis): إخراج بعض أفراد العام عن الحكم
كذب (kadhib): الخبر المخالف للواقع
بداء (bada'): ظهور رأي جديد بعد خفاء
مجاز (majaz): اللفظ المستعمل في غير ما وضع له بعلاقة

Key Takeaway
Takhsis is rationally permissible and linguistically attested; specified statements are metaphorically true, not false.

Validity of Specification by Reason (Takhsis bi al-Aql)
Defending specification by reason against four objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on takhsis bi al-aql, identify the four objections of the opponents, and explain the Hanafi responses to each.

Core Idea
Specification by reason is valid: the general word includes the excluded item linguistically, but reason excludes it.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul dispute: can reason specify a general text? The Hanafi school says yes—the general word remains general in language, but reason removes certain individuals from its scope. The opponents, reportedly including Imam al-Shafi'i, argue that if reason excludes an item, it was never included in the word's meaning. This lesson unpacks the debate, the four objections, and the rigorous Hanafi rebuttals that preserve the integrity of both language and reason.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the nature of the dispute
According to al-Subki, the disagreement is not about whether the excluded item is actually outside the ruling (all agree it is), but whether the general word includes it linguistically. Hanafis say yes; Shafi'is (according to the text) say no, and therefore do not call it takhsis.
This reframing avoids a strawman and focuses on the linguistic point.
2
State the Hanafi proof from the Quran
The Hanafi position is supported by verses like {And He is over all things competent} and {Hajj is a duty upon people}. The word 'everything' linguistically includes impossible things, but reason excludes them. Similarly, 'people' includes children and the insane, but they are excluded by reason because they cannot understand the address.
The first example is from al-Ma'idah 5:120, the second from Aal Imran 3:97.
3
Identify the four objections of the opponents
The passage lists four objections: (1) If takhsis by reason were valid, intending the general meaning would be correct, but rational people do not intend the impossible. (2) Takhsis is a bayan (clarification) and must follow the statement, but reason precedes. (3) If takhsis by reason is valid, then abrogation by reason should also be valid, but it is not. (4) Takhsis by reason leads to arbitrary preference between reason and revelation.
These objections are attributed to a group including al-Shafi'i.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses
For each objection, the Hanafis provide a precise response: (1) The word's indication does not require actual intention; linguistic correctness suffices. (2) The essence of reason is prior, but its attribute as a specifier is posterior—like a later discovered evidence. (3) Abrogation requires knowledge of the time period, which reason cannot grasp; specification only removes individuals. (4) The first proof does not involve actual conflict; it uses reason to understand the text, not to override it.
The author also notes that if takhsis by reason were invalid, takhsis by consensus, khabar wahid, and qiyas would also be invalid, which is not the case.
5
Apply the concept to new texts
Now that you understand the principle, consider any general Quranic or hadith text. When you encounter a phrase like 'kill the polytheists wherever you find them' (general), reason excludes minors and those under treaty, but the word includes them linguistically. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology.
Always check if reason provides a clear universal exclusion (like the impossible or the non-addressable).

Source Text
