Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Effect of an Unknown Mukhassis on the General Term
Analyzing the effect of an unknown mukhassis using analogies to naskh and istithna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dual resemblance of an unknown mukhassis to naskh and istithna, and state the Hanafi rule that the general term descends from qat'i to zanni.

Core Idea
An unknown mukhassis does not nullify the general completely; it only lowers its certainty to speculation.

In this lesson, we examine the intricate usul discussion on how an unknown mukhassis affects the general term (amm). The Hanafi school argues that the mukhassis, being independent, resembles both an abrogator (naskh) and an exception (istithna). This dual resemblance creates a conflict that prevents total nullification, but introduces doubt, reducing the general's indication from certain (qat'i) to speculative (zanni). Understanding this is crucial for determining the epistemological weight of a general text when a specifier is unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the mukhassis
Recognize that the mukhassis is an independent statement, not connected to the general. This independence is key to its resemblance to naskh.
2
Analyze the resemblance to naskh
Understand that an unknown mukhassis, like an unknown abrogator, is nullified because ambiguity cannot oppose a clear text. This aspect would demand complete nullification of the mukhassis and, by extension, the general.
3
Analyze the resemblance to istithna
Like an exception, the ignorance extends to the general, making the remaining scope unknown. This aspect would nullify the general itself.
4
Resolve the conflict
Apply the Hanafi principle: established certainty is not nullified by doubt; instead, it descends to zanniyyah. The dual resemblance prevents total nullification but creates doubt.
The general remains certain in knowledge but becomes speculative in practice.
5
Note the practical consequence
The general term is no longer qat'i for action (fi al-amal) but remains qat'i in knowledge (fi al-ilm). This affects how a mujtahid treats such a text.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية لاستقلاله) أي لكون هذا المخصص كلاماً مستقلاً فإذا كان المخصص (مجهولاً يبطل ذلك) المخصص (شبه الناسخ لبطلان الناسخ المجهول) فكذا ما يشبهه (ويبطل العام) بجهالته (شبه الاستثناء لتعدي جهالته إليه أي يصير العام مجهولاً لجهالة الباقي بجهالة الاستثناء، فكذا حكم ما يشبهه من المخصص (وإذا كان المخصص معلوماً فشبه الناسخ يبطل العام لصحة تعليله) أي تعليل المخصص لكونه كلاماً مستقلاً كالناسخ فإنه مستقل، لا أنه كما يصح تعليل الناسخ تعليله، وإذا صح التعليل وهي غير معلومة فلا يدري كم خرج به فجهل المخرج) فجهل الباقي وشبه الاستثناء يبقى (قطعيته كما كان، لأن الاستثناء لا يغير العام عما كان عليه قبله من القطعية، وإذا اقتضى أحد الشبهين البطلان بالكلية في صورتي الجهالة والعلم، والآخر البقاء على ما كان فلا يبطل العام من كل وجه في (الوجهين لأن عمل ما كان ثابتاً لا يبطل بالشك بل ينزل من القطعية إلى الظنية للشبهين المورثين الشك، فيظهر في حق العلم دون العمل (وفي نظر ظاهر ، لأن الحكم شبه الناسخ ليس في المخصص (المجهول إلا لفظاً، والمعتبر المعنى وليس في المعنى مشابهاً له، كيف والناسخ رافع بعد ثبوت الحكم، وههنا من بدء الأمر الحكم على الباقي كما في الاستثناء والعام مع المخصص مثله مع الاستثناء، ثم لا يظهر لقوله في المجهول فائدة، فإنه عام في المعلوم والمجهول وبعضهم تجاوز الحد وأفرط في سوء الأدب وقال : هذه مقدمات شعرية لا علمية وتحقيق كلام هذا الحبر الإمام البارع في الفن أن المخصص لكونه كلاماً مستقلاً غير مرتبط بالصدر، وتخصيصه ليس إلا لأنه مفيد لحكم مخالف لحكم العام في بعض الأفراد، فيفهم منه أن المراد بالعام سوى ما يتناوله هذا، فتخصيصه لأجل المعارضة، كما أن الناسخ يرفع الحكم لأجل المعارضة، وهذا شبه معنوي وليس كالاستثناء، فإنه قيد المستثنى منه ووضع لإفادة الحكم على هذا المقيد، ويفهم ضمناً الحكم على غيره الذي هو المخرج ثم إن المخصص يحكم على أن الحكم على بعض أفراد العام من بدء الأمر كما في الاستثناء الحكم على الباقي المعين بهذا المقيد، ففي المجهول شبه الناسخ يقتضي أن يبطل المخصص لأن المبهم لا يصلح معارضاً، وهذا يبطل الناسخ المجهول، وشبه الاستثناء يقتضي بطلان العام فلا يبطل بالشك، بل ينزل إلى الظنية، فإن قلت : كيف لا تصلح المعارضة فيمن قال : اقتل المشركين ولا تقتل بعضاً منهم، قلت : على هذا يلزم أن يصح النسخ به أيضاً وقد نهوا عنه، وفي المعلوم بالعكس كما قررنا، فقد ظهر أن هذه مقدمات علمية لا شعرية أصلاً، ثم إن القول بأن صحة التعليل تبطل العام لعله تنزلي جرى على تسليم ما بنى عليه الإمام الكرخي وإلا فاحتمال التعليل لا يبطل بل يورث شبهة فقط، ثم أورد الشيخ ابن الهمام أن القول ببطلان العام لجهالة القياس المخرج الموجبة للجهالة في الباقي لا يتأتى على رأيه، فإنه رضي الله عنه لا يبطل العام بجهالة المخصص، وأجاب بأنه بناء على المنع بالعمل بالعام قبل البحث عن المخصص، ولما كان احتماله قائماً يبطل العمل حتى يظن أنه لا تعليل هذا وهذا الإيراد لا يرد، فإنه رحمه الله لم يقل بأن العام يبطل ههنا، بل إنما قال : إن هذه الجهالة تقتضي بطلان العام، وهو رضي الله عنه لم ينفعه، بل يقول : إن مقتضى الجهالة في المجهول، ذلك لكن لا يبطل لمانع آخر يقتضي بطلان هذا المجهول، وما أفاد في الجواب فغير مرضي، فإن التوقف في العام إلى البحث عن المخصص لم يقل به أحد كما يلوح من الأسرار، وإن شئت أن تقرر الكلام نحواً أخصر فقل : إن المخصص المجهول يبطل في نفسه لعدم صلوحه معارضاً للنص العام، لكن يورث احتمال الخصوص، فلم يبق قطعياً، والمعلوم يورث الاحتمال لاحتماله التعليل، لكن الاحتمال لا يبطل الموجود فافهم.
The third treatise on linguistic principles [regarding] its independence – that is, because this mukhassis is an independent statement. So if the mukhassis is unknown, that mukhassis is nullified by the resemblance to the abrogator, due to the nullification of an unknown abrogator. Likewise, what resembles it. And it nullifies the general term by its ignorance, resembling the exception because its ignorance extends to it, meaning the general term becomes unknown due to the ignorance of the remaining part by the ignorance of the exception. So likewise the ruling of what resembles it from the mukhassis. And if the mukhassis is known, then the resemblance to the abrogator nullifies the general term due to the validity of its ta'lil (causal reasoning) – that is, the ta'lil of the mukhassis because it is an independent statement like the abrogator, not that it is as valid as the abrogator's ta'lil. And when the ta'lil is valid and it is not known, then it is not known how many are excluded, so the excluded is unknown, so the remaining is unknown. And the resemblance to the exception remains its qat'iyyah as it was, because the exception does not change the general term from its previous qat'iyyah. And when one of the two resemblances requires complete nullification in the cases of ignorance and knowledge, and the other requires remaining as before, then the general term is not nullified completely in both cases, because what is established by operation is not nullified by doubt; rather it descends from qat'iyyah to zanniyyah due to the two resemblances that cause doubt. So it appears in the realm of knowledge but not operation. And in a clear view, because the ruling of the resemblance of the abrogator is not in the unknown mukhassis except verbally, and the meaning is considered, and in meaning it does not resemble it. How? The abrogator raises after the ruling is established, while here from the beginning the ruling is on the remaining, as in the exception. The general term with the mukhassis is like it with the exception. Then there does not appear any benefit in his statement regarding the unknown, for it is general to the known and unknown. Some have transgressed the limit and excessively shown bad manners, saying: these are poetic premises, not scientific. The realization of the speech of this scholar, the leading Imam skilled in the discipline, is that the mukhassis, because it is an independent statement not connected to the beginning, and its specification is only because it conveys a ruling contrary to the ruling of the general term in some individuals, so it is understood that the general term intends other than what this covers. So its specification is for the sake of opposition, just as the abrogator raises the ruling for the sake of opposition. This is a meaning-based resemblance, not like the exception, for it restricts the excepted-from and is placed to convey a ruling on this restricted, and implicitly conveys the ruling on other than it, which is the excluded. Then the mukhassis rules that the ruling on some individuals of the general term is from the beginning, as in the exception the ruling is on the remaining specified by this restricted. So in the unknown, the resemblance of the abrogator requires that the mukhassis be nullified because the ambiguous is not fit for opposition, and this nullifies the unknown abrogator. And the resemblance of the exception requires nullification of the general term, so it is not nullified by doubt; rather it descends to zanniyyah. If you say: How is opposition not fit in one who says: 'Kill the polytheists but do not kill some of them?' I say: On this, abrogation by it would also be valid, and they are forbidden from that. In the known, it is the opposite as we set forth. Thus it appears that these are scientific premises, not poetic at all. Then, the statement that the validity of ta'lil nullifies the general term is probably a concessive argument built on accepting what Imam al-Karkhi based, otherwise the possibility of ta'lil does not nullify; it only causes a doubt. Then Shaykh Ibn al-Humam objected that the statement that the general term is nullified due to ignorance of the analogical exclusion causing ignorance in the remaining is not consistent with his view, for he (may Allah be pleased with him) does not nullify the general term due to ignorance of the mukhassis. And he answered that it is based on preventing operation with the general term before seeking the mukhassis, and when its possibility remains, operation is nullified until it is thought that there is no ta'lil here. This objection is not valid, for he (may Allah have mercy on him) did not say that the general term is nullified here; rather he said that this ignorance requires nullification of the general term, and he (may Allah be pleased with him) does not deny that, but says that what the ignorance in the unknown requires is that, but it is not nullified due to another preventer that requires nullification of this unknown. And what he benefited in the answer is not satisfactory, for pausing the general term until seeking the mukhassis has not been stated by anyone as appears from al-Asrar. And if you wish to set forth the speech in a more concise way, say: The unknown mukhassis is nullified in itself because it is not fit to oppose the general text, but it causes the probability of specification, so it no longer remains qat'i. And the known mukhassis causes probability due to the possibility of ta'lil, but probability does not nullify what exists. So understand.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based analysis within the Hanafi usul tradition concerning the effect of a mukhassis (specifier) on a general term (amm) when the mukhassis is either unknown or known. The author begins by stating that the mukhassis is an independent statement. When the mukhassis is unknown (majhul), it is nullified because it resembles a naskh (abrogation) that is unknown—ambiguity cannot serve as a valid counterproof. However, it also resembles an exception (istithna), which extends its ignorance to the general term, making the entire general term unknown. This dual resemblance leads to a conflict: the naskh-like aspect demands complete nullification, while the istithna-like aspect demands that the general retain its original qat'iyyah (certainty). The resolution is that the general term is not wholly nullified but drops from qat'iyyah to zanniyyah (speculative certainty) in practice, though it remains qat'i in knowledge. The author defends this view against critics who dismissed it as 'poetic premises,' explaining the substantive difference between naskh and specification. Naskh removes an established ruling, while specification from the beginning restricts the scope of the ruling. When the mukhassis is known, the resemblance to naskh would nullify the general if the ta'lil (causal reasoning) is valid; but since the cause is unknown, doubt arises. The author then addresses an objection by Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, who argued that the Hanafi imam (Abu Hanifa or al-Karkhi) does not nullify the general due to an unknown mukhassis. The author responds that the imam only says that ignorance requires nullification, but it is prevented by another factor. Ultimately, the concise rule is: an unknown mukhassis nullifies itself and renders the general non-qat'i; a known mukhassis only creates probability, which does not remove what is already established.

Key Terms
المخصص (al-mukhassis): A specifier that restricts the scope of a general term (amm), presented as an independent statement.
الناسخ (al-nasikh): An abrogator that removes a previously established ruling; used here as an analogy for the mukhassis due to their independence.
الاستثناء (al-istithna): An exception that excludes some individuals from the general term; it does not change the qat'iyyah of the general.
القطعية (al-qat'iyyah): Certainty of indication; here, the general term is qat'i before specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of an unknown mukhassis on the general term?
A) The general term is completely nullified.
B) The general term descends from qat'i to zanni.
C) The general term remains qat'i without change.
D) The mukhassis is ignored and the general stays as is.

Key Takeaway
An unknown mukhassis does not render the general term void; it only reduces its certainty to speculative, preserving its validity while acknowledging doubt.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
Effect of a Known Mukhassis and the Objection of Ibn al-Humam
Analyzing the effect of a known mukhassis and addressing a scholarly objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on a known mukhassis and evaluate Ibn al-Humam's objection regarding nullification of the general.

Core Idea
A known mukhassis introduces probability (zann) but does not remove the general's established qat'iyyah in knowledge.

In this lesson, we explore the effect of a known mukhassis on the general term. Unlike an unknown mukhassis, a known specifier is valid but introduces doubt about the precise scope of the general due to the possibility of ta'lil (causal reasoning). The Hanafi school holds that this probability does not nullify the general's certainty in knowledge, only in practice. We also examine the objection raised by Ibn al-Humam, who argued that the general should not be nullified even by an unknown mukhassis, and the response that ignorance requires nullification but is prevented by another factor.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish known from unknown mukhassis
Understand that a known mukhassis is a valid independent statement with clear content, unlike an unknown one which is ambiguous.
2
Analyze the resemblance to naskh for a known mukhassis
A known mukhassis resembles naskh in that it opposes the general, but since its ta'lil (causal reasoning) is not known, it cannot nullify the general completely; it only creates doubt.
3
Analyze the resemblance to istithna for a known mukhassis
Like istithna, a known mukhassis does not change the general's qat'iyyah in knowledge; it only restricts the scope.
4
Apply the resolution: probability does not nullify
The existence of a known mukhassis raises the possibility that the general was intended to be specified, but this probability does not remove the established certainty of the general; it remains qat'i in knowledge.
The general becomes zanni only in practice, i.e., one cannot act on it with full conviction without further investigation.
5
Evaluate Ibn al-Humam's objection
Ibn al-Humam argued that the Hanafi imam (al-Karkhi) does not nullify the general due to an unknown mukhassis. The response clarifies that the imam acknowledged the tendency to nullify but held that another preventer blocks it.
The objection is about nullification, not descent to zanni. The response uses the principle that doubt does not nullify established certainty.

The third treatise on linguistic principles [regarding] its independence – that is, because this mukhassis is an independent statement. So if the mukhassis is unknown, that mukhassis is nullified by the resemblance to the abrogator, due to the nullification of an unknown abrogator. Likewise, what resembles it. And it nullifies the general term by its ignorance, resembling the exception because its ignorance extends to it, meaning the general term becomes unknown due to the ignorance of the remaining part by the ignorance of the exception. So likewise the ruling of what resembles it from the mukhassis. And if the mukhassis is known, then the resemblance to the abrogator nullifies the general term due to the validity of its ta'lil (causal reasoning) – that is, the ta'lil of the mukhassis because it is an independent statement like the abrogator, not that it is as valid as the abrogator's ta'lil. And when the ta'lil is valid and it is not known, then it is not known how many are excluded, so the excluded is unknown, so the remaining is unknown. And the resemblance to the exception remains its qat'iyyah as it was, because the exception does not change the general term from its previous qat'iyyah. And when one of the two resemblances requires complete nullification in the cases of ignorance and knowledge, and the other requires remaining as before, then the general term is not nullified completely in both cases, because what is established by operation is not nullified by doubt; rather it descends from qat'iyyah to zanniyyah due to the two resemblances that cause doubt. So it appears in the realm of knowledge but not operation. And in a clear view, because the ruling of the resemblance of the abrogator is not in the unknown mukhassis except verbally, and the meaning is considered, and in meaning it does not resemble it. How? The abrogator raises after the ruling is established, while here from the beginning the ruling is on the remaining, as in the exception. The general term with the mukhassis is like it with the exception. Then there does not appear any benefit in his statement regarding the unknown, for it is general to the known and unknown. Some have transgressed the limit and excessively shown bad manners, saying: these are poetic premises, not scientific. The realization of the speech of this scholar, the leading Imam skilled in the discipline, is that the mukhassis, because it is an independent statement not connected to the beginning, and its specification is only because it conveys a ruling contrary to the ruling of the general term in some individuals, so it is understood that the general term intends other than what this covers. So its specification is for the sake of opposition, just as the abrogator raises the ruling for the sake of opposition. This is a meaning-based resemblance, not like the exception, for it restricts the excepted-from and is placed to convey a ruling on this restricted, and implicitly conveys the ruling on other than it, which is the excluded. Then the mukhassis rules that the ruling on some individuals of the general term is from the beginning, as in the exception the ruling is on the remaining specified by this restricted. So in the unknown, the resemblance of the abrogator requires that the mukhassis be nullified because the ambiguous is not fit for opposition, and this nullifies the unknown abrogator. And the resemblance of the exception requires nullification of the general term, so it is not nullified by doubt; rather it descends to zanniyyah. If you say: How is opposition not fit in one who says: 'Kill the polytheists but do not kill some of them?' I say: On this, abrogation by it would also be valid, and they are forbidden from that. In the known, it is the opposite as we set forth. Thus it appears that these are scientific premises, not poetic at all. Then, the statement that the validity of ta'lil nullifies the general term is probably a concessive argument built on accepting what Imam al-Karkhi based, otherwise the possibility of ta'lil does not nullify; it only causes a doubt. Then Shaykh Ibn al-Humam objected that the statement that the general term is nullified due to ignorance of the analogical exclusion causing ignorance in the remaining is not consistent with his view, for he (may Allah be pleased with him) does not nullify the general term due to ignorance of the mukhassis. And he answered that it is based on preventing operation with the general term before seeking the mukhassis, and when its possibility remains, operation is nullified until it is thought that there is no ta'lil here. This objection is not valid, for he (may Allah have mercy on him) did not say that the general term is nullified here; rather he said that this ignorance requires nullification of the general term, and he (may Allah be pleased with him) does not deny that, but says that what the ignorance in the unknown requires is that, but it is not nullified due to another preventer that requires nullification of this unknown. And what he benefited in the answer is not satisfactory, for pausing the general term until seeking the mukhassis has not been stated by anyone as appears from al-Asrar. And if you wish to set forth the speech in a more concise way, say: The unknown mukhassis is nullified in itself because it is not fit to oppose the general text, but it causes the probability of specification, so it no longer remains qat'i. And the known mukhassis causes probability due to the possibility of ta'lil, but probability does not nullify what exists. So understand.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based analysis within the Hanafi usul tradition concerning the effect of a mukhassis (specifier) on a general term (amm) when the mukhassis is either unknown or known. The author begins by stating that the mukhassis is an independent statement. When the mukhassis is unknown (majhul), it is nullified because it resembles a naskh (abrogation) that is unknown—ambiguity cannot serve as a valid counterproof. However, it also resembles an exception (istithna), which extends its ignorance to the general term, making the entire general term unknown. This dual resemblance leads to a conflict: the naskh-like aspect demands complete nullification, while the istithna-like aspect demands that the general retain its original qat'iyyah (certainty). The resolution is that the general term is not wholly nullified but drops from qat'iyyah to zanniyyah (speculative certainty) in practice, though it remains qat'i in knowledge. The author defends this view against critics who dismissed it as 'poetic premises,' explaining the substantive difference between naskh and specification. Naskh removes an established ruling, while specification from the beginning restricts the scope of the ruling. When the mukhassis is known, the resemblance to naskh would nullify the general if the ta'lil (causal reasoning) is valid; but since the cause is unknown, doubt arises. The author then addresses an objection by Shaykh Ibn al-Humam, who argued that the Hanafi imam (Abu Hanifa or al-Karkhi) does not nullify the general due to an unknown mukhassis. The author responds that the imam only says that ignorance requires nullification, but it is prevented by another factor. Ultimately, the concise rule is: an unknown mukhassis nullifies itself and renders the general non-qat'i; a known mukhassis only creates probability, which does not remove what is already established.

Key Terms
المخصص (al-mukhassis): A specifier that restricts the scope of a general term (amm), presented as an independent statement.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): Causal reasoning; the rationale behind a legal ruling. Here, the mukhassis's ta'lil is unknown.
الناسخ (al-nasikh): An abrogator that removes a previously established ruling; used as an analogy for the mukhassis due to independence.
الاستثناء (al-istithna): An exception that excludes some individuals from the general term; preserves the general's qat'iyyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a known mukhassis on the general term?
A) The general term becomes completely null and void.
B) The general term remains absolutely certain with no doubt.
C) The general term remains certain in knowledge but becomes speculative in practice.
D) The general term is suspended until the mukhassis is investigated.

Key Takeaway
A known mukhassis introduces doubt in practice but does not remove the general's certainty in knowledge; the Hanafi response to Ibn al-Humam clarifies that ignorance requires nullification but is prevented by another factor.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
15 min
The Specified General: Haqiqah or Majaz? The Eight Madhhabs
Catalogue and analysis of eight madhhabs on a key usul dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the eight madhhabs on the status of 'amm after takhsis, and apply the Hanafi position that distinguishes between independent and non-independent specifiers.

Core Idea
A general term after specification remains literal only when the specifier is non-independent.

The status of a general term after it has been specified is one of the most subtle and debated issues in usul al-fiqh. Does the word still mean what it originally meant, or has it become metaphorical? This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut maps out eight different scholarly positions, then defends the nuanced Hanafi view that the answer depends on the type of specifier. Understanding this debate clarifies how general proofs retain their authority after being restricted.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Core Issue
Identify the problem: a general word (e.g. 'al-sariq' = the thief) originally covers all individuals. When a specifier (e.g. 'except the one who steals less than a quarter dinar') restricts it to some individuals, does the word continue to be used literally (haqiqah) for the remaining individuals, or does it become metaphorical (majaz) because it no longer covers its original full range?
The answer affects whether the specified general is considered qat'i (definitive) or zanni (probable) as a proof for the remaining individuals.
2
Catalogue the Eight Madhhabs
List each position from the text: (1) Ash'aris & Mu'tazila: majaz absolutely; (2) Hanbalis, most Shafi'is, Hanafi al-Sarakhsi: haqiqah absolutely; (3) Imam al-Haramayn & some Hanafis: haqiqah in the remaining, majaz if confined; (4) Abu Bakr al-Jassas (Shafi'i transmission): haqiqah if remaining not limited; (5) al-Jassas (Hanafi transmission): haqiqah if remaining plural; (6) Abu al-Husayn & some Hanafis: haqiqah if specified by non-independent, majaz if independent; (7) al-Baqillani: haqiqah if by condition/exception; (8) Abd al-Jabbar: haqiqah if by condition/adjective; plus a variant: haqiqah if verbal, majaz if sensory.
This is a catalogue; later we will focus on the Hanafi position which is number 6, and the author defends this view.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Proof
The Hanafi argument: if 'amm after takhsis were haqiqah in the remaining, then the word would have at least two literal meanings (the whole and the part), which is homonymy (ishtirak lafzi). Homonymy is invalid by consensus, contrary to the default, and would lead to infinite meanings because specification can be to any number. Therefore, the general cannot be haqiqah in the remaining when specified by an independent specifier. However, for non-independent specifiers, the general is still used in its original meaning (the whole) but with a restricted ruling, so no homonymy occurs.
Key distinction: independent specifier (mustaqill) is a separate text that contradicts the general; non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) is a grammatical element like condition, exception, adjective, limit.
4
Distinguish Specifier Types
Examine examples: (a) Exception: 'The thieves are to be cut off, except the repentant' – the word 'thieves' is used generally, but the ruling applies only to non-repentant. The general is still used for all thieves in the statement; the exception restricts the ruling, not the meaning. (b) Independent specifier: a hadith specifying that a thief's hand is cut only for a quarter dinar – this contradicts the general verse 'cut off their hands' for all theft. The general must be reinterpreted as referring only to thieves of ≥ quarter dinar, so the word is used metaphorically for that subset.
The author explains that for non-independent specifiers, the general is like a composite expression (e.g., 'tall of the sword-belt' alludes to tall stature) – it mentions the whole but means the part via context. This is not metaphor in the usual sense.
5
Synthesise the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if the specified general remains haqiqah, it retains its original force (qat'i) for the remaining individuals; if it becomes majaz, it becomes zanni and requires further evidence. This affects how a mujtahid weighs the general text after specification. For example, the verse on theft (al-Ma'idah 5:38) is general; after specifying the hand to the wrist by Sunnah, is the remaining generality (e.g., the left hand if the right is missing) still a definitive proof?
The Hanafi position that it remains haqiqah when specified by non-independent specifiers strengthens the authority of general texts.

The requirement of [ignorance] in the unknown [specifier] is that, but it does not nullify due to another preventer that requires the nullification of this unknown. What was beneficial in the answer is unsatisfactory, for suspending [judgment on] the general until searching for the specifier has not been said by anyone, as appears from al-Asrar. If you wish to express the speech more succinctly, say: The unknown specifier is invalid in itself because it is not suitable to oppose the general text, but it gives rise to the possibility of specificity, so it does not remain definitive. The known specifier gives rise to possibility due to its possibility of [being based on] reasoning, but possibility does not nullify what exists, so understand. The followers of Abu Thawr said: Generality becomes invalid after specification (and what is under it) to the single [individual] (are probable metaphors, and none of them is more worthy of intention, so it becomes ambiguous regarding them) and it is not a proof. We said: That, i.e., ambiguity, is when the metaphors are equal. Here, what remains after specification is preponderant because it is closer to the literal meaning and the mind immediately turns to it. Issue: The Imams: The specified general is metaphorical according to the majority of Ash'aris, followers of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and famous Mu'tazila. The Hanbalis and most Shafi'is, rather the majority of jurists including Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi from us [Hanafis] said: The specified general is literal. Imam al-Haramayn from the Shafi'is and some Hanafis, including Sadr al-Shari'a, said: The specified general is literal regarding what remains, metaphorical regarding confining to it, except that according to this leader it is specific to when it is specified by an independent [specifier]; rather there is no specification except by it. It is narrated from Shaykh Imam Abu Bakr al-Jassas from the Hanafis, according to what the Shafi'is transmitted, that the specified general is literal if what remains is not limited. It is also narrated from him as the Hanafis transmitted, and they are more worthy of transmitting his madhhab, for they are more knowledgeable of the madhhab of their shaykhs, especially one like him. The specified general is literal if what remains is a plural. Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili and some Hanafis said: The specified general is literal if specified by a non-independent [specifier]; if specified by an independent [specifier], it is metaphorical. No disagreement is known among the Hanafis that the general that is accompanied by a condition, an adjective, a limit, or an exception is not metaphorical at all; rather the disagreement is only regarding what is specified by an independent [specifier]. The phrase 'some' is not in its place. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani from the Shafi'is said: The specified general is literal if specified by a condition or exception; what is specified by others is metaphorical. Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili, according to what is famous from him, said: The specified general is literal if specified by a condition or an adjective; if specified by others, it is metaphorical. It is said it is literal if specified by a verbal [specifier]; if specified by something else like reason, sense, or custom, then it is metaphorical. So these are eight madhhabs. For us [Hanafis], it is literal regarding totality, unanimously according to everyone who holds that it has a form. So if what remains were also literal after specification, there would be verbal homonymy between the whole and the part, contradicting the consensus on its invalidity. Also because homonymy is contrary to the default. Also because it is verbal homonymy for unlimited meanings, because specification down to one and above that up to totality is unlimited. The statement that permits homonymy between the whole and the common denominator, then the levels of specification are among its individuals, so using the word for it as literal is useless, for the discussion here is about applying it to the part specifically, and that is not sufficient for it to be literal. Understand. This proof does not hold in the case of restriction by a non-independent [specifier], for the general is not confined to the part used in it, but rather is used in what it was placed for in the first placement, which is the whole, so it is not homonymous nor metaphorical. Indeed, the general in a condition is used for the whole, and it is the locus of the suspensive ruling, but the consequent is not realized for some individuals due to the lack of the condition. As for the limit, the general is used for the whole, and the ruling is on the individuals before the limit; either the genus is considered restricted by the limit, then its generality is considered over the individuals of this restricted [genus]. On both estimations, there is no restriction nor any use for some of what it was placed for originally. In exception, the general is general, and the ruling is on what the restricted applies to by excluding some. In the adjective, generality is from the apparent [meaning] for what the genus restricted by the adjective is suitable for. In a substitute for some, it is used as it was, but the intended for the ruling is the substitute, and it has been explained already. And an objection was raised, firstly, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: that the intention of totality in the specified general remains, and the exclusion of some arises from the specifier, so there is no homonymy nor metaphor. I [the author] say in response: If the objector means by his statement 'the intention of totality remains' the intention conceptually, because the whole is conceivable, then in every metaphor that is the case, for the conceptual understanding of the literal meaning remains and does not harm the metaphoricality. If he means the intention of totality in usage, such that it is used in it, then there is no doubt that the ruling in the specified general is on the part, and what is considered is the usage which is the basis of the ruling, so there is no intention of totality in usage, rather of the part, so metaphoricality or homonymy is necessary. Moreover, if it were used in the whole while the ruling is on the part, it entails uselessness, necessarily because the ruling on the part is complete with the part, i.e., it is complete by intending the part that is the locus of the ruling, so intending the other part with it is useless, so understand. It has been answered to both [objections] that the intended is the second side, and the general is used in the whole, then the specifier removes some, then a ruling is made on what remains, so the ruling is on the part that is expressed by the whole from which the part has been excluded. In other words, this is like metaphor (kinnaya), for in it something is mentioned, and the basis of the ruling is something else that is alluded to by it, like 'long of the sword-belt'. So here the general is mentioned, and what is intended by the ruling is the part by the indication of the specifier. This is a way of expression, the utmost of it being longer than expression by another concept, and there is no uselessness in it. It is like saying 'you' and 'the son of your maternal aunt's sister' are two ways of expression; the first is shorter, the second longer. So both answers are repelled. This is only valid in exception and its like, for due to its non-independence and its inclusion under the rule, it is valid regarding it that the general with restriction is used to express what remains, and it indicates it by the indication of composites according to type-placement as we said, or by way of metaphor as it is said. As for the independent [specifier], it is not valid in it, for it is not connected to the general but rather provides a ruling contradicting the ruling of the general in some individuals, and to repel the contradiction it becomes a contextual indicator that the ruling in the general is on the part that this specifier does not cover, so necessarily the general is used only for the part, otherwise there would be definite uselessness. Also, usage is only the application of a word to a meaning so that what is derived from it is the basis of the ruling, and there is no doubt that it is the part, so the word is used in it and the independent specifier is a contextual indicator, so ponder and be grateful. And a second objection: that the intention of what remains in the specified general is not a second placement and usage other than the first placement for totality and usage in it, but rather its intention is by the first and usage by it, unlike
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on the status of a general term ('amm) after it has been specified (takhsis). The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, first refutes the idea that uncertainty from an unknown specifier makes the general ambiguous, then presents eight different madhhabs on whether the specified general remains literal (haqiqah) or becomes metaphorical (majaz). The eight positions are: (1) Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: it becomes majaz; (2) Hanbalis, most Shafi'is, and Hanafi Imam al-Sarakhsi: it remains haqiqah; (3) Imam al-Haramayn and some Hanafis: haqiqah in the remaining, majaz if confined to it; (4) Abu Bakr al-Jassas (according to Shafi'i transmission): haqiqah if remaining is not limited; (5) al-Jassas (according to Hanafi transmission): haqiqah if remaining is a plural; (6) Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili and some Hanafis: haqiqah if specified by non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) specifier, majaz if by independent; (7) al-Baqillani: haqiqah if by condition or exception; (8) Abd al-Jabbar: haqiqah if by condition or adjective; and a variant: haqiqah if by verbal specifier, majaz if by reason/sense/custom. The author then argues for the Hanafi position that 'amm is haqiqah in totality, and after specification it cannot be haqiqah in the remaining because that would entail homonymy (ishtirak) between the whole and part, which violates consensus and default principle. He distinguishes between independent and non-independent specifiers: for non-independent (like condition, exception, etc.), the general remains used in its original meaning (the whole) but the ruling is restricted, so no homonymy or metaphor; for independent specifiers, the general is necessarily used only for the remaining part due to the contextual indicator, so it becomes majaz. He defends this against two objections: first, that the intention of totality remains conceptually and the specifier merely excludes; he responds that if conceptual intention suffices, then all metaphors would be literal, and if usage intention is meant, then the ruling is on the part, so totality is not intended in usage. The counter-response using kinnaya (metaphorical allusion) works only for non-independent specifiers like exception, where the general with restriction expresses the part by composition or metaphor. For independent specifiers, the general is used directly for the part. The second objection is that the intention of the remaining is by the first placement; the author hints at a response but the text cuts off at 'bikhilaf' (unlike).

Key Terms
عام ('amm): A word that covers all individuals of its category without restriction, e.g., 'man' or 'people'.
تخصيص (takhsis): The act of restricting a general term to some of its individuals by a specifier (mukhassis).
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning: a word used for what it was originally placed for.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning: a word used for something other than its original meaning due to a connection.
مستقل (mustaqill): An independent specifier: a textual evidence that stands alone and contradicts the general in some individuals, requiring specification.
غير مستقل (ghayr mustaqill): A non-independent specifier: a grammatical or contextual element (like condition, exception, adjective, limit) that restricts the general without contradicting it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi nuanced view presented in the passage, what is the status of a general term specified by a condition (shart)?
A) It becomes majaz because the ruling is restricted.
B) It remains haqiqah because the word is still used in its original meaning.
C) It becomes ambiguous (mujmal) because the condition is not independent.
D) It is haqiqah only if the condition is textual; otherwise majaz.

Key Takeaway
The status of a specified general depends on the type of specifier: non-independent specifiers preserve literalness, while independent specifiers render it metaphorical.

The Hanafi Distinction: Independent vs Non-Independent Specifiers
Explaining the Hanafi rule for specified general terms based on specifier independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a specifier as independent or non-independent and state the corresponding Hanafi ruling on whether the general term remains literal or becomes metaphorical.

Core Idea
A general term specified by a non-independent specifier remains literal; by an independent specifier, it becomes metaphorical.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author lays out the foundational Hanafi principle regarding the linguistic status of a general term after specification. The key determinant is whether the specifier is independent (mustaqill) or non-independent (ghayr mustaqill). Non-independent specifiers like exception, condition, limit, and adjective do not alter the literal usage of the general term, while independent specifiers, which provide a contradictory ruling, force the general term to be understood metaphorically or as shared. This distinction has profound implications for how we interpret legal texts and derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general term and its specifier
Recognize that the discussion begins with the general term ('amm) and the specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) that restricts its scope.
2
Classify the specifier
Determine if the specifier is non-independent (e.g., exception, condition, limit, adjective) or independent (e.g., a contradictory verse or hadith).
Non-independent specifiers are those that do not provide a separate ruling contradicting the general term.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
For non-independent specifiers, the general term remains literal (haqīqah); for independent specifiers, it becomes metaphorical (majāz) or shared (mushtarak).
4
Understand the rationale
Non-independent specifiers are subsumed under the general rule; independent specifiers create a contradiction that must be resolved by reinterpreting the general term to avoid redundancy (laghw).
5
Contrast with other schools
Note that Hanbalis and some Shafi'is hold the general term remains literal regardless, but Hanafis maintain the distinction.
This contrast will be elaborated in the next lesson.

الآخر معه لغو فافهم، وقد أجيب عنهما بأن المراد الشق الثاني، والعام مستعمل في الكل، ثم أخرج عنه المخرج بالمخصص، ثم حكم على الباقي، فالحكم على البعض الذي عبر عنه بالكل المخرج عنه البعض، وبعبارة أخرى مثل التعبير بمفهوم آخر ولا لغو فيه، ومثله مثل : أنت وابن أخت خالتك طريقتان للتعبير والأوّل أقصر، والثاني أطول فاندفع الجوابان، وهذا إنما يتم في الاستثناء ،ونحوه، فإنه لعدم استقلاله واندراجه تحت القاعدة يصح فيه الحكم بأن العام مع التقيد يعبر به عن الباقي، وهو دال عليه دلالة المركبات بالوضع النوعي كما قلنا، أو بطريق الكناية كما قيل، وأما المستقل فلا يصح ذلك فيه، فإنه ليس مرتبطاً بالعام بل مفيد لحكم معارض لحكم العام في بعض الأفراد، ولدفع المعارضة يصير قرينة على أن الحكم في العام على البعض الغير المتناول له هذا المخصص، فبالضرورة يكون العام مستعملاً في البعض فقط، وإلا اللغو قطعاً، وأيضاً ليس الاستعمال إلا إطلاق لفظ على معنى ليكون ما يستفاد منه مناط الحكم، ولا شك أنه هو البعض، فاللفظ مستعمل فيه والمخصص المستقل قرينة فتدبر وتشكر (و) اعترض (ثانياً بأن إرادة الباقي) في العام المخصوص (ليس بوضع الحقيقة ودفع) بأن لا كلام في إرادة الباقي في ضمن إرادة الكل كما كان قبل التخصيص) إرادة الكل وفي ضمنها إرادة البعض (بل) الكلام ( في إرادته بخصوصه بقرينة التخصيص) فإن الكلام في المخصوص من العام (وهذا معنى ثان لا بد له من استعمال ثان) فإن كان له الوضع فالاشتراك وإلا فالمجاز، وإن قرر بأن في التخصيص استعمالاً في الكل والحكم على البعض كما قرر به الاعتراض الأول ففيه ما قد عرفت من أنه يتم في غير المستقل دون المستقل، ولك أن تقرر الاعتراض بأن الاستعمال في المعنى عبارة عن إرادته من اللفظ ليكون مناطاً للحكم والباقي كما كان يقصد من اللفظ حين الاستغراق، ويحكم عليه بالذات، فإن الحكم المتعلق بالعام متعلق بكل واحد واحد من آحاده؛ غاية ما في الباب أن مع إرادته بعض آخر متعلق الحكم، كذلك بعد التخصيص أيضاً الاستعمال في الباقي بالذات، وهو مناط الحكم كذلك، إلا أنه سقط الحكم على بعض آخر بالمخصص، وهذا لا يغير الاستعمال الأول في الباقي، وإذا لم يتعدد الاستعمال والوضع فهو حقيقة، وهذا بخلاف سائر المدلولات التضمنية، فإن فهم الجزء هناك في ضمن فهم الكل وليس مناطاً للحكم، وإذا أريد الجزء بخصوصه صار متفهماً بالذات ومناطاً للحكم كذلك، فاختلف الاستعمال، ولك أن تجيب عنه بأنه في الاستعمال الأول كان مقصوداً ومنفهماً ليكون مناطاً للحكم الثابت للكل على الاستغراق، وكان المقصود من استعمال اللفظ الحكم على الكل، وأما الآن بعد التخصيص فاستعماله فيه وإرادة الباقي منه إنما هو ليقصر الحكم عليه ويبقى الآخر مسكوتاً أو مثبتاً فيه الحكم المخالف، فهذا الاستعمال مغاير للاستعمال الأوّل، فإن كان بوضع آخر فالاشتراك لازم وإلا فالمجاز فافهم (و) اعترض (ثالثاً) كما قال الإمام شمس الأئمة (السرخسي : أن الصيغة للكل فإنه موضوع له وبعد التخصيص البعض هو الكل فهي مستعملة فيما وضعت أوّلاً، فلا اشتراك ولا تجوز (أقول) هذا مندفع (فإن العام هو اللفظ المستغرق لما يصلح له حقيقة أو عرفاً، ولذلك أي لكونه مستغرقاً لما يصلح له لم يكن الجمع للمعهودين عاماً) وإذا كان مستغرقاً لما يصلح له فاستغراقه للبعض لو لم يكن غير موضوع له لكان مشتركاً، وتوجيه كلامه بأن العام موضوع لاستغراق جميع أفراد معنى اللفظ، إن مطلقاً فالاستغراق لجميع أفراد المطلق نحو : الرجال، وإن مقيداً فلجميع أفراد المقيد نحو علماء البلد والعام المقرون مع المخصص مقيد به واللفظ متناول لجميع ما يصلح له اللفظ المقيد بهذا القيد نحو : الرجال العلماء، أو الرجال إلا العلماء، لا يتم إلا في غير المستقل، وهذا النحرير الإمام لا يراه مخصصاً، وكلامه إنما هو في العام المخصوص بالمستقل فتدبر (الحنابلة ومن وافقهم من الشافعية والفقهاء :(قالوا :أولاً: التناول) للباقي بعض التخصيص (باق كما كان قبل وقد كان حقيقة قبل) فهو حقيقة الآن (قلنا) لا نسلم أن التناول له باقٍ كما كان قبل بل (كان) التناول قبل له (مع غيره) فإنه كان للكل (والآن) التناول له (وحده، فقيل : هذا أي كون التناول له وحده (لا يغير صفة تناوله لما يتناوله) وإنما يغير تناوله للمخرج (قلنا) لا نسلم أنه لا يغير صفة التناول (بل) نقول :
The third discourse on linguistic principles: the placement of the common meaning, so understand. And this proof is not complete in the case of shortening with non-independent [specifiers], for the general term therein is not restricted to some [individuals] and used in them, rather it is used in what it was placed for with the first placement, which is the whole, so it is neither shared nor metaphorical. For the general term in the condition is used in the whole, and it is the object of the suspensory ruling, but the consequence is not actualized in some individuals due to the absence of the condition. And in the [case of] limit, the general term is used in the whole, and the ruling applies to the individuals before the limit; either the genus is considered restricted by the limit, then its generality is considered in the individuals of this restricted [genus]; on both estimates, there is no shortening, nor any use in some of what it was placed for originally. And in exception, the general term is general, and the ruling applies to what the restricted [term] is true of by excluding some. And in the adjective, generality is clear for what the genus restricted by the adjective is suitable for. And in substitution of some, it is used as it was, but the intended ruling is the substitute, and it has been explained previously. And [the author] objected firstly, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: "that the intention of inclusiveness in the specified general term remains, and the exclusion of some occurs from the specifier, so there is neither sharing nor metaphor." I say in refutation: if the objector means by his saying "the intention of inclusiveness remains" [as] intellectual intention, since the whole is intellectually conceived, then in every metaphor likewise, the conception of the literal meaning remains, so it does not harm metaphoricity. And if he means [the intention of inclusiveness] in usage, such that it is used in it, then there is no doubt that the ruling in the specified general term applies to some, and the relevant consideration is the usage that is the basis of the ruling, so there is no intention of inclusiveness in usage, but rather of some, so metaphoricity or sharing is necessary. Moreover, if it were used in the whole while the ruling applies to some, it would entail redundancy, necessarily, because the ruling on some is accomplished by some, i.e., it is accomplished by intending some [individuals] that are the subject of the ruling, so intending other [individuals] with it is redundant, so understand. And it has been answered [to both objections] that the intended [meaning] is the second aspect, and the general term is used in the whole, then what is excluded is removed by the specifier, then the ruling is applied to the remainder. So the ruling is on some, which is expressed by the whole with the exclusion of some. In other words, this is like metonymy, for in it something is mentioned, and the basis of the ruling is something else alluded to by it, such as "long of sword-belt" [meaning tall]. Likewise here, the general term is mentioned, and the ruling's intended [scope] is some, by indication of the specifier. This is a way of expression; its utmost is that it is longer than expression by another concept, and there is no redundancy in it. Its example is like: "you and the son of your maternal aunt's sister" — two ways of expression, the first shorter, the second longer. So the two answers are repelled. And this is valid only in exception and its like, for due to its lack of independence and its being subsumed under the rule, it is valid in it to say that the general term with restriction expresses the remainder, and it indicates it by the indication of compounds with the specific placement as we said, or by way of metonymy as is said. As for the independent [specifier], this is not valid in it, for it is not connected to the general term but rather beneficial of a ruling contradictory to the general term's ruling in some individuals. To repel the contradiction, it becomes a contextual indicator that the ruling in the general term applies to some individuals not covered by this specifier, so necessarily the general term is used in only some, otherwise there would be definite redundancy. Also, usage is nothing but uttering a word for a meaning so that what is understood from it is the basis of the ruling, and there is no doubt that it is some, so the word is used in it, and the independent specifier is a contextual indicator, so reflect and be grateful. And [the author] objected secondly: that the intention of the remainder in the specified general term is not by a second placement and usage different from the first placement for inclusiveness and usage in it, but rather its intention is by the first [placement] and usage by it, unlike the shared [term], for in it the intention of the other meaning is by another placement, and unlike metaphor, for it is by another usage different from the usage of the literal meaning. And it is repelled that there is no discussion about the intention of the remainder within the intention of the whole as before specification — intention of the whole and within it intention of some — but rather the discussion is about its intention specifically by the context of specification, for the discussion is about the specified [term] from the general. This is a second meaning that necessarily requires a second usage; if it has the placement, then it is sharing, otherwise metaphor. And if it is established that in specification there is usage in the whole and ruling on some as the first objection established, then it has what you know: that it is valid in non-independent [specifiers] but not in independent. And you may establish the objection as: usage in a meaning is an expression of intending it from the word to be the basis of the ruling, and the remainder, as it was intended from the word during inclusiveness, is ruled upon directly; for the ruling related to the general term is related to each and every one of its individuals; the most that can be said is that along with it, some other [individuals] are intended as subject of the ruling. Likewise after specification, usage in the remainder is direct, and it is the basis of the ruling similarly, except that the ruling fell from some others by the specifier, and this does not change the first usage in the remainder. If usage and placement are not multiple, it is literal. And this is unlike other connoted meanings, for understanding the part therein is within understanding the whole and is not the basis of the ruling, and if the part is specifically intended, it becomes understood directly and the basis of the ruling similarly, so usage differs. And you may answer it by saying that in the first usage, [the remainder] was intended and understood to be the basis of the ruling established for the whole over inclusiveness, and the purpose of using the word was the ruling on the whole. As for now after specification, using it in [the remainder] and intending the remainder from it is only to restrict the ruling to it and leave the other either unaddressed or affirm the contradictory ruling in it. So this usage is different from the first usage; if by another placement, then sharing is necessary, otherwise metaphor, so understand. And [the author] objected thirdly, as said by Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi: that the form is for the whole, for it is placed for it, and after specification the some is the whole, so it is used in what it was first placed for, so there is neither sharing nor metaphorization. I say: this is repelled, for the general term is the word that encompasses what is suitable for it, literally or customarily. For that reason, i.e., because it encompasses what is suitable for it, the plural of the known individuals is not general. And if it encompasses what is suitable for it, then its encompassing of some, if it were not placed for it, would be sharing. And the interpretation of his statement that the general term is placed for encompassing all individuals of the meaning of the word, whether absolute, then encompassing all individuals of the absolute like "men", or restricted, then all individuals of the restricted like "scholars of the town", and the general term conjoined with the specifier is restricted by it, and the word encompasses all that the word restricted by this restriction is suitable for, like "scholar men" or "men except the scholars", this is only valid in non-independent [specifiers]. And this brilliant imam does not consider it a specifier, and his speech is only about the general term specified by the independent [specifier], so reflect. The Hanbalis and those who agree with them from the Shafi'is and jurists said: firstly, the application to the remainder after specification remains as it was before, and it was literal before, so it is literal now. We say: we do not concede that its application to it remains as it was before, rather before its application was with others, for it was for the whole, and now its application is for it alone. So it was said: this, i.e., its application alone does not change the attribute of its application to what it applies to, but only changes its application to the excluded. We say: we do not concede that it does not change the attribute of application, rather we say:

Commentary
This passage is a dense discussion in Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the linguistic status of a general term ('amm) after it has been specified (takhsīs). The central question is whether such a specified general term remains a literal usage (haqīqah) or becomes metaphorical (majāz) or shared (mushtarak). The author of Fawatih al-Rahamut is commenting on Musallam al-Thubut and here expands on the third discourse (al-maqālah al-thālithah) regarding linguistic principles, specifically 'the placement of common meaning.'

The author begins by distinguishing between independent (mustaqill) and non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) specifiers. For non-independent specifiers like exception (istithnā'), limiting (ghāyah), condition (shart), and description (sifah), the general term is used in its original sense for the whole, and the restriction is understood from the specifier without altering the literal usage. For example, in 'Zayd came except his servant,' the word 'Zayd' is used for all individuals, but the exception modifies the ruling. Thus, there is no metaphor or sharing.

For independent specifiers (e.g., another verse that contradicts the general term), the general term must be considered used only for the remaining individuals, because otherwise there would be redundancy. In such cases, the general term becomes metaphorical or shared.

The author then presents and refutes three objections to the view that specified general terms can be metaphorical:
1. The first objection, from Sharh al-Mukhtasar, argues that the intention of inclusiveness (irādah al-istighrāq) remains, so the term is still literal. The author responds by distinguishing between intellectual intention (ta'aqqul) and actual usage (isti'māl). If the intention is intellectual, it does not prevent metaphor. If it is actual usage, then since the ruling is on some individuals, the term must be used for them, making it metaphorical.
2. The second objection claims that intending the remainder is not a new usage but the same as the original. The author explains that after specification, the remainder is intended specifically (bi khuṣūṣihi), which is a new meaning requiring a new usage, leading to sharing or metaphor.
3. The third objection, from Imam Sarakhsi, states that the linguistic form is for the whole, and after specification the some is the whole (since the term still applies to the remaining individuals). The author refutes this by noting that the general term encompasses everything suitable for it; after specification, it only encompasses some, so if it were not placed for that some, it would be shared. Sarakhsi's view only holds for non-independent specifiers.

Finally, the author mentions the Hanbali and some Shafi'i view that the term remains literal because its application to the remainder is unchanged. The author replies that before specification, the term applied along with others (the whole), now it applies alone, which changes the application's attribute.

Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi position that after specification by an independent specifier, the general term is used metaphorically or becomes shared, while with non-independent specifiers it remains literal.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm): A term that encompasses all individuals it is suitable for, either literally or customarily.
المخصص (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ): Attribut that excludes some individuals from the general term's scope.
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): Literal usage where the word is used for what it was originally placed for.
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original placement due to a contextual clue.
المستقل (al-mustaqill): An independent specifier that provides its own ruling contradicting the general term's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this lesson, which of the following specifiers would cause a general term to become metaphorical?
A) Exception
B) Condition
C) A contradictory verse
D) Adjective

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between independent and non-independent specifiers: the former makes the general term metaphorical, while the latter keeps it literal.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Objections and Refutations in the Debate on Specified General Terms
Systematic refutation of three objections to the Hanafi view on specified general terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and evaluate the three objections to the Hanafi position on specified general terms and explain the Hanafi responses using the independent/non-independent distinction.

Core Idea
The Hanafi view survives three objections: from intention, from usage continuity, and from formal identity.

In this continuation of the passage, the author presents and refutes three objections that challenge the Hanafi position on specified general terms. These objections come from within the Hanafi tradition (e.g., from Sarakhsi) and from other schools. The Hanafi responses demonstrate the school's rigorous linguistic methodology and reinforce the importance of the independent/non-independent distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection from Sharh al-Mukhtasar
The objection claims that the intention of inclusiveness remains in the specified general term, and the exclusion of some individuals occurs from the specifier, so there is no sharing or metaphor.
This objection relies on the idea that the general term's meaning is unchanged.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first objection
The response distinguishes between intellectual intention (ta'aqqul) and actual usage (isti'māl). If the intention is intellectual, it does not prevent metaphor. If it is usage, then since the ruling applies to some, the term must be used for them, making it metaphorical. Also, using the term for the whole while the ruling is on some would entail redundancy (laghw).
The response also offers an alternative via metonymy, but only for non-independent specifiers.
3
Identify the second objection
The objection argues that intending the remainder in the specified general term is not by a second placement and usage, but by the first, unlike shared terms and metaphors.
This objection claims that the remainder was already intended in the first usage.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the second objection
The response states that after specification, the remainder is intended specifically (bi khuṣūṣihi), which is a new meaning requiring a new usage. If a placement exists for this meaning, it is shared; otherwise, metaphorical. The objection conflates implicit intention within the whole with explicit intention as the sole referent.
The response uses the analogy of connoted meanings (dalālāt tadamun) to clarify the difference.
5
Identify the third objection from Sarakhsi
Sarakhsi argues that the form is for the whole, and after specification the some is the whole, so the term is used in its original placement, implying no metaphor or sharing.
6
Analyze the Hanafi response to the third objection
The response points out that the general term encompasses what is suitable for it; after specification, it encompasses only some. If that some were not placed for it, it would be shared. The response emphasizes that Sarakhsi's view is only valid for non-independent specifiers, not for independent ones.
This reinforces the core Hanafi distinction.

يصح فيه الحكم بأن العام مع التقيد يعبر به عن الباقي، وهو دال عليه دلالة المركبات بالوضع النوعي كما قلنا، أو بطريق الكناية كما قيل، وأما المستقل فلا يصح ذلك فيه، فإنه ليس مرتبطاً بالعام بل مفيد لحكم معارض لحكم العام في بعض الأفراد، ولدفع المعارضة يصير قرينة على أن الحكم في العام على البعض الغير المتناول له هذا المخصص، فبالضرورة يكون العام مستعملاً في البعض فقط، وإلا اللغو قطعاً، وأيضاً ليس الاستعمال إلا إطلاق لفظ على معنى ليكون ما يستفاد منه مناط الحكم، ولا شك أنه هو البعض، فاللفظ مستعمل فيه والمخصص المستقل قرينة فتدبر وتشكر (و) اعترض (ثانياً بأن إرادة الباقي) في العام المخصوص (ليس بوضع دون المستقل، ولك أن تقرر الاعتراض بأن الاستعمال في المعنى عبارة عن إرادته من اللفظ ليكون مناطاً للحكم والباقي كما كان يقصد من اللفظ حين الاستغراق، ويحكم عليه بالذات، فإن الحكم المتعلق بالعام متعلق بكل بعد التخصيص فاستعماله فيه وإرادة الباقي منه إنما هو ليقصر الحكم عليه ويبقى الآخر مسكوتاً أو مثبتاً فيه الحكم المخالف، فهذا الاستعمال مغاير للاستعمال الأوّل، فإن كان بوضع آخر فالاشتراك لازم وإلا فالمجاز فافهم (و) اعترض (ثالثاً) كما قال الإمام شمس الأئمة (السرخسي : أن الصيغة للكل فإنه موضوع له وبعد التخصيص البعض هو الكل فهي مستعملة فيما وضعت أوّلاً، فلا اشتراك ولا تجوز (أقول) هذا مندفع (فإن العام هو اللفظ المستغرق لما يصلح له حقيقة أو عرفاً، ولذلك أي لكونه مستغرقاً لما يصلح له لم يكن الجمع للمعهودين عاماً) وإذا كان مستغرقاً لما يصلح له فاستغراقه للبعض لو لم يكن غير موضوع له لكان مشتركاً، The third discourse on linguistic principles: the placement of the common meaning, so understand. And this proof is not complete in the case of shortening with non-independent [specifiers], for the general term therein is not restricted to some [individuals] and used in them, rather it is used in what it was placed for with the first placement, which is the whole, so it is neither shared nor metaphorical. For the general term in the condition is used in the whole, and it is the object of the suspensory ruling, but the consequence is not actualized in some individuals due to the absence of the condition. And in the [case of] limit, the general term is used in the whole, and the ruling applies to the individuals before the limit; either the genus is considered restricted by the limit, then its generality is considered in the individuals of this restricted [genus]; on both estimates, there is no shortening, nor any use in some of what it was placed for originally. And in exception, the general term is general, and the ruling applies to what the restricted [term] is true of by excluding some. And in the adjective, generality is clear for what the genus restricted by the adjective is suitable for. And in substitution of some, it is used as it was, but the intended ruling is the substitute, and it has been explained previously. And [the author] objected firstly, as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: "that the intention of inclusiveness in the specified general term remains, and the exclusion of some occurs from the specifier, so there is neither sharing nor metaphor." I say in refutation: if the objector means by his saying "the intention of inclusiveness remains" [as] intellectual intention, since the whole is intellectually conceived, then in every metaphor likewise, the conception of the literal meaning remains, so it does not harm metaphoricity. And if he means [the intention of inclusiveness] in usage, such that it is used in it, then there is no doubt that the ruling in the specified general term applies to some, and the relevant consideration is the usage that is the basis of the ruling, so there is no intention of inclusiveness in usage, but rather of some, so metaphoricity or sharing is necessary. Moreover, if it were used in the whole while the ruling applies to some, it would entail redundancy, necessarily, because the ruling on some is accomplished by some, i.e., it is accomplished by intending some [individuals] that are the subject of the ruling, so intending other [individuals] with it is redundant, so understand. And it has been answered [to both objections] that the intended [meaning] is the second aspect, and the general term is used in the whole, then what is excluded is removed by the specifier, then the ruling is applied to the remainder. So the ruling is on some, which is expressed by the whole with the exclusion of some. In other words, this is like metonymy, for in it something is mentioned, and the basis of the ruling is something else alluded to by it, such as "long of sword-belt" [meaning tall]. Likewise here, the general term is mentioned, and the ruling's intended [scope] is some, by indication of the specifier. This is a way of expression; its utmost is that it is longer than expression by another concept, and there is no redundancy in it. Its example is like: "you and the son of your maternal aunt's sister" — two ways of expression, the first shorter, the second longer. So the two answers are repelled. And this is valid only in exception and its like, for due to its lack of independence and its being subsumed under the rule, it is valid in it to say that the general term with restriction expresses the remainder, and it indicates it by the indication of compounds with the specific placement as we said, or by way of metonymy as is said. As for the independent [specifier], this is not valid in it, for it is not connected to the general term but rather beneficial of a ruling contradictory to the general term's ruling in some individuals. To repel the contradiction, it becomes a contextual indicator that the ruling in the general term applies to some individuals not covered by this specifier, so necessarily the general term is used in only some, otherwise there would be definite redundancy. Also, usage is nothing but uttering a word for a meaning so that what is understood from it is the basis of the ruling, and there is no doubt that it is some, so the word is used in it, and the independent specifier is a contextual indicator, so reflect and be grateful. And [the author] objected secondly: that the intention of the remainder in the specified general term is not by a second placement and usage different from the first placement for inclusiveness and usage in it, but rather its intention is by the first [placement] and usage by it, unlike the shared [term], for in it the intention of the other meaning is by another placement, and unlike metaphor, for it is by another usage different from the usage of the literal meaning. And it is repelled that there is no discussion about the intention of the remainder within the intention of the whole as before specification — intention of the whole and within it intention of some — but rather the discussion is about its intention specifically by the context of specification, for the discussion is about the specified [term] from the general. This is a second meaning that necessarily requires a second usage; if it has the placement, then it is sharing, otherwise metaphor. And if it is established that in specification there is usage in the whole and ruling on some as the first objection established, then it has what you know: that it is valid in non-independent [specifiers] but not in independent. And you may establish the objection as: usage in a meaning is an expression of intending it from the word to be the basis of the ruling, and the remainder, as it was intended from the word during inclusiveness, is ruled upon directly; for the ruling related to the general term is related to each and every one of its individuals; the most that can be said is that along with it, some other [individuals] are intended as subject of the ruling. Likewise after specification, usage in the remainder is direct, and it is the basis of the ruling similarly, except that the ruling fell from some others by the specifier, and this does not change the first usage in the remainder. If usage and placement are not multiple, it is literal. And this is unlike other connoted meanings, for understanding the part therein is within understanding the whole and is not the basis of the ruling, and if the part is specifically intended, it becomes understood directly and the basis of the ruling similarly, so usage differs. And you may answer it by saying that in the first usage, [the remainder] was intended and understood to be the basis of the ruling established for the whole over inclusiveness, and the purpose of using the word was the ruling on the whole. As for now after specification, using it in [the remainder] and intending the remainder from it is only to restrict the ruling to it and leave the other either unaddressed or affirm the contradictory ruling in it. So this usage is different from the first usage; if by another placement, then sharing is necessary, otherwise metaphor, so understand. And [the author] objected thirdly, as said by Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi: that the form is for the whole, for it is placed for it, and after specification the some is the whole, so it is used in what it was first placed for, so there is neither sharing nor metaphorization. I say: this is repelled, for the general term is the word that encompasses what is suitable for it, literally or customarily. For that reason, i.e., because it encompasses what is suitable for it, the plural of the known individuals is not general. And if it encompasses what is suitable for it, then its encompassing of some, if it were not placed for it, would be sharing. And the interpretation of his statement that the general term is placed for encompassing all individuals of the meaning of the word, whether absolute, then encompassing all individuals of the absolute like "men", or restricted, then all individuals of the restricted like "scholars of the town", and the general term conjoined with the specifier is restricted by it, and the word encompasses all that the word restricted by this restriction is suitable for, like "scholar men" or "men except the scholars", this is only valid in non-independent [specifiers]. And this brilliant imam does not consider it a specifier, and his speech is only about the general term specified by the independent [specifier], so reflect. The Hanbalis and those who agree with them from the Shafi'is and jurists said: firstly, the application to the remainder after specification remains as it was before, and it was literal before, so it is literal now. We say: we do not concede that its application to it remains as it was before, rather before its application was with others, for it was for the whole, and now its application is for it alone. So it was said: this, i.e., its application alone does not change the attribute of its application to what it applies to, but only changes its application to the excluded. We say: we do not concede that it does not change the attribute of application, rather we say:
