Commentary
This passage is a dense discussion in Hanafi usul al-fiqh concerning the linguistic status of a general term ('amm) after it has been specified (takhsīs). The central question is whether such a specified general term remains a literal usage (haqīqah) or becomes metaphorical (majāz) or shared (mushtarak). The author of Fawatih al-Rahamut is commenting on Musallam al-Thubut and here expands on the third discourse (al-maqālah al-thālithah) regarding linguistic principles, specifically 'the placement of common meaning.'

The author begins by distinguishing between independent (mustaqill) and non-independent (ghayr mustaqill) specifiers. For non-independent specifiers like exception (istithnā'), limiting (ghāyah), condition (shart), and description (sifah), the general term is used in its original sense for the whole, and the restriction is understood from the specifier without altering the literal usage. For example, in 'Zayd came except his servant,' the word 'Zayd' is used for all individuals, but the exception modifies the ruling. Thus, there is no metaphor or sharing.

For independent specifiers (e.g., another verse that contradicts the general term), the general term must be considered used only for the remaining individuals, because otherwise there would be redundancy. In such cases, the general term becomes metaphorical or shared.

The author then presents and refutes three objections to the view that specified general terms can be metaphorical:
1. The first objection, from Sharh al-Mukhtasar, argues that the intention of inclusiveness (irādah al-istighrāq) remains, so the term is still literal. The author responds by distinguishing between intellectual intention (ta'aqqul) and actual usage (isti'māl). If the intention is intellectual, it does not prevent metaphor. If it is actual usage, then since the ruling is on some individuals, the term must be used for them, making it metaphorical.
2. The second objection claims that intending the remainder is not a new usage but the same as the original. The author explains that after specification, the remainder is intended specifically (bi khuṣūṣihi), which is a new meaning requiring a new usage, leading to sharing or metaphor.
3. The third objection, from Imam Sarakhsi, states that the linguistic form is for the whole, and after specification the some is the whole (since the term still applies to the remaining individuals). The author refutes this by noting that the general term encompasses everything suitable for it; after specification, it only encompasses some, so if it were not placed for that some, it would be shared. Sarakhsi's view only holds for non-independent specifiers.

Finally, the author mentions the Hanbali and some Shafi'i view that the term remains literal because its application to the remainder is unchanged. The author replies that before specification, the term applied along with others (the whole), now it applies alone, which changes the application's attribute.

Throughout, the author maintains the Hanafi position that after specification by an independent specifier, the general term is used metaphorically or becomes shared, while with non-independent specifiers it remains literal.

Key Terms
العام (al-‘āmm): A term that encompasses all individuals it is suitable for, either literally or customarily.
المخصص (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ): Attribut that excludes some individuals from the general term's scope.
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): Literal usage where the word is used for what it was originally placed for.
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for something other than its original placement due to a contextual clue.
المستقل (al-mustaqill): An independent specifier that provides its own ruling contradicting the general term's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the objections discussed in this lesson?
A) Intention of inclusiveness remains
B) Intending the remainder is not a new usage
C) The specifier must be independent to cause metaphor
D) The form is for the whole, so after specification the some is the whole

Key Takeaway
The three objections fail against the Hanafi framework because they do not account for the essential distinction between independent and non-independent specifiers.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
Effect of Takhsis on General Word: Haqiqah or Majaz?
Dispute on whether a general word remains literal after specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether a general word after specification remains literal or becomes metaphorical, and evaluate the opposing arguments from Hanbalis and Shafi'is.

Core Idea
After specification, the general word loses literal status for the remainder; it becomes either common (ishtirak) or metaphor (majaz).

This passage from Musallam al-Thubut and its commentary tackles a fundamental question in usul al-fiqh: What happens to the general word when part of its scope is excluded? Does it remain literal for the remaining individuals? The Hanafi school, represented by the author, argues that the usage changes, leading to either commonality or metaphor. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts after specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine the exact point of disagreement: whether the general word ('amm) remains haqiqah after being specified (takhsis) by a specifier (mukhasis).
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
State that the Hanafis hold the general becomes either ishtirak or majaz, and summarize their main linguistic proofs: changed usage, need for context, and failure of the repetition analogy.
3
State the opposing position and its proof
Present the Hanbali/Shafi'i view that the general remains haqiqah, with their proofs: unchanged inclusion, quick understanding, and analogy to repetition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
For each opposing argument, explain how the Hanafis rebut it: inclusion changed from whole to alone, quick understanding requires context, and the analogy fails due to single vs multiple words.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if the specified general is treated as literal, the remaining part has the same authority; if metaphorical, it requires context and may be weaker.
The author sides with the Hanafi view, citing Ibn al-Humam.

المشترك) فإن فيه إرادة المعنى الآخر بالوضع الآخر (و) بخلاف (المجاز) فإنه باستعمال آخر غير استعمال الحقيقة ودفع) بأن لا كلام في إرادة الباقي في ضمن إرادة الكل كما كان قبل التخصيص) إرادة الكل وفي ضمنها إرادة البعض (بل) الكلام ( في إرادته بخصوصه بقرينة التخصيص) فإن الكلام في المخصوص من العام (وهذا معنى ثان لا بد له من استعمال ثان) فإن كان له الوضع فالاشتراك وإلا فالمجاز، وإن قرر بأن في التخصيص استعمالاً في الكل والحكم على البعض كما قرر به الاعتراض الأول ففيه ما قد عرفت من أنه يتم في غير المستقل دون المستقل، ولك أن تقرر الاعتراض بأن الاستعمال في المعنى عبارة عن إرادته من اللفظ ليكون مناطاً للحكم والباقي كما كان يقصد من اللفظ حين الاستغراق، ويحكم عليه بالذات، فإن الحكم المتعلق بالعام متعلق بكل واحد واحد من آحاده؛ غاية ما في الباب أن مع إرادته بعض آخر متعلق الحكم، كذلك بعد التخصيص أيضاً الاستعمال في الباقي بالذات، وهو مناط الحكم كذلك، إلا أنه سقط الحكم على بعض آخر بالمخصص، وهذا لا يغير الاستعمال الأول في الباقي، وإذا لم يتعدد الاستعمال والوضع فهو حقيقة، وهذا بخلاف سائر المدلولات التضمنية، فإن فهم الجزء هناك في ضمن فهم الكل وليس مناطاً للحكم، وإذا أريد الجزء بخصوصه صار متفهماً بالذات ومناطاً للحكم كذلك، فاختلف الاستعمال، ولك أن تجيب عنه بأنه في الاستعمال الأول كان مقصوداً ومنفهماً ليكون مناطاً للحكم الثابت للكل على الاستغراق، وكان المقصود من استعمال اللفظ الحكم على الكل، وأما الآن بعد التخصيص فاستعماله فيه وإرادة الباقي منه إنما هو ليقصر الحكم عليه ويبقى الآخر مسكوتاً أو مثبتاً فيه الحكم المخالف، فهذا الاستعمال مغاير للاستعمال الأوّل، فإن كان بوضع آخر فالاشتراك لازم وإلا فالمجاز فافهم (و) اعترض (ثالثاً) كما قال الإمام شمس الأئمة (السرخسي : أن الصيغة للكل فإنه موضوع له وبعد التخصيص البعض هو الكل فهي مستعملة فيما وضعت أوّلاً، فلا اشتراك ولا تجوز (أقول) هذا مندفع (فإن العام هو اللفظ المستغرق لما يصلح له حقيقة أو عرفاً، ولذلك أي لكونه مستغرقاً لما يصلح له لم يكن الجمع للمعهودين عاماً) وإذا كان مستغرقاً لما يصلح له فاستغراقه للبعض لو لم يكن غير موضوع له لكان مشتركاً، وتوجيه كلامه بأن العام موضوع لاستغراق جميع أفراد معنى اللفظ، إن مطلقاً فالاستغراق لجميع أفراد المطلق نحو : الرجال، وإن مقيداً فلجميع أفراد المقيد نحو علماء البلد والعام المقرون مع المخصص مقيد به واللفظ متناول لجميع ما يصلح له اللفظ المقيد بهذا القيد نحو : الرجال العلماء، أو الرجال إلا العلماء، لا يتم إلا في غير المستقل، وهذا النحرير الإمام لا يراه مخصصاً، وكلامه إنما هو في العام المخصوص بالمستقل فتدبر (الحنابلة ومن وافقهم من الشافعية والفقهاء :(قالوا :أولاً: التناول) للباقي بعض التخصيص (باق كما كان قبل وقد كان حقيقة قبل) فهو حقيقة الآن (قلنا) لا نسلم أن التناول له باق كما كان قبل بل (كان) التناول قبل له (مع غيره) فإنه كان للكل (والآن) التناول له (وحده، فقيل : هذا أي كون التناول له وحده (لا يغير صفة تناوله لما يتناوله) وإنما يغير تناوله للمخرج (قلنا) لا نسلم أنه لا يغير صفة التناول (بل) نقول : حتى هو (مغير، لأن ذلك التناول كان في ضمن الكل إجمالاً، وهذا) أي التناول الذي بعد التخصيص له) بخصوصه) ثم إنه لو كان الأمر كما ذكر لكان الإنسان المستعمل في الحيوان حقيقة، لأنه كان متناولاً ،له والآن أيضاً متناول، ولم تتغير صفة التناول فافهم وتذكر ما أسلفنا فإنه ينفعك كثيراً (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : يسبق الباقي بعد التخصيص إلى الفهم وهو دليل الحقيقة، قلنا لا نسلم أنه يسبق إلى الفهم عند الإطلاق، بل يتبادر مع القرينة، وهو دليل المجاز) ويحتمل أن يحرّر معارضة (قيل : إرادة الباقي معلومة بدون (القرينة فإنه كان مفهوماً قبل أيضاً و(إنما المحتاج إليها عدم إرادة المخرج فالباقي متبادر ، وهو دليل الحقيقة ويدفع بأن الكلام في إرادة الباقي بخصوصه (لا إرادة الباقي في ضمن إرادة الكل وهذا) أي إرادة الباقي بخصوصه لا يعلم بدون القرينة) وهو علامة المجاز فتدبر ثم تذكر الفرق بين المستقل وغيره لا تغلط (قال الإمام في الاستدلال العام كتكرير (الآحاد فكل رجل بمنزلة زيد وبكر إلى غير ذلك من الأفراد، إلا أنه وضع العام له ليسهل التعبير (وفيه) أي في تكرير الآحاد (إذا بطل إرادة البعض لم يصر الباقي (مجازاً) فكذا العام (قلنا : ليس العام (مثله) أي مثل تكرير الآحاد من كل وجه بل في إفادة المعنى فقط، وفي التكرير ألفاظ متعددة مستعملة في معان متعددة، ويبطلان إرادة الموضوع له في البعض لا يبطل في الباقي من الألفاظ، وفي العام استعمال واحد للفظ واحد، فإذا بطل إرادة البعض تغير استعماله قطعاً (أقول : (و) قلنا (أيضاً لا تقريب) فيه فإنه لا يستلزم المجازية من حيث الاقتصار) وقد كان داخلاً في المدعي (بل ينافيه كما لا يخفى) لأن تكرير الآحاد إذا بطل إرادة البعض لم يصر الباقي مجازاً أصلاً، فكذا ههنا، ولعلك تقول : هب أن العام كتكرير الآحاد ، إلا أنه إذا سقط البعض فقد وجد الاقتصار في المعنى، فللباقي حيثيتان حيثية أنه بعض الآحاد المتكررة، وبهذه الحيثية، حقيقة وحيثية كونه مقتصراً عن بعض آخر وبهذه الحيثية مجاز ولا يلزم المجازية بحسب الاقتصار كونه مستعملاً فيه حتى يكلف ببيانه كما يفهم من «التحرير»، والجواب : أن الباقي بالحيثية الأولى هو مدلول مطابقي للفظ، فيلزم الاشتراك لكونه موضوعاً للكل أيضاً، وإلا يلزم المجاز، لأنه غير موضوع له، ولنعم ما قال الشيخ ابن الهمام أن مذهبه مخالف للإجماع على أن لفظاً واحداً بالنسبة إلى وضع واحد بمعنى واحد لا يكون حقيقة ومجازاً معاً فافهم وقال (أبو الحسين: لو كان الإخراج بما لا يستقل يوجب تجوزاً في العام لزم كون المسلم للمعهود (مجازاً) بيان الملازمة أن غير المستقل كالاستثناء ونحوه قيد في العام وهو مقيد به كما أن التعيين قيد مستفاد من اللازم، فلو أوجب التقييد التجوز فيه لأوجب في المعهود، وقد مر من الكلام ما يكفي لإتمام هذا المرام وما أجيب به من منع الملازمة بأن هذا العام إنما صار مجازاً لكونه استعمل في غير ما وضع له وهو البعض بخلاف المعهود فإن الاسم باق على معناه، والتعيين استفيد من اللام فساقط، فإنه قد ظهر لك فيما سبق أن العام المقترن بغير المستقل باق على معناه لا أنه مقيد بقيد غير مستقل يستفاد منهما مفهوم تقييدي يصدق على بعض الأفراد فيراد هذا البعض، فلو كان فيه تجوّز لكان من جهة التقييد وهو موجود في المعهود بعينه فإن
(The common word) because in it is intending the other meaning by a different wad (and) as opposed to (the metaphorical) because it is by a different usage other than the literal usage. And it is rebutted that there is no discussion about intending the remainder within intending the whole as it was before specification—intending the whole and within it intending the part. Rather, the discussion is about intending it specifically by the context of specification, for the discussion is about the particular from the general. This is a second meaning that necessarily requires a second usage. If it has a wad' then it is common, otherwise it is metaphorical. And if it is explained that in specification there is a usage in the whole and a ruling on the part, as stated in the first objection, then in it is what you already know—that it applies to non-independent (specifiers) but not to independent. And you may formulate the objection that usage in the meaning is expression of intending it from the word to be the basis of the ruling, and the remainder was intended from the word at the time of generality, and ruled upon essentially. For the ruling related to the general relates to every single individual; the utmost in the matter is that with intending it another part is subject to the ruling. Similarly after specification the usage in the remainder is essential, and it is the basis of the ruling as such, except that the ruling on some other part fell by the specifier, and this does not change the first usage in the remainder. And if the usage and wad' are not multiple then it is literal. This is contrary to other implicative meanings, because understanding the part there is within understanding the whole and is not the basis of the ruling, and when the part is intended specifically it becomes understood essentially and the basis of the ruling as such, so the usage differs. And you may answer that in the first usage it was intended and understood to be the basis of the ruling established for the whole in a general sense, and the purpose of using the word was to rule on the whole. But now after specification, its usage in it and intending the remainder from it is only to restrict the ruling to it and leave the other either unstated or with a contrary ruling. So this usage is different from the first usage. If it is by another wad' then commonality is necessary; otherwise it is metaphorical, so understand. (And) a third objection (as stated by Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi): that the form is for the whole because it is wad' for it, and after specification the part is the whole, so it is used in what it was originally wad' for, so there is neither commonality nor metaphor. (I say) this is repelled: for the general is the word that encompasses what is suitable for it literally or commonly, and therefore (because it encompasses what is suitable for it) the plural of the well-known is not general. And if it encompasses what is suitable for it, then its encompassing of the part, if it were not wad' for it, would be common. And the interpretation of his statement that the general is wad' for encompassing all individuals of the meaning of the word—if absolute then encompassing all individuals of the absolute like 'men', and if restricted then for all individuals of the restricted like 'scholars of the town', and the general connected to the specifier is restricted by it, and the word includes all that is suitable for the word restricted by this restriction like 'scholarly men' or 'men except scholars'—this only applies to non-independent (specifiers). And this accomplished Imam does not consider it a specifier, and his statement is only about the general specified by an independent (specifier), so reflect. (The Hanbalis and those who agree with them among the Shafi'is and jurists said: First: the inclusion) of the remainder after specification (remains as it was before, and it was literal before) so it is literal now. (We say) we do not concede that the inclusion of it remains as it was before; rather (it was) inclusion of it before (with other), for it was for the whole, (and now) the inclusion is for it (alone). So it is said: this, i.e., that inclusion is for it alone, (does not change the quality of its inclusion of what it includes, but only changes its inclusion of the excluded). (We say) we do not concede that it does not change the quality of inclusion; rather (we say): it even (changes it), because that inclusion was within the whole in a general sense, while this (inclusion after specification is for it) specifically. Then if the matter were as mentioned, then the word 'insan' used for animal would be literal, because it included it before and now also includes it, and the quality of inclusion did not change. So understand and remember what we have presented, for it will benefit you greatly. (And) they said: (Second: the remainder after specification comes to mind quickly, and that is evidence of literalness. We say) we do not concede that it comes to mind quickly without context; rather it is understood with a context, and that is evidence of metaphor. And it may be formulated as an objection: (It is said: intending the remainder is known without context, for it was understood before as well, and only need of context is to exclude the excluded, so the remainder comes to mind quickly, and that is evidence of literalness. And it is rebutted that the discussion is about intending the remainder specifically, not intending the remainder within intending the whole. And this) i.e., intending the remainder specifically is not known without context, and that is a sign of metaphor. So reflect, then remember the difference between independent and non-independent specifiers so you do not err. (The Imam said in the proof: the general is like the repetition of individuals, so every man is like Zayd and Bakr and so on among individuals, except that the general is wad' for it to ease expression. (And in it) i.e., in the repetition of individuals, (if intending some individuals fails, the remaining does not become metaphorical) so likewise the general. (We say: the general is not like it) i.e., like the repetition of individuals in every respect, but only in conveying the meaning. In repetition there are multiple words used for multiple meanings, and the failure of intending the wad'-for in some does not fail in the remaining words. In the general there is a single usage of a single word, so if intending some fails, its usage certainly changes. (I say: (and) we also say there is no comparison) in it, for it does not necessitate metaphoricalness due to restriction, and it was included in the claim (rather it contradicts it, as is not hidden). Because in the repetition of individuals, if intending some fails, the remaining does not become metaphorical at all, so likewise here. And you might say: suppose the general is like the repetition of individuals, but when some part falls, restriction in meaning occurs. So for the remainder there are two aspects: an aspect that it is some of the repeated individuals, and in that aspect it is literal; and an aspect that it is restricted from some other, and in that aspect it is metaphorical. And metaphoricalness due to restriction does not necessitate it being used in that meaning such that it requires explanation as understood from al-Tahrir. The answer: that the remainder in the first aspect is the literal meaning of the word, so commonality is necessary because it is wad' for the whole as well; otherwise metaphor is necessary because it is not wad' for it. And excellent is what Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: his madhhab contradicts the consensus that one word with respect to one wad' in one meaning cannot be both literal and metaphorical simultaneously, so understand. And Abu al-Husayn said: If the exit by what is not independent necessitated metaphoricalness in the general, then 'al-Muslimun' for the well-known would be metaphorical. Explanation of the entailment: that the non-independent specifier like exception and the like is a restriction in the general, and it is restricted by it just as specification is a restriction derived from the necessary. So if restriction necessitated metaphoricalness in it, it would necessitate it in the well-known. And the discussion that has passed is sufficient to complete this aim, and what was answered by denying the entailment—that this general became metaphorical because it is used in other than what it was wad' for, namely the part, unlike the well-known because the name remains on its meaning and specification is derived from the lām so it is omitted—has become clear to you from what preceded: that the general connected to a non-independent specifier remains on its meaning, not that it is restricted by a non-independent restriction from which a restrictive concept is derived that applies to some individuals so that this part is intended. So if there were metaphoricality, it would be due to restriction, and that exists in the well-known exactly, for in

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the effect of specification (takhsis) on the general word (عام). The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, defends the position that after specification, the remaining part of the general word is used either as a common word (if a second wad' exists) or as a metaphor (if no second wad'). The opponents—Hanbalis and some Shafi'is—argue that the general remains literal (haqiqah).

The passage begins by addressing an objection: After takhsis, the general word is used for the remainder, but this remainder was already intended within the whole. The author clarifies that the issue is about intending the remainder specifically, not merely within the whole. This specific intention requires a second usage, leading to either commonality (if a second wad' exists) or metaphor.

A second objection from Sarakhsi states that the form is for the whole, and after takhsis the part is the whole, so there is no change. The author refutes this by defining the general as encompassing all that is suitable, and notes that if the general encompassed the part without being wad' for it, it would be common. He also distinguishes between independent and non-independent specifiers.

The Hanbalis/Shafi'is raise two arguments: (1) Inclusion of the remainder remains as before, so it remains literal. The author responds that the inclusion is now for the remainder alone, changing the quality. (2) The remainder comes to mind quickly, indicating literalness. The author counters that it comes only with context, indicating metaphor.

The analogy to repetition of individuals is also refuted: in repetition, multiple words are used; in the general, a single word is used, so failure of some individuals changes the usage.

The passage ends with a discussion of independent vs. non-independent specifiers, quoting Ibn al-Humam and Abu al-Husayn, emphasizing that a single word cannot be both literal and metaphorical with respect to one wad'.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): The word that encompasses (يستغرق) everything that is suitable for it, either literally or by custom.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): Specification: restricting a general word to some of its individuals, usually by a specifier (mukhasis).
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal usage: using a word in its original wad' meaning without need for context.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage: using a word in a meaning other than its original wad' with a contextual clue (qarīnah).
اشتراك (ishtirāk): Common word: a word with multiple wad's for different meanings.
استعمال (istiʿmāl): Usage of a word to convey a meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, after a general word is specified, the remaining part is:
A) Always literal (haqiqah)
B) Always metaphorical (majaz)
C) Either a common word (ishtirak) or metaphorical (majaz)
D) Always a common word (ishtirak)

Key Takeaway
After takhsis, a general word's usage for the remainder changes; it becomes either ishtirak or majaz, not haqiqah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Does Takhsis of a General Term Make the Remaining Metaphorical?
Refuting the claim that takhsis renders the remaining part metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that takhsis does not render the remaining part metaphorical, and evaluate the key arguments in the usul dispute.

Core Idea
Takhsis does not change literalness; the general term remains haqiqah in the remaining.

This passage tackles a fundamental usul dispute: does specifying a general term (takhsis) transform the remaining individuals into a metaphorical usage? The Hanafis argue no, and the author dismantles the opposing claims through subtle linguistic reasoning. Understanding this debate is crucial for knowing when a specified general command remains a binding proof (hujja).

Guided Path
1
State the dispute and the Hanafi position
Identify the central issue: after takhsis, does the remaining part (baqi) become majaz or stay haqiqah? The Hanafi position is that it remains haqiqah.
2
Trace the opponent's argument on immediate understanding
The opponent claims the remaining part comes to mind immediately, a sign of haqiqah. The Hanafis respond that it only comes to mind with a contextual indicator (qarina), which is a sign of majaz.
This is the second argument in the passage.
3
Examine the repetition analogy and its refutation
The opponent analogizes the general to repetition of individuals (taktir al-ahad). The Hanafis point out that in repetition, multiple words are used; invalidating one does not affect the others. In a general, one word is used; invalidating part changes its usage.
The analogy fails because the general is a single word, not multiple.
4
Analyze the two-aspect objection and Ibn al-Humam's consensus
The opponent suggests the remaining has two aspects: literal as part of the whole, and metaphorical due to restriction. The Hanafis say a single word cannot be both literal and metaphorical for one meaning; Ibn al-Humam states this is consensus.
This refutes the idea that the word could have dual status.
5
Evaluate the definite article comparison and the final consensus on lam al-jins
The opponent compares to the definite article (al-mu'ahhad), where specification does not cause metaphoricalness. The Hanafis distinguish because the article is external. The passage concludes with consensus that lam al-jins is a form for umum, supporting the Hanafi position.
This consensus includes all major madhhabs.

: ليس العام (مثله) أي مثل تكرير الآحاد من كل وجه بل في إفادة المعنى فقط، وفي التكرير ألفاظ متعددة مستعملة في معان متعددة، ويبطلان إرادة الموضوع له في البعض لا يبطل في الباقي من الألفاظ، وفي العام استعمال واحد للفظ واحد، فإذا بطل إرادة البعض تغير استعماله قطعاً (أقول : (و) قلنا (أيضاً لا تقريب) فيه فإنه لا يستلزم المجازية من حيث الاقتصار) وقد كان داخلاً في المدعي (بل ينافيه كما لا يخفى) لأن تكرير الآحاد إذا بطل إرادة البعض لم يصر الباقي مجازاً أصلاً، فكذا ههنا، ولعلك تقول : هب أن العام كتكرير الآحاد ، إلا أنه إذا سقط البعض فقد وجد الاقتصار في المعنى، فللباقي حيثيتان حيثية أنه بعض الآحاد المتكررة، وبهذه الحيثية، حقيقة وحيثية كونه مقتصراً عن بعض آخر وبهذه الحيثية مجاز ولا يلزم المجازية بحسب الاقتصار كونه مستعملاً فيه حتى يكلف ببيانه كما يفهم من «التحرير»، والجواب : أن الباقي بالحيثية الأولى هو مدلول مطابقي للفظ، فيلزم الاشتراك لكونه موضوعاً للكل أيضاً، وإلا يلزم المجاز، لأنه غير موضوع له، ولنعم ما قال الشيخ ابن الهمام أن مذهبه مخالف للإجماع على أن لفظاً واحداً بالنسبة إلى وضع واحد بمعنى واحد لا يكون حقيقة ومجازاً معاً فافهم وقال (أبو الحسين: لو كان الإخراج بما لا يستقل يوجب تجوزاً في العام لزم كون المسلم للمعهود (مجازاً) بيان الملازمة أن غير المستقل كالاستثناء ونحوه قيد في العام وهو مقيد به كما أن التعيين قيد مستفاد من اللازم، فلو أوجب التقييد التجوز فيه لأوجب في المعهود، وقد مر من الكلام ما يكفي لإتمام هذا المرام وما أجيب به من منع الملازمة بأن هذا العام إنما صار مجازاً لكونه استعمل في غير ما وضع له وهو البعض بخلاف المعهود فإن الاسم باق على معناه، والتعيين استفيد من اللام فساقط، فإنه قد ظهر لك فيما سبق أن العام المقترن بغير المستقل باق على معناه لا أنه مقيد بقيد غير مستقل يستفاد منهما مفهوم تقييدي يصدق على بعض الأفراد فيراد هذا البعض، فلو كان فيه تجوّز لكان من جهة التقييد وهو موجود في المعهود بعينه فإن مدخول اللام على معناه وقد تقيد بالتعيين المستفاد من اللام فيستفاد معنى مركب تقييدي يصدق على فرد معين أو أفراد معينة فتدبر (والجواب عنه (كما في «المختصر بأن المجموع) المركب من الاسم واللام (هو) الدال على المعين المعهود وكل من جزأيه، كزاي زيد، لأن الكلمتين من شدة الامتزاج صارتا كلمة واحدة مندفع لأنه بعد العلم بأنهما كلمتان موضوع كل منهما لمعنى مجرد اعتبار مناولاً واقعية له مع أنه قال الخصم به) أي بكون الدال هو المجموع من العام والمخصص على ما نقل عنه في المعتمد فإنه نقل عنه أن العام في صورة التخصيص ليس حقيقة ولا مجازاً، ومجموع الأمرين من العام والاستثناء حقيقة، ثم إن هذا القول بعيد محض، ولعل مراده أن العام في صورة التخصيص ليس حقيقة في الباقي ولا مجازاً فيه، فإنه غير مستعمل فيه : بل في الكل، وإنما الحقيقة فيه مجموع العام والاستثناء، فإنه موضوع للباقي بالوضع النوعي الذي للمركبات فتدبر (وما قيل في الجواب (إن للمعرف بالعهد وضعين للجنس قبل دخول اللام حالة التنكير (وللمعهود) بعده فلا يلزم المجازية فيه بخلاف هذا العام، لأن وضعه ليس إلا للكل فلا يخفى ما فيه لأنه ليس الاسم موضوعاً لمعنيين، وإلا لزم الاشتراك بل الاسم موضوع للجنس واللام للمعهودية، فيحصل من المجموع الشخص المعهود كهذا الإنسان، وفيه تأمل يظهر بالتأمل، ولأنا سلمنا أن العام موضوع للكل لكنه مستعمل فيه والإخراج من الاستثناء فيحصل من المجموع معنى هو الباقي. وبعد التنزل يمكن أن يقال مثله في العام المقارن لغير المستقل. ثم أراد أن يحقق الحق في وضع المعرف فقال : والحق أن لا فرق بين المعرفة والنكرة إلا بالإشارة إلى (المعلومية في الأولى (وعدمها) في الثانية وتحقيقه أن الاسم موضوع للجنس من حيث هو والانتشار إنما يجيء من التنوين، وإذا دخل عليه اللام الموضوع للإشارة وأسقط منه التنوين زال الانتشار وصار الجنس مشاراً معهوداً، فاللام ليس إلا لتعريف ،الجنس، ثم قد يقصد الإشارة لخصوص المقام إلى حصته المعينة المعهودة، وقد يقصد إلى حصة منتشرة، وقد يقصد إلى جميع الأفراد وقد لا تقصد الفردية أصلاً على حسب ما يقتضيه المقام، كذا قال أهل العربية (وعلى هذا فعموم المعرف بتعريف الجنس إنما ينشأ من المقام ونشأته من المقام تحتمل وجهين أحدهما: أن يفهم الجنس المشار إليه من المعرّف ويعلم تحققه في كل الأفراد من قرينة خارجة وهي المقام، وهذا باطل قطعاً وإن ارتضى به أكثر علماء العربية، فإنه قد تواتر استدلال الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم ومن بعدهم من المهرة بنفس اللفظ على عموم الأحكام ولم ينظروا إلى أمر آخر على أن الجنس المحكوم عليه متحقق في الكل بل إنما حكموا لانفهام العموم بنفس اللفظ فقط .

،
الثاني : أن يكون المعرّف بلام الجنس مستعملاً في العموم مجازاً، وهذا أيضاً بعيد، وإلا منع العموم في صورة عدم القرينة الصارفة من مطلق الجنس إليه ولم ينقل بل الذي تواتر هو حملهم المعرف على العموم من دون حاجة إلى قرينة دالة عليه وصرفهم إلى الجنس إنما كان للصارف من العموم لكن عدوه من الصيغ الموضوعة له والعادون هم أهل الأصول قاطبة من الحنفية والشافعية والمالكية والحنبلية بل الظاهرية أيضاً، وهذا أيضاً يدل
The third discourse on linguistic preliminaries. Up to: He is (a changer, because that [previous] inclusion was within the whole implicitly, and this) i.e., the inclusion after specification is specific to it. Then if the matter were as mentioned, the word 'human' used to mean animal would be literal, because it included it before and now also includes it, and the nature of inclusion did not change. So understand and recall what we have presented, for it will benefit you greatly. (And) they said: (Secondly: the remaining part after specification comes to mind, and that is evidence of literalness.) We say: we do not concede that it comes to mind absolutely; rather it occurs to mind with a contextual indicator, and that is evidence of metaphoricalness. (And it is possible to formulate an objection. It was said: the intention of the remaining part is known without [contextual indicator] because it was understood before as well; what needs the indicator is the non-intention of the excluded part; thus the remaining is immediate, and that is evidence of literalness. And the repulsion is that the discussion is about intending the remaining specifically (not intending the remaining within the intention of the whole). And this) i.e., intending the remaining specifically is not known without a contextual indicator, and that is a sign of metaphoricalness. So reflect, then recall the difference between independent and other, lest you err. (The Imam said in inference: the general is like the repetition of individuals, so every man is like Zaid and Bakr and so on among individuals, except that the general was put in place to ease expression. (And in it) i.e., in the repetition of individuals (if the intention of some is invalidated, the remaining does not become metaphorical), so also the general. (We say: the general is not like it) i.e., like the repetition of individuals in every respect; rather [only] in conveying meaning. In repetition there are multiple words used for multiple meanings, and the invalidation of the intended meaning in some does not invalidate it in the remaining words; while in the general there is one usage of one word; so if the intention of some [individuals] is invalidated, its usage certainly changes. (I say: (And) we also (say: no approximation in it) for it does not necessitate metaphoricalness by virtue of restriction, and it was already included in the claim (rather it contradicts it, as is not hidden). Because in repetition of individuals, if the intention of some is invalidated, the remaining does not become metaphorical at all, so here too. And perhaps you say: suppose the general is like repetition of individuals, but when some fall away, restriction in meaning has occurred. So the remaining has two aspects: an aspect that it is some of the repeated individuals—in this aspect it is literal—and an aspect that it is restricted from some others—in this aspect it is metaphorical. And metaphoricalness by virtue of restriction does not necessitate it being used in that [restricted meaning] such that one is required to explain it, as understood from al-Tahrir. The answer: that the remaining in the first aspect is the intended meaning of the word by direct implication, so either it entails sharedness (because the word is also placed for the whole) or else it entails metaphoricalness because it is not placed for that [remaining]. And excellent is what Shaykh Ibn al-Humam said: his position contradicts consensus, that a single word with respect to one placement for one meaning cannot be both literal and metaphorical at once. Understand. And (Abu al-Husayn said: if exclusion by a non-independent [specifier] necessitated metaphoricalness in the general, then the word 'Muslim' used for the previously known [group] would be metaphorical. Explanation of the entailment: the non-independent [specifier] like an exception is a qualification of the general, and it is qualified by it, just as determination is a qualification derived from the necessary [implication]. So if qualification necessitated metaphoricalness in it, it would necessitate it in the previously known [group]. And what has passed of discussion is sufficient to complete this aim. And what was answered by denying the entailment: that this general became metaphorical because it was used for other than what it was placed for, i.e., the part, unlike the previously known; there the noun remains on its original meaning, and determination is derived from the definite article, so it falls away. For it has become clear to you earlier that the general accompanied by a non-independent [specifier] remains on its original meaning; it is not qualified by a non-independent specifier from which a qualification concept is derived that applies to some individuals, and this part is intended. So if there were metaphoricalness, it would be due to qualification, and that exists in the previously known as well, for the object of the definite article is on its original meaning and has been qualified by the determination derived from the definite article, so a composite qualification meaning is derived that applies to a particular individual or particular individuals. So reflect. (And the answer to it is (as in al-Mukhtasar: that the composite) of the noun and the definite article (is) what indicates the determined previously known, and each of its parts is like the letter 'zay' in Zaid, because the two words due to strong amalgamation became one word.) This is rejected because after knowing that they are two words each placed for a meaning, one cannot ignore this reality. Especially since the opponent admitted that (the indicator is the composite of the general and the specifier, as transmitted from him in al-Mu'tamad: he transmitted that the general in the case of specification is neither literal nor metaphorical, and the composite of the two things, the general and the exception, is literal.) Then this saying is purely far-fetched. Perhaps he meant that the general in the case of specification is not literal in the remaining nor metaphorical in it, for it is not used in it; rather it is used in the whole; the literalness is only in the composite of the general and the exception, for it is placed for the remaining by the type of placement typical of composites. So reflect. (And what was said in answer: that the definite by previous mention has two placements: for the genus before the definite article in the indefinite state, and for the previously known after it, so metaphoricalness does not entail in it, unlike this general because its placement is only for the whole. It is not hidden that this is problematic: the noun is not placed for two meanings, otherwise it would entail homonymy; rather the noun is placed for the genus, and the definite article for previous knownness, so from the composite the previously known individual results, like 'this human'.) In this there is a point for reflection. And we concede that the general is placed for the whole, but it is used in it, and the exclusion is from the exception, so from the composite a meaning results that is the remaining. After descending one can say the same about the general accompanied by a non-independent [specifier]. Then he wanted to verify the truth about the placement of the definite article and said: The truth is that there is no difference between the definite and indefinite except in indicating knownness in the former and its absence in the latter. Its verification: the noun is placed for the genus as such, and spread comes from the tanwin. When the definite article placed for indication enters and the tanwin is dropped, the spread disappears and the genus becomes pointed to as known. So the definite article is only for making the genus definite. Then one may intend to point to its particular determined portion for a specific reason, or to a spread portion, or to all individuals, or may not intend individuality at all, according to what the context requires. This is what the grammarians said. (And on this, the generality of the definite by genus-definition arises only from the context. Its arising from the context may have two aspects: one: that the pointed-to genus is understood from the definite noun and its existence in every individual is known from an external contextual indicator. That is absolutely false, even if most grammarians approve it. For the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and those after them among the proficient, have recurrently inferred from the word itself the generality of rulings and did not look at anything else; they ruled because generality is understood from the word alone. Second: that the definite by lam of genus is used for generality metaphorically. This is also far-fetched; otherwise generality would be prevented in the absence of a contextual indicator diverting from the mere genus to it, and this has not been reported. Rather what is recurrent is that they understood the definite as general without needing any indicator; their diversion to the genus was only because of a diverting factor from generality. But they counted it among the forms placed for it. And those who count it are all the scholars of usul from the Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, even the Zahiris. This too is evidence.)
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a sophisticated refutation of the claim that specification (takhsis) of a general term (amm) renders the remaining part (baqi) a metaphorical usage (majaz). The Hanafi position is that after takhsis, the general term remains haqiqah (literal) in the remaining individuals, not majaz. The author, a Hanafi commentator, dismantles various arguments of the opponents.

The passage begins by rejecting the opponent's argument that after takhsis, the remaining part comes to mind immediately without a contextual indicator (qarina). The Hanafi response clarifies that it comes to mind only with a qarina, which is a sign of majaz, not haqiqah.

Next, the opponent analogizes the general term to a repetition of individuals (taktir al-ahad). The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes: in repetition, many words are used; invalidation of one does not change the others. But in a single general term, one word is used for a set; if part of the set is invalidated, the usage changes.

An intermediate objection considers that the remaining has two aspects (literal as part of the whole, metaphorical due to restriction). The Hanafi response: treating the word as literal for the remaining would require it to be placed for both the whole and the part (homonymy) or else be metaphorical. The statement of Ibn al-Humam is cited: a word cannot be simultaneously literal and metaphorical for one meaning.

Then Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection is addressed: if a non-independent specifier (like istithna) causes metaphoricalness, then a definite noun (like 'al-Muslimun' for a known group) would also be metaphorical, because the definite article specifies. The Hanafi answer: the general term becomes metaphorical because it is used for a part beyond its original placement, whereas the definite noun remains on its original meaning, and the specification comes from the article, which is external. This is weak, and the author notes that the general with a specifier is similar to the definite: the composite indicates the specified meaning.

Finally, the author delves into the nature of the definite article (al-ta'rif). He rejects the idea that 'al' for genus indicates generality by context (maqam) because the Companions inferred generality from the word itself. He also rejects the alternative that it is used metaphorically for generality. Instead, the majority of usulis (including all madhhabs) hold that the definite by lam al-jins is a form placed for 'umum. This supports the Hanafi position that takhsis does not create metaphoricalness but rather the general term remains haqiqah in its proper sense.

The passage is dense but systematically defends the Hanafi usul principle that specification does not transform the literal meaning into metaphorical; rather, the literal meaning is retained for the remaining individuals, and the specifier is an external factor that does not change the word's essential placement.

Key Terms
العام (al-amm): A term that by its placement encompasses all its individuals without restriction.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): The exclusion of some individuals from the general term by a specifier (mukhassis).
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Usage of a word in its original placed-for meaning.
المجاز (al-majaz): Usage of a word in other than its original placed-for meaning with a contextual indicator (qarina) preventing the literal meaning.
الباقي (al-baqi): The individuals of the general term that remain after takhsis.
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator that clarifies the intended meaning, either for haqiqah or majaz.
المعرف بالعهد (al-mu'ahhad): A definite noun referring to a previously known specific entity or group.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in the passage, why does the analogy of the general term with repetition of individuals fail to support the claim that the remaining part becomes metaphorical?
A) Repetition uses multiple words, so invalidating one does not change the others; the general uses one word.
B) Repetition is always literal.
C) The general term is always metaphorical.
D) The repetition analogy actually supports metaphoricalness.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school definitively holds that takhsis does not transform a general term into a metaphor; it remains a literal proof for the remaining individuals, supported by consensus on lam al-jins.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Does the Definite Article Indicate Generality Inherently? Hanafi vs. Arab Linguists
Hanafi usul analysis of lam al-ta'rif and whether it indicates generality by itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on lam al-jins and generality, identify the opposing linguists' argument, and evaluate the Hanafi proof from tawatur and ijma.

Core Idea
The definite article with lam al-jins indicates generality by itself, proven by the tawatur of the Companions and the ijma of usul scholars.

How do we know that a general Arabic phrase like 'al-thief' in the verse on amputation applies to all thieves? This passage tackles a foundational usul dispute: does the definite article inherently indicate generality, or does it only define the genus, leaving generality to context? The Hanafi school, along with other madhahib, insists on the word itself being sufficient, while some Arab linguists argue otherwise. We will explore the evidence and the force of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
Pinpoint the usul scholars (Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, Zahiris) vs. the Arab linguists. The usul scholars claim that the definite article (lam al-jins) indicates generality (istighraq) by itself. The linguists say it only indicates the genus and generality arises from the context (muqam).
This is an ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh, not in fiqh directly.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the tawatur (continuous practice) of the Companions and later skilled scholars who inferred general rulings from the word itself without needing external clues. They understood generality directly from the lafz.
This is a proof from practice (tawatur 'amali) and consensus (ijma).
3
Understand the opposing dalil
The linguists argue that the definite article (lam) is only for definition of the genus (ta'rif al-jins). Generality requires a contextual clue (qarinah) that indicates the intention to encompass all individuals.
They point to the original linguistic placement (wad') as their basis.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response refutes the linguists on two grounds: (1) the Companions did not need context to infer generality, and (2) the claim that the definite article has become a 'customary reality' (haqiqah 'urfiyyah) for generality is forced and contradicts the rules of wad'.
The text also mentions a rebuttal via the possibility of general placement (wad' 'amm) for specific subject matters (al-mawdu' lahu al-khas).
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in fiqh, a general text like 'Wa al-sariqu...' is applied to all thieves unless a specific mukhassis is found. Under the linguists' view, one would need a contextual clue to even apply the general meaning, potentially narrowing the scope before specification.
This difference affects how we read Qur'anic verses and hadith.

فيحصل من المجموع معنى هو الباقي. وبعد التنزل يمكن أن يقال مثله في العام المقارن لغير المستقل. ثم أراد أن يحقق الحق في وضع المعرف فقال : والحق أن لا فرق بين المعرفة والنكرة إلا بالإشارة إلى (المعلومية في الأولى (وعدمها) في الثانية وتحقيقه أن الاسم موضوع للجنس من حيث هو والانتشار إنما يجيء من التنوين، وإذا دخل عليه اللام الموضوع للإشارة وأسقط منه التنوين زال الانتشار وصار الجنس مشاراً معهوداً، فاللام ليس إلا لتعريف الجنس، ثم قد يقصد الإشارة لخصوص المقام إلى حصته المعينة المعهودة، وقد يقصد إلى حصة منتشرة، وقد يقصد إلى جميع الأفراد وقد لا تقصد الفردية أصلاً على حسب ما يقتضيه المقام، كذا قال أهل العربية (وعلى هذا فعموم المعرف بتعريف الجنس إنما ينشأ من المقام ونشأته من المقام تحتمل وجهين أحدهما: أن يفهم الجنس المشار إليه من المعرّف ويعلم تحققه في كل الأفراد من قرينة خارجة وهي المقام، وهذا باطل قطعاً وإن ارتضى به أكثر علماء العربية، فإنه قد تواتر استدلال الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم ومن بعدهم من المهرة بنفس اللفظ على عموم الأحكام ولم ينظروا إلى أمر آخر على أن الجنس المحكوم عليه متحقق في الكل بل إنما حكموا لانفهام العموم بنفس اللفظ فقط . أحد لنقل من ، الثاني : أن يكون المعرّف بلام الجنس مستعملاً في العموم مجازاً، وهذا أيضاً بعيد، وإلا منع العموم في صورة عدم القرينة الصارفة من مطلق الجنس إليه ولم ينقل بل الذي تواتر هو حملهم المعرف على العموم من دون حاجة إلى قرينة دالة عليه وصرفهم إلى الجنس إنما كان للصارف من العموم لكن عدوه من الصيغ الموضوعة له والعادون هم أهل الأصول قاطبة من الحنفية والشافعية والمالكية والحنبلية بل الظاهرية أيضاً، وهذا أيضاً يدل دلالة واضحة على بطلان رأي أهل العربية فإنهم أشد مهارة من أهل العربية، فالقول المخالف لإجماعهم باطل البتة، ثم أشار إلى ما قيل في تأويل الإجماع بقوله (إلا أن يقال : صار) المعرف باللام (حقيقة عرفية) في الاستغراق ( فتدبر) وما قال أهل العربية باعتبار أصل الوضع، وهذا أيضاً بعيد، فإن الوضع إنما يعرف بالاستعمال والتبادر ، وإذا جوّز حمل التبادر على كونه معنی عرفياً وفتح هذا الباب انسد باب العلم بالوضع، قال المصنف في «الحاشية»، أقول: يمكن أن يقال : إن المتعارف في الوضع العام للموضوع له الخاص وإن كان أن الموضوع له جزئيات حقيقة للمفهوم الكلي الذي جعل آلة للوضع لكن يجوز أن تكون كليات تحتها جزئيات، وإذا عرفت ذلك فليجز أن يكون لام التعريف من هذا القبيل فإنها مع إشارتها إلى معلومية الماهية تتنوع إلى أقسامه المعروفة، وحينئذ تكون تلك الأقسام معاني وضعية لها، وعلى هذا عموم مدخولها كعموم مدخل كل والنكرة الواقعة تحت النفي، وهذا وإن كان تكلفاً لكنه أوفق بمذهب أهل العربية وعلماء الأصول انتهى . ولعل وجه التكلف أن اللام لم يبق حيئنذ موضوعاً لتعريف المدخول فقط، بل له مع استغراق الآحاد أو المعهودية، وهذا مخالف لظواهر أقوال أهل العربية، ثم على هذا يكون العهد الذهني والإشارة إلى الجنس من الموضوع له، وهذا ينبو عنه «قواعد الأصول»، وأيضاً يشهد التتبع أن الحمل عليهما فيما إذا لم يستقم العهد والاستغراق ففهمهما بالقرينة ولذا لم ينقل عن أحد التكلم في جواب المستدلين بإبداء احتمال واحد منهما فالحق أن الاسم في حالة التنكير للجنس أو للفرد المنتشر، وفي حالة التعريف إذا لم يكن هناك معهود لجميع الأفراد استغراقاً فالتواتر استدلال السلف به، والمصلح لكلام أهل العربية يستحق أن يقال في حقه : ولن يصلح العطار ما أفسد الدهر هذا والعلم الحق عند علام الغيوب
The meaning of the definite article's object is restricted by the specification derived from the definite article, so a restrictive compound meaning is derived that applies to a particular individual or particular individuals, so reflect. The answer to it is as in al-Mukhtasar: that the compound of the noun and the definite article is what indicates the specified known object, each of its two parts individually is like the za' of Zaid, because the two words have become so fused that they become one word, a rebuttal because after knowing that they are two words each placed for a meaning, a mere mental consideration is not actual. With the opponent's claim that the indicator is the combination of the general and the specific according to what is transmitted from him in al-Mu'tamad: that the general in the form of specification is neither reality nor metaphor, and the combination of the two matters, the general and the exception, is reality. Then this statement is far-fetched. Perhaps his meaning is that the general in the form of specification is not a reality in the remainder nor a metaphor for it, for it is not used for that; rather it is used for the whole. The reality in it is only the combination of the general and the exception, for it is placed for the remainder by the type of placement that compounds have. Then he wanted to verify the truth about the placement of the definite noun and said: The truth is that there is no difference between the definite and the indefinite except by indication of knownness in the first and its absence in the second. The verification is that the noun is placed for the genus as such. The distribution only comes from tanwin. When the definite article, placed for indication, enters upon it and tanwin is removed, the distribution ceases and the genus becomes pointed to and known. So the definite article is only for defining the genus. Then sometimes it indicates a particular specific portion meant by the context, sometimes a distributed portion, sometimes all individuals, and sometimes it does not intend individuality at all, according to what the context requires. This is what the Arabic grammarians said. And on this basis, the generality of the definite noun by definition of the genus arises from the context. Its arising from the context has two possible aspects. One: that the genus pointed to in the definite noun is understood, and its realization in every individual is known from an external clue, which is the context. This is absolutely false, even if most Arabic grammarians approve of it. For indeed, the Companions and the skilled scholars after them constantly used the word itself to infer the generality of rulings without looking to any other matter, in that the genus being ruled upon is realized in all. Rather they ruled because of the understanding of generality from the word itself only. The second: that the definite noun with the definite article of genus is used for generality metaphorically. This is also far-fetched, otherwise generality would be prevented in the absence of a clue turning away from absolute genus to it, and it has not been transmitted. Rather what is frequently transmitted is that they applied the definite noun to generality without needing a clue indicating it, and their turning to the genus only occurred when there was a turning away from generality. But they counted it among the expressions placed for it. And the counters are the usul scholars among the Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis, Hanbalis, and even the Zahiris. This also clearly indicates the invalidity of the opinion of the Arabic grammarians, for they are more adept than the grammarians. So the statement opposing their consensus is absolutely invalid. Then he indicated what was said regarding the interpretation of the consensus by saying: 'unless it is said that the definite noun with the definite article has become a customary reality for generality, so reflect.' And what the Arabic grammarians said considering the original placement is also far-fetched, for placement is only known through usage and immediate understanding. If one allows the understanding of the immediate understanding as being a customary meaning and opens this door, the door of knowledge of placement is closed. The author said in al-Hashiyah: I say: It can be said that the customary practice in the general placement for the specific what-is-placed-for, even if it is that the what-is-placed-for is particulars that are realities of the universal concept which was made a tool for placement, but it is permissible that there be universals under which there are particulars. And if you know that, then let the definite article be of this category, for it, with its indication of the knownness of the essence, is divided into its known divisions. Then those divisions become its placed meanings. On this basis, the generality of its object is like the generality of the entry of 'kull' and the indefinite under negation. Although this is forced, it is more consistent with the school of the Arabic grammarians and the usul scholars. End. Perhaps the reason for the forcedness is that the definite article then is no longer placed only for the definition of its object, but also has meaning with the encompassing of individuals or knownness. This contradicts the apparent statements of the Arabic grammarians. Then on this basis, the mental covenant and indication of the genus would be part of the what-is-placed-for, which is rejected by the rules of usul. Also, examination testifies that applying them to both when the covenant and encompassing are not established, so they are understood by context. Therefore it is not transmitted from anyone speaking in response to those who argue by presenting one of the two possibilities. So the truth is that the noun in the indefinite state is for the genus or the distributed individual, and in the definite state, if there is no known object, it is for all individuals as encompassment. So the frequent practice of the Salaf in using it, and the one who would correct the speech of the Arabic grammarians deserves to be said of him: But the perfume seller will not fix what time has corrupted. This, and true knowledge is with the All-Knower of the Unseen.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between Hanafi usul scholars (and other madhahib) and Arabic linguists (ahl al-'arabiyya) regarding whether the definite article with lam al-jins (definite article of genus) indicates generality (istighraq) inherently or requires external context. The author explains several views, starting with the argument from al-Mukhtasar that the combination of the general term and the exception (istithna) is a compound that indicates the specified remainder, but this is refuted because the words remain separate. Then the discussion shifts to the definite article: the Hanafi position, supported by the consensus of usul scholars including the Companions and all madhahib, is that the definite article alone indicates generality (e.g., 'al-insan' for all humans). The grammarians argued that generality arises from the context (qarinah), but this is refuted by the tawatur (continuous practice) of the Salaf in using the literal word (lafz) for general rulings without needing external clues. The author also considers the possibility of customary reality (haqiqah 'urfiyyah) but finds it forced because it would require the definite article to have multiple placed meanings, conflicting with Arab linguists' principles. The commentary clarifies that this is a foundational usul dispute affecting many furoo' (branches) in fiqh.

Key Terms
اللام (al-lam): The definite article that indicates definition (ta'rif) of the noun; its different uses include lam al-jins (for genus) and lam al-'ahd (for a previously known specific object).
العموم (al-'umum): The inclusion of all individuals of a category by a single word (lafz). In this passage, it is the quality of a general term (amm) that encompasses all individuals without needing external clues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul position presented in the passage, what is the primary evidence that the definite article with lam al-jins indicates generality by itself?
A) The definition of the word 'al' in Arabic grammar books.
B) The tawatur of the Companions inferring general rulings from the word itself.
C) A clear verse from the Quran stating that 'al' means generality.
D) The logical impossibility of understanding generality without external clues.

Key Takeaway
The definite article with lam al-jins inherently indicates generality, proven by the continuous practice of the Companions and the consensus of usul scholars across madhahib.

Are Adjectives Linguistic Specifiers? Hanafi Refutation of Al-Qadi
Hanafi refutation of external specification by adjectives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that adjectives are linguistic specifiers (takhsis lafzi) and refute the opposing view that they are external specifiers.

Core Idea
Adjectives restrict generality linguistically; rare full coverage does not disprove the rule.

In Hanafi usul, adjectives (sifah) are considered linguistic specifiers (mukhassisat lafziyyah) that restrict the generality of the described noun. However, some scholars like al-Qadi argued that adjectives are independent external specifiers, not linguistic ones. This passage presents the Hanafi counter-argument, defending the linguistic nature of adjectival specification through a careful analysis of coverage, restriction, and the role of rare exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
State the Hanafi position (adjectives are linguistic specifiers) and al-Qadi's position (adjectives are external specifiers).
Al-Qadi's view is that specification by adjectives is not linguistic but comes from outside the word.
2
Examine al-Qadi's proof
Understand al-Qadi's argument: the adjective may sometimes include all individuals of the general term, so the description itself is not specification; rather, specification is from an external factor.
The proof is based on the possibility of the adjective covering the entire genus.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Follow the Hanafi response: description is a form of restriction (taqyid); coverage under absoluteness is greater than under restriction. Even if the adjective sometimes covers all, this is rare and does not negate the linguistic nature.
The rebuttal also uses the analogy of condition (shart) which may sometimes equal the consequent but is still linguistic.
4
Grasp the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: if a general command is qualified by an adjective (e.g., 'Honor the learned students'), the Hanafi view holds that the adjective linguistically restricts to learned students only, while al-Qadi would require external evidence.
This affects how rulings are derived from revealed texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي مثله أي مثل ما قال أبو الحسين (إلا أن الصفة عنده كأنه) الظاهر كأنها (مخصص مستقل فلم يجعل المخصوص بها حقيقة، ولما كان الظاهر فيها عدم الاستقلال أشار إلى توجيهه وقال وتحقيقه أن تخصيصها ليست لفظية بل من خارج والمخصوص منه مجاز ،البتة، وعدم كونه لفظياً (بدليل أن الصفة قد تشمل جميع أفراد العام فلا يكون التوصيف نفسه تخصيصاً، بل التخصيص فيه من خارج (كذا في شرح المختصر»، أقول ليس الأمر كما ظن هو (بل) التخصيص بها (لفظية، لأن التوصيف تقييد وهو ضد الإطلاق ومن البين أن التناول حال الإطلاق أكثر منه حال التقييد، فإن قلت : يجوز أن يكون التقييد باعتبار العقل فقط، وحينئذ فتكون الصفة مساوية للموصوف ، قال : وقلما يكون التقييد (اعتبارياً) فلا يقاس عليه ( على أنه منقوض بالشرط) فإنه قد يكون مساوياً للجزاء، فلا يكون نفسه مخصصاً ، فالتخصيص فيه من خارج أيضاً فلا يكون لفظياً (فتأمل
And al-Qadi said the same, i.e., similar to what Abu al-Husain said, except that the adjective according to him is apparently an independent specifier, so he did not make the specified by it a reality. Since its lack of independence was apparent, he pointed to its justification and said: Its specification is not linguistic but from outside, and the specified-from is absolute metaphor. It is not linguistic as evidenced by the fact that the adjective may include all individuals of the general term, so the description itself is not specification; rather the specification in it is from outside (as in Sharh al-Mukhtasar). I say: The matter is not as he thought; rather specification by it is linguistic, because description is restriction and it is the opposite of absoluteness, and it is clear that coverage in the state of absoluteness is more than coverage in the state of restriction. If you say: Restriction may be only mentally, and then the adjective would be equivalent to the described, he said: Mental restriction rarely occurs, so it is not analogized upon it. Furthermore, it is refuted by the condition (shart), for it may be equivalent to the consequent (jaza'), so it itself is not a specifier; thus specification in it is from outside as well, so it is not linguistic. So reflect.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation (critical refinement) of al-Qadi's view that adjectives (sifah) are independent specifiers (mukhassis mustaqill) that are not linguistic (lafzi) but rather external (khariji). The author (sharh) argues that specification by adjectives is indeed linguistic because description (tawsif) is a form of restriction (taqyid), which is the opposite of absoluteness (itlaq). Since a general term in its absolute state has a wider coverage than when it is restricted, the adjective linguistically limits it. Al-Qadi's argument that adjectives sometimes cover all individuals (so the description itself is not specification) is rebutted: rare cases where the adjective is equivalent to the described do not refute the general rule. Similarly, the analogy with conditions (shart) is rejected because conditions are also usually restrictive but sometimes are equivalent to the consequent, but that doesn't disprove their linguistic specification in typical cases. The phrase 'gilt' at the end indicates this is a puzzle for the reader to reflect on. The passage is an important usul principle: adjectives are considered mukhassisat (specificators) at the level of lafz, not just external or rational limitations.

Key Terms
الصفة (al-sifah): Adjective that qualifies a noun; in usul, it is considered a specifier (mukhassis) that restricts the generality of the described term.
التقييد (al-taqyid): Restriction; the act of limiting the scope of a general term by adding a qualification.
الإطلاق (al-itlaq): Absoluteness; the state of a term being unrestricted in scope.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Hanafi argument that specification by adjectives is linguistic (lafzi)?
A) Adjectives are always synonymous with the noun they describe.
B) Description is a form of restriction (taqyid), and restriction reduces coverage compared to absoluteness.
C) Adjectives are external specifiers because they come from outside the word.
D) The Sahaba never used adjectives to specify general terms.

Key Takeaway
Adjectives are linguistic specifiers (takhsis lafzi) that restrict the generality of the described term, even if in rare cases they may extensionally cover all individuals.

Al-Jassas on the Definition of General (Amm)
Al-Jassas's definition of 'amm without istighraq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state al-Jassas's definition of 'amm and distinguish it from the majority Hanafi position regarding istighraq.

Core Idea
Al-Jassas: 'amm is real for any non-limited plurality, not only for all individuals.

This lesson examines a distinctive definition of the general term ('amm) attributed to the early Hanafi usul scholar Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi. Unlike the majority position that requires full universality (istighraq), al-Jassas argues that 'amm is a reality (haqiqah) for any plural set that remains non-limited after specification. Understanding this definition is crucial for debates about takhsis (specification) and the scope of general commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim of al-Jassas
Read the passage and pinpoint al-Jassas's statement: 'al-istighraq fi al-amm ghayr shart' (encompassment is not a condition for the general term). Note that he replaces universal coverage with the requirement of a non-limited plurality.
Compare this with the usual Hanafi definition that 'amm must include every individual to which it applies.
2
Examine the conditions for 'amm according to al-Jassas
From the passage, extract the two conditions: (1) the term must include a plural set (jama') of more than two individuals; (2) the individuals must be non-limited (ghayr mahsurin), i.e., not countable or enumerated. If after specification the remaining set is still non-limited, the term remains real.
The condition of 'more than two' is based on the idea that the plural in Arabic grammar (jam') applies to three or more.
3
Contrast with the majority Hanafi view
The majority Hanafi usul scholars (such as the author of al-Hidayah) hold that 'amm requires istighraq, i.e., inclusion of all individuals of the category. Al-Jassas's view reduces the requirement, allowing the general term to be used in its real sense even after excluding some individuals, as long as the remainder is a non-limited set.
This difference affects rulings when a general command in the Quran or Sunnah is specified by an exception: does the remaining command still apply generally?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشيخ أبو بكر الجصاص الرازي الاستغراق في العام غير شرط، فيكفي انتظام الجمع فيكون حقيقة فيما بقي أكثر من اثنين كذا نقل الحنفية، والشافعية نقلوا عنه أن العام ما يشمل غير المحصورين فإذا بقي غير محصور يكون حقيقة، وعلم من هذا
And Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi said: Encompassment (istighraq) in the general term is not a condition; rather the arrangement of the plural suffices. So it is a reality (haqiqah) in what remains more than two. This is how the Hanafis transmitted from him. And the Shafi'is transmitted from him that the general term is that which includes non-limited (ghayr mahsur) individuals. So if non-limited remains, it is a reality. And from this it is known.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents a definition of the general term ('amm) attributed to the early Hanafi usul scholar Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi. According to him, 'amm does not require full universality (istighraq) over all individuals; it suffices that it includes a plurality (jama') of individuals that are not limited (ghayr mahsurin). He specifies that 'amm is a reality (haqiqah) in what remains more than two (i.e., after specification, the term remains general if at least three individuals are left). The Hanafis transmitted this from him, while the Shafi'is transmitted a slightly different version: 'amm includes non-limited individuals, so if non-limited remains, it is used in its real sense. This view is important in the context of specification (takhsis): when a part of the general term is excluded, the remaining part must still be general enough (i.e., non-limited) for the term to be considered true. The phrase 'ghayr mahsur' (non-limited) refers to individuals that are not countable or not enumerated. This definition affects how we understand the scope of general commands after specification by a mukhassis.

Key Terms
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Full universality, i.e., inclusion of all individuals of a category. Al-Jassas denies it as a necessary condition for 'amm.
غير المحصورين (ghayr al-mahsurin): Non-limited individuals that cannot be counted or enumerated. For al-Jassas, this is the essential characteristic of the subjects of 'amm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Jassas, what is the minimum number of individuals that must remain for a general term to be used in its real sense after specification?
A) Exactly two
B) More than two
C) All individuals
D) At least one

Key Takeaway
Al-Jassas defines 'amm as encompassing a non-limited plurality, not requiring full universality.

The Linguistic Nature of Specification by Attribute: Hanafi vs. Opposing View
Debate on whether specifying 'amm by a sifah is a linguistic or external specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that specification by an attribute is linguistic (lafzi) and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in whether the remaining individuals are literal or metaphorical.

Core Idea
Specification by an attribute is linguistic because description is restriction, and restriction is the opposite of absoluteness.

In this passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut, the author analyzes a key usul dispute: whether an attribute (sifah) that specifies a general term does so through the linguistic formulation itself or through an external factor. The Hanafi school holds it is linguistic; al-Qadi and others argue it is external. The consequence affects the status of the remaining individuals as literal (haqiqah) or metaphorical (majaz).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the passage to find the core disagreement about specification by an attribute. Note the positions of al-Qadi and the Hanafi commentator.
The dispute is whether the specification is lafzi (linguistic) or khariji (external).
2
Examine al-Qadi's position and evidence
Al-Qadi argues that the attribute may encompass all individuals of the general, so the description itself is not specification; specification comes from outside. Therefore, the noun specified is metaphorical.
His dalil: 'الصفة قد تشمل جميع أفراد العام فلا يكون التوصيف نفسه تخصيصاً'.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
The commentator refutes by stating that description is taqyid (restriction), which is the opposite of itlaq (absoluteness). Unrestricted comprehension is greater than restricted, so the restriction is a linguistic operation.
Principle: 'التوصيف تقييد وهو ضد الإطلاق'.
4
Refute counter-arguments
The commentator addresses the possibility that restriction is purely rational (i'tibari) and the analogy with condition (shart) where condition may be equal to result. He argues that rational restriction is rare and the condition analogy fails because a condition may equal the conditional, so it does not specify alone.
This step shows how the Hanafi position defends against objections.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to the Hanafi position, the noun specified by an attribute remains haqiqah in the remaining individuals; according to al-Qadi, it becomes majaz.
This affects the overall usul rule for interpreting legal texts.
