The third article on linguistic principles. A clear indication of the invalidity of the opinion of the Arab grammarians, for they are more skilled than the people of Arabic, so any opinion contradicting their consensus is absolutely false. Then he pointed to what was said in interpreting consensus by saying: 'Except that it may be said: The (noun defined by al-) became a customary (ʿurfī) reality in the sense of inclusivity, so contemplate.' And what the Arab grammarians said regarding considering the original lexical meaning is also far-fetched, because lexical meaning is only known through usage and immediate understanding (tabādur). If one allows interpreting immediate understanding as being a customary meaning and opens this door, the door of knowledge of lexical meaning is closed. The author said in the footnote: I say: It can be said that the customary practice in placing a general lexical meaning for a specific referent, although the referent is particular individuals in reality for the universal concept which is made an instrument for the placement, it is permissible that they be universals under which are particulars. And if you know that, then it is permissible for the definite article (lām al-taʿrīf) to be of this category, for while it indicates the knownness of the essence, it branches into its known divisions. Then those divisions are lexical meanings for it. And on this, the generality of its noun is like the generality of the noun of 'every' (kull) and the indefinite noun under negation. And although this is artificial, it is more in accordance with the school of the Arab grammarians and the scholars of uṣūl. End. And perhaps the aspect of artificiality is that then the lām is no longer placed only for the definition of its noun, but rather has alongside inclusivity of individuals or specific reference, and this contradicts the apparent statements of the Arab grammarians. Then on this, mental reference (al-ʿahd al-dhihnī) and indication of the genus are among the referents, and this is shunned by the rules of uṣūl. Also, examination shows that applying it to both (mental reference and genus) when specific reference and inclusivity are not established, they are understood by context. Hence it is not reported that anyone argued against the deducers by suggesting one of these possibilities. So the truth is that the noun in the indefinite state is for the genus or for the individual without specification, and in the definite state, if there is no specific reference, it is for all individuals by inclusivity. Thus the transmission (tawātur) of the salaf's deduction with it. The reformer of the statements of the Arab grammarians deserves to be said about him: 'The perfumer cannot fix what time has ruined.' This, and true knowledge is with the Knower of the Unseen. (And) al-Qāḍī said similarly, i.e., similar to what Abū al-Ḥusayn said, except that the adjective (ṣifah) according to him — it appears that it (the adjective) is an independent specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ mustaqill), so he did not make the specified noun as a reality (ḥaqīqah) in the remaining individuals. And since the apparent principle regarding it is lack of independence, he pointed to its justification and said: Its verification is that its specification is not verbal (lafẓiyyah) but rather external, and the noun from which specification is made (al-mukhakhkhaṣ minhu) is absolutely metaphorical. And its not being verbal is evidenced by the fact that the adjective may encompass all individuals of the general ('āmm), so the description itself is not specification; rather the specification in it is external. (Thus in Sharḥ al-Mukhtaṣar). I say: The matter is not as he thought; rather, specification by it is verbal, because description is restriction (taqyīd) which is the opposite of absoluteness (iṭlāq), and it is evident that unrestricted comprehension (tənāwul) is more than restricted comprehension. If you say: It is possible that the restriction is only by reason, in which case the adjective would be equal to the described. He said: Restriction is rarely purely rational (iʿtibārī), so it should not be analogized to it. Moreover, it is refuted by the condition (sharṭ), for it may be equal to the conditional result, so it itself is not a specifier; thus the specification in it is also external, so it is not verbal. (So contemplate.) And Shaykh Abū Bakr al-Jaṣṣāṣ al-Rāzī said: Inclusivity (istighrāq) in the general ('āmm) is not a condition; rather it suffices that it comprises a plural, so it is a reality in what remains of more than two. This is reported by the Ḥanafīs. And the Shāfiʿīs reported from him that the 'āmm is what includes uncountable (ghayr maḥṣūr) individuals; if what remains is uncountable, it is a reality. And it is known from this statement that this Imam's statement is only regarding the word 'āmm (laẓ al-'āmm), so it has no relevance to this topic; for the discussion here is about the forms of generality (ṣiyagh al-ʿumūm), not the word 'āmm. So citing his statement in this issue is inappropriate — contemplate.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of 'amm (general) and its specification. It begins by emphasizing the superiority of Arab grammarians (ahl al-'arabiyyah) and the necessity of following their consensus. It then addresses the interpretation of the definite article (al-) and its use for inclusivity. The author critiques the view that 'amm can be interpreted purely by customary usage, arguing that lexical knowledge depends on immediate understanding (tabādur). He then discusses the view of al-Qāḍī that specification by an adjective (ṣifah) is external, not linguistic. The commentator refutes this, arguing that description is a form of restriction (taqyīd), which is linguistic. The passage concludes with the view of al-Jaṣṣāṣ that inclusivity (istighrāq) is not necessary for 'amm; rather, it suffices that a plural is included. However, the commentator clarifies that this discussion is about the word 'āmm itself, not about the forms of generality (ṣiyagh al-ʿumūm), so citing al-Jaṣṣāṣ here is inappropriate.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): A term that encompasses multiple individuals, understood according to linguistic context.
صفة (ṣifah): An adjective or attribute that restricts the general term, potentially acting as a specifier.
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ): A specifier that removes some individuals from the scope of the general term.
استغراق (istighrāq): The inclusiveness of all individuals; here discussed as a condition for the general according to some.
توصيف (tawṣīf): The act of describing or attributing; the process of adding an adjective to a noun.
تقييد (taqyīd): Restriction; making a term specific by adding a qualifying condition or attribute.
إطلاق (iṭlāq): Absoluteness; the state of a term without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main argument that specification by an attribute (ṣifah) is linguistic?
A) Because the attribute is equal to the described noun.
B) Because description is a restriction (taqyīd), and restriction is the opposite of absoluteness.
C) Because the attribute encompasses all individuals of the general.
D) Because the general term without attribute is more comprehensive than with it.

Key Takeaway
Specification by an attribute is a linguistic restriction, making the remaining individuals literal (haqiqah) in the Hanafi school.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Hanafi Classification of Mukhassis: Only Munfasil is a Specifier
Defining mukhassis types and analyzing the linguistic reality of istithnā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the Hanafi position that only the disconnected specifier (munfasil) qualifies as a true mukhassis, and evaluate the linguistic dispute over the term istithnā'.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, a true specifier must be independent; connected exceptions are not specifiers.

This passage clarifies a foundational distinction in Hanafi uṣūl: not every element that restricts a general term counts as a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). While the Shāfiʿīs include both connected and disconnected specifiers, the Hanafis restrict the term to independent (munfasil) specifiers. The passage also delves into the linguistic nature of istithnā' (exception), particularly the disconnected type, and the disagreement over whether it is truly called an exception.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Muttaṣil and Munfaṣil
Identify the two categories of specifiers: connected (muttasil, dependent on the general statement) and disconnected (munfasil, independent). Note that the Hanafi school recognizes only munfasil as a true mukhassis.
The term 'mukhassis' is reserved for independent specifiers; connected ones are merely explanatory.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Dispute on Istithnā' Munfaṣil
Examine the four views on whether the word 'istithnā'' applies to disconnected exception: metaphorical, homonymous, univocal, or not at all. Understand the Hanafi preference for 'metaphorical' based on usage frequency.
The dispute is largely terminological except in oath cases.
3
Understand the Condition for Correctness
For an istithnā' munfaṣil to be correct, it must oppose the preceding statement where agreement was imagined. The purpose is to dispel false inference.
Example: 'The people came except a donkey' – donkey is not part of the genus 'people'.
4
Apply the Hanafi Position to Text Interpretation
Recognize that when a general text is followed by a connected exception, Hanafis do not treat it as a specification of the general. Instead, the general remains operative over the rest, and the exception is simply a limitation.
This has direct implications for how Quranic verses and prophetic traditions are interpreted.

Source Text

ثم المخصص متصل إن كان غير مستقل (ومنفصل) إن كان مستقلاً، هذا على مذهب الشافعية، وأما عندنا فالمخصص هو الثاني فقط والأول خمسة : الأول الاستثناء المتصل والمنقطع لا تخصيص فيه إذ لا إخراج فيه بوجه اعلم أنه اختلف في إطلاق لفظ الاستثناء عليه أي على الاستثناء المنقطع (فقيل) لفظ الاستثناء (مجاز) فيه حقيقة في المتصل (وقيل مشترك بين المتصل والمنقطع (وقيل هو متواطىء) موضوع لمعنى واحد مشترك فيهما (وهو ما دل على مخالفة للحكم السابق (بإلا وأخواتها سواء كان بحيث لولا إلا وأخواتها لدخل ما بعدها فيما قبل أولا وقيل لا يسمى المنقطع استثناء حقيقة ولا مجازاً، وهذا المذهب الأخير لا يعود إلى طائل فإن إطلاق لفظ الاستثناء على المنقطع أجل من أن يخفى على أحد أو المعنى أن هذا النزاع لا يعود إلى طائل فإنه يرجع إلى الاصطلاح، لكن الأخير فيه أن تظهر فائدة الخلاف فيمن حلف لا أستثني أو إن استثنيت فكذا فاستثني باستثناء منقطع (ثم لا خلاف) لأحد في (صحته) أي صحة الاستثناء المنقطع (لغة والشرط لصحته (المخالفة) للصدر بوجه ما فيما يتوهم فيه (الموافقة) فالفائدة فيه دفع هذا التوهم مثل لكن) فإنه للاستدراك أي دفع التوهم من السابق ( نحو : جاء القوم إلا حماراً فإنه يتوهم من مجيء القوم مجيء الحمار لأنه المركب، فدفع بالاستثناء المنقطع وما زاد إلا ما نقص فإنه يتوهم من نفي الزيادة وجود النقصان، فنفي النقص بإلا دفعاً لهذا الوهم، وهذا المثال يحتمل الاتصال أيضاً، لكن إذا قصد وجود النقصان على الكمال والمعنى ما زاد شيء إلا النقصان، وإذا كان من شرطه المخالفة فيما يتوهم الموافقة (فلا يقال : ما جاءني زيد إلا أن الجوهر الفرد للجزئيات بوضع عام فسر فقال : (أي وضعت لهما (لمعنى فيهما) أي لأجل تصور معنى واحد مشترك بينهما وجعل مرآة لهما (وضعاً واحداً عاماً لنا أن المتصل أظهر) منه في الاستعمال فلا يتبادر من نحو جاء القوم إلا أي قبل ذكر المستثنى (إلا إرادة إخراج البعض فلا يكون مشتركاً لفظياً بينهما، وإلا لاحتيج في معرفة الإرادة إلى القرينة (ولا) موضوعاً (للمشترك بينهما، وإلا لتبادر هو وفيه نوع مسامحة كما لا يخفى (ومن ثمة) أي من أجل تبادر إرادة الاتصال (لم يحمله علماء الأمصار عليه ما أمكن المتصل ولو) كان بتأويل فحملوا له علي ألف إلا كرا على قيمته لا على الانقطاع وإن خلا عن التأويل فتدبر.
Then the specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is either connected (muttasil) if it is not independent, or disconnected (munfasil) if it is independent. This is according to the Shāfiʿī school. As for us (Hanafis), the specifier is only the second (munfasil), and the first (muttasil) has five types: first, exception (istithnā') — both connected (muttasil) and disconnected (munfasil) — but there is no specification in it because there is no extraction in any way. Know that there is disagreement about applying the term istithnā' to the disconnected exception. Some say the word istithnā' is metaphorical (majāz) for it, being literal in connected; some say it is a homonym (mushtarak) between connected and disconnected; some say it is univocal (mutawāṭi') placed for a single meaning common to both, which is: what indicates opposition to the previous ruling by 'illā' and its sisters, whether without 'illā' and its sisters the following would have been included in the preceding or not. And some say that the disconnected is not called istithnā' at all, neither literally nor metaphorically. This last opinion is of no utility, because the application of the word istithnā' to disconnected is too clear to be hidden from anyone. Or the meaning is that this dispute is of no utility because it reduces to terminology. However, there is benefit in the last opinion in terms of the effect of the dispute on the case of one who swears 'I will not make an exception' or 'if I make an exception then such and such' and then makes an exception using a disconnected exception. Then there is no disagreement about its correctness linguistically, and the condition for its correctness is opposition (mukhālafah) to the preceding statement in some way where agreement (muwāfaqah) is imagined. So the benefit is in dispelling this imagined agreement. For example, 'the people came except a donkey' — because from the people's coming, the donkey's coming is imagined because it is a mount, so the disconnected exception repels that. And 'nothing increased except decrease' — from the negation of increase, the existence of decrease is imagined, so the negation of decrease by 'illā' repels this imagination. This example may also be interpreted as connected, but when the intention is the existence of decrease upon completeness, the meaning is 'nothing increased except decrease'. And since the condition is opposition where agreement is imagined, one should not say: 'Zayd did not come to me except that the atom is true' (which is irrelevant). [Section] The particle of exception (adāt al-istithnā') is literal in connected by agreement, and metaphorical in disconnected according to the chosen view. Some say it is literal in both. Then they disagreed: some say it is a homonym, some say it is univocal. Since univocality is not sensible in a particle (because particles are placed for particular meanings by a general placement), he explained: i.e., it was placed for both with a single meaning common to them, made a mirror for them by a single general placement. For us (Hanafis), the connected is more apparent (aẓhar) in usage, so from 'the people came except' before mentioning the excluded, one only immediately understands the intention of extracting some, so it is not a homonym (otherwise a context would be needed), nor is it placed for the common meaning between them (otherwise that common meaning would immediately come to mind, which involves some approximation as is clear). Hence, the scholars of the lands (ʿulamā' al-amṣār) do not apply it to the disconnected as long as the connected is possible, even if by interpretation. Thus they interpreted 'for him is one thousand except a load (kirā)' based on its value, not on disconnectedness, even if it lacks interpretation. So contemplate.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage classifies specifiers (mukhassis) into connected (muttasil) and disconnected (munfasil). According to the Shāfiʿī school, both are considered specifiers, but for Hanafis, only the disconnected is a true specifier. The connected specifier consists of five types, the first of which is exception (istithnā'). The passage then delves into the linguistic classification of istithnā' munfasil (disconnected exception), detailing four views: that it is metaphorical, homonymous, univocal, or not called exception at all. The preferred view is that it is metaphorical. The condition for its correctness is that it opposes the preceding statement in a context where agreement is imagined, thus dispelling that false impression. Examples illustrate this: 'the people came except a donkey' and 'nothing increased except decrease.' The particle (adāt) of exception is literal in muttasil and metaphorical in munfasil. The Hanafis argue that because muttasil is more apparent in usage, it is the primary meaning.

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhaṣṣiṣ): A specifier that removes some individuals from the scope of a general term; per Hanafis, only munfasil qualifies.
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception using 'illā' or its sisters, indicating opposition to the previous ruling; divided into muttasil (connected) and munfasil (disconnected).
متصل (muttasil): A connected specifier that is not independent; for Hanafis, it is not a true specifier.
منفصل (munfasil): A disconnected specifier that is independent; the only type considered a specifier by Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī view presented in this passage, which of the following is considered a true specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ)?
A) Both connected (muttasil) and disconnected (munfasil) specifiers.
B) Only the disconnected (munfasil) specifier.
C) Only the connected (muttasil) specifier.
D) Neither connected nor disconnected specifiers.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi uṣūl, only independent (munfasil) specifiers are true mukhassis; connected exceptions do not constitute takhsīṣ.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Types of Mukhassis and the Ruling on Disconnected Exception
Defining muttasil and munfasil mukhassis, and discussing istithna' munqati'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muttasil and munfasil mukhassis and apply the Hanafi position on the linguistic validity of disconnected exception.

Core Idea
Disconnected exception is linguistically valid, but its purpose is to correct misunderstanding, not to extract.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi view on specifiers of general statements. It distinguishes between connected (muttasil) and disconnected (munfasil) mukhassis, then delves into the terminological dispute over whether 'istithna'' applies to disconnected exception. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct usul analysis of general texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of mukhassis
According to the Shafi'is, both muttasil (non-independent) and munfasil (independent) are specifiers. The Hanafi school only considers munfasil a true mukhassis.
Muttasil includes exception, condition, attribute, and purpose.
2
Understand the Hanafi position on muttasil
For Hanafis, muttasil elements do not actually extract from the general meaning; they clarify the intended scope. They are not mukhassis but bayan (clarification).
3
Analyze the dispute over the term 'istithna'' for disconnected exception
The passage presents four views: metaphor, shared term, univocal, and outright denial. It dismisses the last as without practical benefit except in oaths.
The Hanafi emphasis is on linguistic validity, not terminology.
4
Recognize the condition for validity of disconnected exception
The disconnected exception must contradict the initial statement in a context where agreement is assumed, to prevent misunderstanding.
Example: 'The people came except a donkey' corrects the assumption that the donkey came.
5
Apply the concept with examples
Evaluate given examples to determine if disconnected exception is valid and why.
Examples from the passage illustrate correct and incorrect uses.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام أن قول هذا الإمام ليس إلا في لفظ العام، فهو ليس من هذا المقام في شيء، فإن الكلام ههنا في صيغ العموم لا في لفظ العام فإيراد قوله هذا في المسألة غير مناسب فتأمل . (ثم المخصص متصل إن كان غير مستقل (ومنفصل) إن كان مستقلاً، هذا على مذهب الشافعية، وأما عندنا فالمخصص هو الثاني فقط والأول خمسة : الأول الاستثناء المتصل والمنقطع لا تخصيص فيه إذ لا إخراج فيه بوجه اعلم أنه اختلف في إطلاق لفظ الاستثناء عليه أي على الاستثناء المنقطع (فقيل) لفظ الاستثناء (مجاز) فيه حقيقة في المتصل (وقيل مشترك بين المتصل والمنقطع (وقيل هو متواطىء) موضوع لمعنى واحد مشترك فيهما (وهو ما دل على مخالفة للحكم السابق (بإلا وأخواتها سواء كان بحيث لولا إلا وأخواتها لدخل ما بعدها فيما قبل أولا وقيل لا يسمى المنقطع استثناء حقيقة ولا مجازاً، وهذا المذهب الأخير لا يعود إلى طائل فإن إطلاق لفظ الاستثناء على المنقطع أجل من أن يخفى على أحد أو المعنى أن هذا النزاع لا يعود إلى طائل فإنه يرجع إلى الاصطلاح، لكن الأخير فيه أن تظهر فائدة الخلاف فيمن حلف لا أستثني أو إن استثنيت فكذا فاستثني باستثناء منقطع (ثم لا خلاف) لأحد في (صحته) أي صحة الاستثناء المنقطع (لغة والشرط لصحته (المخالفة) للصدر بوجه ما فيما يتوهم فيه (الموافقة) فالفائدة فيه دفع هذا التوهم مثل لكن) فإنه للاستدراك أي دفع التوهم من السابق ( نحو : جاء القوم إلا حماراً فإنه يتوهم من مجيء القوم مجيء الحمار لأنه المركب، فدفع بالاستثناء المنقطع وما زاد إلا ما نقص فإنه يتوهم من نفي الزيادة وجود النقصان، فنفي النقص بإلا دفعاً لهذا الوهم، وهذا المثال يحتمل الاتصال أيضاً، لكن إذا قصد وجود النقصان على الكمال والمعنى ما زاد شيء إلا النقصان، وإذا كان من شرطه المخالفة فيما يتوهم الموافقة (فلا يقال : ما جاءني زيد إلا أن الجوهر الفرد حق).
The discussion is that the statement of this Imam is only about the word 'amm, so it is not of this topic at all. For the discussion here is about the forms of 'umum, not about the word 'amm. So mentioning his statement in this issue is inappropriate. Ponder. Then the mukhassis is [muttasil] if it is non-independent, and [munfasil] if it is independent. This is according to the Shafi'i school. But according to us (Hanafis), the mukhassis is only the second (munfasil), and the first (muttasil) are five: The first is the connected exception and the disconnected exception; there is no specification in it because there is no extraction in it in any way. Know that there is disagreement in applying the word 'istithna'' to it, i.e., to the disconnected exception. It was said that the word 'istithna'' is a metaphor in it, while it is literal in the connected. And it was said that it is shared (lafzi) between connected and disconnected. And it was said that it is univocal (mutawati'), placed for a single meaning common to both, which is: what indicates a departure from the previous ruling with 'illa' and its sisters, whether if not for 'illa' and its sisters, what comes after would enter into what comes before or not. And it was said that the disconnected is not called 'istithna'' literally nor metaphorically. This last opinion does not lead to any tangible result, because the application of the word 'istithna'' to the disconnected is too evident to be hidden from anyone; or the meaning is that this dispute does not lead to any tangible result because it goes back to terminology. However, the last [opinion] has the benefit of the consequence of the dispute in the case of one who swears 'I will not make an exception' or 'if I make an exception, then such-and-such' and then he makes an exception with a disconnected exception. Then there is no disagreement among anyone about its validity, i.e., the validity of the disconnected exception literally. And the condition for its validity is that it contradicts the initial statement in some way in a context where agreement is assumed [to avoid misunderstanding]. So the benefit of it is to remove this misunderstanding, like the word 'lakinna', for it is used for correction, i.e., to remove misunderstanding from the preceding statement. For example: 'The people came, except a donkey' - because from the coming of the people, the coming of the donkey is assumed because it is a riding animal, so it was removed by the disconnected exception. And 'Nothing increased except that it decreased' - because from the negation of increase, the existence of decrease is assumed, so negation of decrease with 'illa' removes this misunderstanding. This example also admits the possibility of connection, but if one intends the existence of decrease upon completeness, then the meaning is 'Nothing increased except decrease.' And if the condition of it is that it contradicts in a context where agreement is assumed, then one does not say: 'Zaid did not come to me except that the indivisible atom is true.'
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of mukhassis (specifiers of general statements) according to the Hanafi school. It begins by distinguishing between muttasil (connected) and munfasil (disconnected) mukhassis. According to the Shafi'is, both types are considered mukhassis: muttasil is that which is not independent, like exception, condition, etc., while munfasil is independent, such as a separate textual proof. However, the Hanafi school holds that only the munfasil is a true mukhassis; the muttasil are not considered mukhassis because they are part of the statement itself and do not truly extract from the general meaning. The first type of muttasil is istithna' (exception), which includes both muttasil (connected exception) and munqati' (disconnected exception). The author clarifies that there is no takhsis in exception because it does not actually extract anything; rather, it clarifies the intended scope. The passage then delves into a terminological dispute about whether the term 'istithna'' applies to the disconnected exception (istithna' munqati') or only to the connected one. Some say it is a metaphor for the disconnected, others say it is shared, others say it is univocal, and one view denies the application altogether. The author dismisses the last view as without practical benefit, except in cases of oaths. Importantly, the passage affirms the linguistic validity of disconnected exception and states its condition: it must contradict the initial statement in a context where the listener might otherwise assume agreement. The examples illustrate that the purpose is to prevent misunderstanding, such as in 'The people came except a donkey' to avoid assuming the donkey also came because it is a riding animal, or 'Nothing increased except decrease' to avoid assuming that no decrease occurred. Finally, it gives an example where disconnected exception is invalid: 'Zaid did not come to me except that the indivisible atom is true' because there is no preceding assumption to correct.

Key Terms
المخصص المتصل (al-mukhassis al-muttasil): ما لا يكون مستقلاً بنفسه كالاستثناء والشرط والصفة والغاية
المخصص المنفصل (al-mukhassis al-munfasil): ما يكون مستقلاً بنفسه كدليل منفصل يخصص العام
الاستثناء المنقطع (al-istithna' al-munqati'): ما يكون المستثنى غير داخل في المستثنى منه لولا أداة الاستثناء

B) أن يكون مخالفاً للصدر فيما يتوهم فيه الموافقة
C) أن يكون متصلاً بالكلام
D) أن يكون متفقاً مع الصدر

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats only independent (munfasil) specifiers as true mukhassis, and disconnected exceptions are valid only when they correct an assumed understanding.

The Linguistic Status of the Exception Tool (Illa)
Determining the literal/metaphorical status of the exception tool

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether 'illa' is used literally or metaphorically in connected and disconnected exceptions, and apply the Hanafi criterion of tabaddur (immediate understanding) to determine the linguistic status of a particle.

Core Idea
'Illa' is literal in connected exception but metaphorical in disconnected, based on immediate understanding (tabaddur).

What does the word 'except' actually mean? Is it always used literally, or can it be metaphorical? This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut explores the linguistic status of the Arabic exception particle 'illa'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts that contain exceptions, especially when the excepted item is not part of the general category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The linguistic status of 'illa' in connected vs. disconnected exception: is it literal, metaphorical, homonym, or univocal?
The connected exception excludes a member of the same category; the disconnected excludes something not of that category.
2
Understand the agreed position
All scholars agree that 'illa' is literal (haqiqah) in connected exception. The dispute is over disconnected exception.
Connected example: 'The students came except Zayd' (Zayd is a student).
3
Explore the dispute
There are four views: literal in both, metaphorical in disconnected (Hanafi chosen), verbal homonym, and univocal. The passage argues against homonymy and univocality.
Verbal homonym would require context to determine meaning; univocality would require a single common meaning that immediately comes to mind.
4
Examine the Hanafi evidence
When 'illa' is uttered, the immediate understanding (tabaddur) is extraction of some from the whole (connected). No context is needed. This shows the primary literal meaning is connected, so disconnected is metaphorical.
Tabaddur is the Hanafi criterion for literalness: if a meaning springs to mind without context, it is haqiqah.
5
Apply to a concrete example
In the phrase 'Alif except Kaf' (a thousand except a load), early scholars interpreted 'Alif' as 'value' to make the exception connected, avoiding taking 'illa' as metaphorical. This preserves the literal sense of 'illa'.
This shows the practical impact: they prefer a metaphorical reading of the general term over a metaphorical reading of 'illa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (أداة الاستثناء) حقيقة في المتصل اتفاقاً و(مجاز في المنقطع) في المختار (وقيل حقيقة) فيهما ثم اختلفوا فقيل مشترك) لفظي فيهما (وقيل متواطىء ولما لم يكن التواطؤ معقولا في الأداة فإنها موضوعة للجزئيات بوضع عام فسر فقال : (أي وضعت لهما (لمعنى فيهما) أي لأجل تصور معنى واحد مشترك بينهما وجعل مرآة لهما (وضعاً واحداً عاماً لنا أن المتصل أظهر) منه في الاستعمال فلا يتبادر من نحو جاء القوم إلا أي قبل ذكر المستثنى (إلا إرادة إخراج البعض فلا يكون مشتركاً لفظياً بينهما، وإلا لاحتيج في معرفة الإرادة إلى القرينة (ولا) موضوعاً (للمشترك بينهما، وإلا لتبادر هو وفيه نوع مسامحة كما لا يخفى (ومن ثمة) أي من أجل تبادر إرادة الاتصال (لم يحمله علماء الأمصار عليه ما أمكن المتصل ولو) كان بتأويل فحملوا له علي ألف إلا كرا على قيمته لا على الانقطاع وإن خلا عن التأويل فتدبر.
Issue: The tool of exception is literal in the connected [exception] by agreement, and metaphorical in the disconnected [exception] according to the chosen view. And it was said it is literal in both. Then they differed: it was said it is a verbal homonym for both, and it was said it is univocal. And since univocality is not reasonable for a particle, for they are placed for particulars with a general placement, he [the author] explained by saying: 'i.e., it is placed for them with a single general placement for a meaning in them, i.e., for the purpose of conceiving a single meaning common to both and making it a mirror for them.' Our proof is that the connected is more apparent in usage; so from the expression 'the people came except' — before mentioning the excepted — nothing is understood except the intention to extract some, so it is not a verbal homonym between them, because if it were, a contextual clue would be needed to know the intention. And it is not placed for the common meaning between them, because if it were, that common meaning would be understood immediately, and there is some leniency in that, as is not hidden. And hence, i.e., due to the immediate understanding of the intention of connection, the scholars of the regions did not take it upon the disconnected when the connected is possible, even if [the disconnected] requires interpretation. So they interpreted for him [the example] 'Alif except Kaf' as [the total] upon his value, not upon disconnection, even if it is free from interpretation; so ponder.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of the word 'illa' (the tool of exception). It begins by stating the agreed-upon position that 'illa' is literal (haqiqah) when used in connected exception (istithna' muttasil), where the excepted item is part of the general category. The chosen view is that it is metaphorical (majaz) when used in disconnected exception (istithna' munqati'), where the excepted item is not part of the general category. There are alternative views: some say it is literal in both, some say it is a verbal homonym (mushtarak lafzi) between the two, and some say it is univocal (mutawati'). The author argues that univocality is not reasonable for a tool word because particles are placed for particular instances under a general placement; a single common meaning is not apparent. The preferred evidence is that when 'illa' is used, the immediate understanding is connected exception (i.e., extracting some from the whole). If 'illa' were a homonym, context would be needed to determine the intended meaning. Since no context is needed, it is not a homonym. Therefore, 'illa' is literal in connected exception, and its use in disconnected exception is metaphorical. This is why, in the example 'Alif except Kaf' (meaning a thousand except a load), scholars interpreted 'Alif' as referring to the value (the remaining amount) rather than taking it as a disconnected exception, because the connected interpretation is possible even if it requires metaphorical interpretation of the word 'Alif' itself.

Key Terms
أداة الاستثناء (adat al-istithna'): لفظ 'إلا' وأخواتها (غير، سوى، حاشا...)
التبادر (al-tabaddur): أن يفهم من اللفظ معنى معيناً دون قرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) حقيقة
B) مجاز
C) مشترك لفظي
D) متواطئ

Key Takeaway
The exception particle 'illa' is literal in connected exception and metaphorical in disconnected, as determined by immediate understanding (tabaddur).

How Does Exception Affect Number Nouns? Hanafi vs. Shafi'i Views
Dispute over whether the number is used metaphorically or the exception is by mu'arada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on interpreting exception in number nouns (e.g., 'ten except three') and apply the correct usul principle to derive the meaning of such phrases in Quranic verses like 29:14.

Core Idea
Number nouns are precise and cannot be used metaphorically; the exception specifies the intended meaning without altering the literal number.

The phrase 'ten except three' appears contradictory—'ten' affirms ten, but 'except three' removes three. How do usul scholars resolve this? The Shafi'i majority holds that 'ten' is used metaphorically for 'seven', while the Hanafi school insists that number nouns remain literal and the exception works by opposition (mu'arada) or by specification. This lesson unpacks the debate and its practical implications for interpreting divine texts, using the Quranic verse about Noah's stay (29:14) as a case study.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi view from the passage: number nouns like 'ten' are precise and cannot be used metaphorically. Instead, the exception (istithna') provides an opposing ruling that cancels the initial generalization in part, leaving the remainder specified.
This is the mu'arada (opposition) interpretation, though the passage later criticizes it.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i majority argues that 'ten' is used metaphorically to mean 'seven', and the exception serves as a contextual clue (qarina sarifa) that turns the word from its literal meaning to the metaphorical remainder.
This avoids the apparent contradiction without positing a logical opposition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique of mu'arada
The author criticizes the mu'arada interpretation because it leads to a logical contradiction: the initial statement affirms ten while the exception denies three, and cancelling them would leave no clear meaning. Also, it conflicts with explicit Shafi'i texts.
Sadr al-Shari'ah refined the view: the initial part is metaphorical for the remainder, and the exception is a qarina, but this fails for number nouns.
4
Examine the special case of number nouns
Number nouns are precise terms (like 'ten') that cannot be applied to a different number (like 'seven') without being metaphorical. The Hanafi position is that they remain literal, and the exception simply indicates that the initial statement was meant to be limited—without changing the number's meaning.
This is contrasted with other specifiers where the initial word can be metaphorical.
5
Apply to a Quranic example
Apply the Hanafi analysis to 'a thousand years except fifty years' (29:14). The number 'a thousand' remains literal; the exception shows that only 950 are intended in the specific context, without the word 'thousand' becoming metaphorical.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether the word 'alf' is used metaphorically for 950 or remains literal but specifically intended for 950.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية مسألة (قد اختلف في نحو علي عشرة إلا ثلاثة دفعاً للتناقض المتوهم بين ثبوت العشرة وبين إخراج الثلاثة عنها (فالجمهور) من الشافعية قالوا المراد بعشرة إنما هو السبعة) مجازاً (وإلاّ ثلاثة قرينة عليه صارفة عن حقيقتها إلى مجازها اعلم أن مشايخنا حكوا عن الشافعي رضي الله عنه أن الاستثناء يدفع عن بعض المستثنى منه بطريق المعارضة، وفسره بعضهم بأن الحكم في المستثنى منه على أو موجودة (إن الإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان بل) الذي (ليس بإنسان من الأفراد الفرضية للإنسان، وقيدوا في الأفراد بالإمكان لخروجه فهو مخالف للعرف واللغة) وكلامنا فيما يرضيان به والمنع مكابرة بل مخالف للعقل أيضاً كما قال بعض المحققين إن الفرد للكلي حقيقة ما يصدق هو عليه في نفس الأمر بالفعل أو بالإمكان، وليس الإنسان الذي ليس بحيوان مما يصدق عليه الحيوان أصلاً، قد يكون فرداً له حقيقة (ولو سلم الاتصاف) أي The third chapter on linguistic principles. Issue: There is a dispute regarding [the phrase] 'ten except three', to remove the assumed contradiction between affirming ten and extracting three from it. The majority of Shafi'is said that the intended meaning of 'ten' is only 'seven', metaphorically, and 'except three' is a contextual clue turning it from its literal meaning to its metaphorical meaning. Know that our scholars (Hanafis) reported from al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, that exception removes from some of the excepted-from by way of opposition (mu'arada). And some of them interpreted it as: the ruling in the excepted-from applies to the whole, then the exception gives an opposing ruling in some part; when they oppose each other, they cancel out, and the ruling of the excepted-from remains in the rest. But this is nothing, because besides being invalid in itself and causing contradiction in the statement, it necessitates that there be no ruling in the excepted opposite to the ruling of the initial part, which contradicts the statements of the Shafi'is. And Sadr al-Shari'ah said: its summary is that the intended meaning of the initial part is the remainder, metaphorically, and the exception is a contextual clue. Perhaps he alluded to this by saying 'like other specifiers'. The verification is that the exception gives a ruling opposing the apparent ruling of the initial part. For this reason, the intellect rules that the intended [meaning] in the initial part is other than it, like independent specifiers. So the exception by its ruling is a contextual clue turning to specification. Then he, may Allah have mercy on him, invalidated that this does not apply to number nouns, for 'ten' for example is placed for a specific number, absolutely not intended to be applied to what is below it or above it in numerical ranks. So it is not permissible to intend by it the remaining [number]. Even if conceded, it would be metaphorical, which is contrary to the principle. And 'what for it and against it' will come, if Allah wills. If you say: The noble scholars have invalidated the statement of opposition because it necessitates contradiction in His saying: 'So he stayed among them a thousand years except fifty years' [29:14]. And this is only correct if interpreted by the first explanation, not the second, as is not hidden. I say: Its formulation on the second [explanation] is to say: number noun does not admit being applied to the lesser, so 'a thousand' cannot be taken to mean 950, so the ruling remains on the first with the affirmation of its opposite in some part. So ponder in it, for the apparent words of the scholars are averse to it. I say: And it is correct, because the inclusion of the excepted-from word for the excepted remains after the exception just as it was before. For 'ten' is a single concept that neither increases nor decreases. So from its very essence, it cannot be described as extracting three from it. If 'ten' were remaining in its literal meaning, there would be no exception and extraction. So it is used for 'seven' by the contextual clue of the exception. If you say: We do not accept that 'ten' is a reality that neither increases nor decreases. Rather, 'seven' is also an individual of 'ten'. Do you not see that the logicians said: 'Man who is not an animal' is an individual of 'man', and they needed to exclude it by a condition of possibility. If it were not an individual of 'man', they would not need to restrict it. He said: And what they said on the verification of the 'real' proposition that rules over individuals absolutely, whether conceptual or existent, that 'man who is not an animal, but rather who is not a man' is from the conceptual individuals of 'man'. And they restricted it by possibility for its exclusion. So it contradicts custom and language. And our speech is about what both are satisfied with; and refusal is obstinacy, rather it contradicts intellect as well, as some investigators said: 'An individual of the universal is, in reality, what it applies to in actual existence or in possibility. And man who is not an animal is not something to which 'animal' applies at all. He may be an individual of it in reality.' And even if we accept the description, i.e., describing 'ten' with increase and decrease, it does not prevent the inclusion of 'three' also, so it would necessitate that it is extracted from it.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a dispute concerning the interpretation of phrases like 'ten except three'. The issue is how to avoid an apparent contradiction: the word 'ten' seems to affirm ten, but the exception 'except three' removes three, leaving seven. According to the Shafi'i majority, the word 'ten' is used metaphorically to mean 'seven', and the exception serves as a contextual clue (qarina) that turns it from its literal meaning to this metaphorical meaning. The Hanafi scholars (including Sadr al-Shari'ah) reject this and propose instead that the exception operates by mu'arada (opposition): the apparent ruling of the initial part (ten) is affirmed for the whole, then the exception presents an opposing ruling for some part, and they cancel out, leaving the ruling for the rest. However, the author criticizes this view as invalid, as it leads to logical contradiction. He then sides with the correction by Sadr al-Shari'ah, who refined the Shafi'i view: the initial statement's intended meaning is the remainder, metaphorically, and the exception is the contextual clue. But Sadr al-Shari'ah also points out a problem with applying this to number nouns: number nouns like 'ten' are precise terms that cannot be used metaphorically for a different number without being a new creation. The author then engages with a potential objection from logicians about 'individuals' of a universal, ultimately upholding the precision of number words. The correct view according to the author is that the word 'ten' remains literal in its meaning, and the exception is a separate ruling that contradicts the apparent general ruling of the initial part; thus, the intellect understands that the initial part is meant to be specified, similar to other independent specifiers. The passage includes a reference to the Quranic verse about Noah's stay (29:14) as an example where this analysis applies.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): أن الاستثناء يفيد حكماً معارضاً لظاهر حكم الصدر، فيتساقطان ويثبت حكم الصدر في الباقي
القرينة الصارفة (al-qarīna al-ṣārifa): دليل يصرف اللفظ من حقيقته إلى مجازه
اسم العدد (ism al-ʿadad): لفظ يدل على كمية معينة كعشرة، مائة، ألف

Checkpoint
According to the final preferred position in the source text, when someone says 'ten except three', how should 'ten' be understood?
A) 'Ten' is metaphorical for seven.
B) 'Ten' remains literal for ten, and the exception cancels the initial statement.
C) 'Ten' remains literal for ten, but the exception specifies that the intended meaning is seven.
D) 'Ten' is literal for seven because the exception redefines it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that number nouns are semantically precise and cannot be used metaphorically; exception specifies the intended referent while preserving literal meaning.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

15 min
The Three Madhhabs on Exception Clauses: 'Ten Except Three'
Debating the linguistic status of exception clauses in Hanafi usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi madhhabs on the meaning of exception clauses and explain why the third madhhab yields a definitive (qat'i) indication for the remainder.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'ten except three' is a compound word that directly designates seven—no metaphor needed.

This lesson dives into a classical Hanafi debate: how does the phrase 'ten except three' convey the number seven? While some argued for metaphor or implicit rulings, the preferred Hanafi view holds that the composite expression itself is a distinct linguistic unit, placed by type-based assignment (al-wad' al-naw'i) to definitively indicate the remainder. Understanding this clarifies why Hanafis treat exception clauses as providing certain (qat'i) rulings, unlike specification (takhsis) which yields probability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Madhhabs
List the three positions regarding the meaning of the excepted-from (mustathna minhu) in phrases like 'ten except three': (1) metaphor for the remainder, (2) ruling on some individuals, (3) real meaning with exception, composite as a compound word for the remainder.
The third madhhab is preferred by the author and most Hanafi verifiers.
2
Analyze the Objections to Al-Qadi's View
Review the four objections raised against the view that the composite is a single word placed for seven: infinite names, pronoun reference, implication of title, composition rules. Assess each objection's strength.
The author shows the objections are not fatal due to type-based assignment (al-wad' al-naw'i).
3
Understand Type-Based Assignment
Grasp the concept of al-wad' al-naw'i: a pattern or rule assigning meanings to an entire category of compound expressions, allowing infinite compounds for infinite meanings.
This is key to the third madhhab: the composite is placed for the remainder by a general rule.
4
Derive the Usul Consequence
From the third madhhab, conclude that the indication of the composite for the remainder is by original assignment (wad'), which is definitive (qat'i). Contrast with takhsis which is probabilistic (zanni).
The author emphasizes this is not dependent on the ruling of the excepted part.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Understand why the author prefers the third madhhab: it preserves the literal meaning of individual words, avoids metaphor, and provides a sound linguistic basis for definitive rulings.
The first madhhab is definitively false; the second is true only if interpreted as the third.

أن لا تكون من اللفظ نفسه، بل إنما تكون من خارج فلو كان العدد نصاً كان نصوصية بخارج وها هنا الخارج وهو الاستثناء قائم دال على أنه أريد به معنى آخر فيكون نصاً في الباقي بعد الاستثناء، ولا يبعد أن يقال : أي تركيب الاسم (من) كلمات (ثلاثة أقول بل التركيب (من) كلمات أربعة في نحو ثلاثون إلا أحد عشر وهو) أي تركيب اسم من ثلاثة حال كونه (في غير المحكي نحو تأبط شراً، فإنه يجوز (و) الحال (الأول): غير مضاف نحو : أبي عبد الله فإنه جائز، اتفاقاً ولا معرب) ظاهره يفيد أن امتناع التركيب من ثلاثة إنما هو إذا لم يكن الأول معرباً، وهنا كذلك، فينبغي أن لا يمتنع، وهو خلاف المدعي والأظهر في العبارة الأول معرب غير مضاف والأولى أن يجعل من التعريب فيكون إشارة إلى جواز ذلك في الأسماء المنقولة الأعجمية ولا حرف خلاف اللغة بالاستقراء ثم لما كان هذه الإيرادات إنما ترد إذا أراد القاضي أنها كلمات ركبت وجعلت كلمة واحدة وكان (قول القاضي المنسوب إليه عند هذه الإرادة مع قطع النظر عن لزوم تلك الاستحالات اسماً . خلاف البديهة للقطع بأن المفردات باقية على أوضاعها . اعلم أن المصنف قد سلم البديهة ببقاء المفردات على أوضاعها وحينئذ قد بطل القول بأن العشرة مستعملة في سبعة فتدبر (أوّل) قوله ( بأن مراده أن المجموع حقيقة في السبعة وأنه موضوع بالوضع النوعي الذي للمركبات بإزاء السبعة (بمعنى أن المفردات مستعملة في معانيها الموضوعة لها هي ومحصل المجموع معنى يصدق على السبعة وهو عشرة نقص عنها ثلاثة وليس هو إلا السبعة (لا يتبادر إلى الفهم غيرها) قال المصنف موافقاً لما في «التحرير» وغيره: (وهذا يرجع إلى أحد المذهبين لكن الرجوع إلى المذهب الأول غير صحيح، فإن المذهب الأول محصله أن المستثنى منه مجاز عن الباقي، وفي هذا المستثنى منه مستعمل فيما وضع له، فأين هذا من ذلك، وأما المذهب الثاني فقد عرفت أنه يحتمل احتمالين أحدهما أن الحكم على بعض المستثنى منه والمذكور الكل بقرينة الاستثناء، وعلى هذا لا رجوع إليه، فإن محصله أن الحكم على أفراد يصدق عليه هذا المجموع المدلول لهذا المركب وبناء على هذا ثلث صدر الشريعة وقال المذاهب ثلاثة واختار هو الأخير الذي ذهب إليه القاضي وحينئذ لا يتوجه ما في التلويح أن الدلالة على الباقي بالوضع النوعي للمركب مسلم عند الجميع، لكن الكلام في كيفية الدلالة فهذا المذهب ليس قسيماً للمذاهب الباقية، والعجب منه كيف خفي عليه أنه إذا كان المراد بالمستثنى منه الباقي مجازاً بقرينة الاستثناء، فأين الوضع النوعي للمركب. وإنما الدلالة للمستثنى منه فقط لا للمركب وإن حمل المذهب الثاني على هذا الاحتمال فالرجوع فقد ظهر لك مما تلونا عليك مراراً أن المذهب الأول باطل قطعاً والمذهب الثالث هو صحيح الحق، ومحصله أن المستثنى منه على حقيقته وأخرج عنه المستثنى والدال عليه الأداة، فحصل هذا المركب مفهوم مركب يعبر به عن الباقي، واللفظ المركب موضوع بإزاء هذا المفهوم المركب بالوضع النوعي كما مر مراراً، وأن المذهب الثاني إن حمل عليه فهو حق وإلا فهو باطل مشتمل على اللغو، وقد ظهر لك أيضاً أن هذا التركيب يدل على الباقي بالوضع، وقد تقدم أن المدلول الوضعي يكون مقطوعاً، وأن هذه الدلالة غير متوقفة على حكم المستثنى كما في التخصيص فلا تعليل حكم المستثنى المعارض لهذا الحكم على الباقي كما قدمنا يصح فتدبر، ونقول أيضاً: إن في ذكر العشرة ثم تقييده بما يفيد إخراج البعض، ثم الحكم على ما يصدق عليه هذا المركب إشارة إلى أن حكم المخرج مخالف لهذا الحكم أي الحكم المخالف يستفاد ضمناً لا أنه لا يكون مقصوداً أصلاً بالذات ولا بالعرض فثبوت الحكم المخالف في المستثنى بطريق المنطوق فليس كمفهوم اللقب فافهم، وقد أطنبنا الكلام في هذا المقام وإن أفضى إلى التكرار لما أنه كان قد ارتكز في أذهان الفحول من العلماء أن قول الحنفية في تجويز تعليل المخصص دون الاستثناء، وكون الأول موجباً للظنية دون الثاني شيء فري حتى سمعت بعض من يشار إليهم بالبنان يقول قولاً لا يليق بمن له حسن وأدب بالراسخين الكرام أن يتفوه من فيمن وصلوا المقامات العظام والله الهادي وبه الاعتصام.
The third article on linguistic principles. That (the definitiveness) is not from the wording itself, but only from outside. If the number were definitive, it would be definitiveness from outside, and here the outside—the exception—exists, indicating that another meaning is intended, so it becomes definitive in the remainder after the exception. It is not unlikely to say: the meaning of the definitiveness of the number is the impossibility of metaphorical usage from what it is placed for to a lower or higher level; in summary, it is not permissible to apply one number to another, and it does not admit this kind of metaphor. The Arabic linguists have stated this, so there is no room for denial. Then it may be argued for the original claim that if the intended meaning of the excepted-from were the remainder metaphorically, then the definitive texts—i.e., the explicative ones—would not remain explicative; the possibility of exception would remain there, and thus the possibility of metaphor would remain. The answer in al-Tahrir applies perfectly: this definitiveness is from outside, and when there is an exception, it becomes definitive in the remainder. The most apparent is to say: just as in the explicative (mufassar) the possibility of another metaphor from outside is nullified, likewise the possibility of exception is also nullified from outside—so reflect. Al-Qadi said: The composite (al-majmu‘)—i.e., ten except three—is placed in exchange for seven. That is, the excepted-from along with the tool of exception and the excepted part is placed in exchange for the remainder, just as two names—a simple and a compound—are placed for one thing. The speech of a group of Hanafis, indeed their verifiers, among them Sadr al-Shariah (may Allah have mercy on him), inclined to this. I say: It would follow from this that for each number there would be infinite names, for the ranks of numbers are not limited; and every number, when that which increases it over a certain number is excepted from it, that number remains. You have said: The composite is placed for the remainder. So it necessarily follows that all ranks, with the exception of what increases them over a number, are placed in exchange for it—so reflect. For the impossibility of the necessary consequence is not hidden. How not? Infinite composite words have been placed by type-based assignment (al-wad‘ al-naw‘i) in exchange for non-exhaustive meanings. Also, just as it is permissible to place one word in exchange for infinite meanings by one assignment, likewise the opposite is permissible—so understand. It is also refuted by the necessary consequence of the pronoun in “except half of it” referring to a part of the word, because “the slave-girl except half of her” becomes like Ma‘dikarib in that case, and “the slave-girl” is a part of it. (They said: this after understanding from al-Qadi’s statement that “the slave-girl except half of her” becomes of the type of Ba‘labakk. So where is the pronoun so that it refers? Rather, the pronoun in that case is like the zay of Zayd. Yes, it was originally a pronoun referring, and it was not a part of the word in that case—so understand.) And it is refuted by the necessary consequence of specification (takhsis) and its giving a ruling contrary to the implication of the title (mafhum al-laqab). For in that case it would give a ruling on a generic noun, negating the ruling from what is other than it—and this is rejected by the majority, although some who are not considered adhere to it. And it is refuted by the necessary consequence of composition (tarkib)—i.e., composing the word from three words. I say: rather, composition from four words in cases like “thirty except eleven.” And it—i.e., composing a name from three while it is not in the narrative (ghayr al-mahki) like Ta’abbat Sharran—is permissible. And the first case (al-hal al-awwal) is not a genitive construction (ghayr mudaf) like Abu ‘Abdillah, for it is permissible by agreement, and it is not inflected (mu‘rab). Its apparent meaning indicates that the prohibition of composition from three is only when the first is not inflected, and here it is similar, so it should not be prohibited—contrary to the claim. The more apparent in the expression is that the first is inflected and not a genitive construction. It is better to take it from ta‘rib (Arabization), indicating the permissibility of that in nouns transferred from foreign languages, and there is no letter contrary to language by induction. Then, since these objections only apply if al-Qadi intended that they are words composed and made into one word—and al-Qadi’s statement attributed to him with this intention, disregarding the necessity of those impossibilities, is (a name) contrary to intuition, because it is certain that individual words remain in their original assignments. Know that the author (musannif) has accepted intuition that individual words remain in their original assignments. Then the statement that ten is used in the sense of seven becomes false—so reflect. (Interpret) his statement (that his intention is that the composite is real in seven and that it is placed by the type-based assignment which is for compounds in exchange for seven—meaning that the individual words are used in their assigned meanings, and the outcome of the composite is a meaning that is true of seven, which is ten minus three—and it is nothing but seven, (nothing else comes to mind). The author said, agreeing with what is in al-Tahrir and others: This reverts to one of the two madhhabs. But reversion to the first madhhab is not correct, for the first madhhab’s outcome is that the excepted-from is metaphorical for the remainder, while in this (interpretation) the excepted-from is used in what it is placed for—so how is this compatible with that? As for the second madhhab, you know it admits two possibilities: one is that the ruling is on some of the excepted-from, and the whole is mentioned due to the qarina of exception. On this, there is no reversion to it, for its outcome is that the ruling is on individuals of which this composite, indicated by this compound, is true. Based on this, Sadr al-Shariah divided (the positions) and said the madhhabs are three, and he chose the last, which is al-Qadi’s. Then what is in al-Talwih—that the indication of the remainder by type-based assignment of the compound is agreed upon by all, but the discussion is about the manner of indication, so this madhhab is not a subdivision of the remaining madhhabs—is not valid. And it is surprising how it was hidden from him that if the intended meaning of the excepted-from is the remainder metaphorically by the qarina of exception, then where is the type-based assignment of the compound? Rather, the indication is only for the excepted-from, not for the compound. And if the second madhhab is interpreted with this possibility, then the reversion (to it) is (correct). It has become clear to you from what we have recited to you repeatedly that the first madhhab is definitively false, and the third madhhab is the sound truth. Its outcome is that the excepted-from is in its real meaning, and the excepted part is excluded from it by the tool, so this composite becomes a composite concept expressed by it for the remainder, and the compound word is placed in exchange for this composite concept by type-based assignment, as has been mentioned repeatedly. And that the second madhhab, if interpreted in this way, is true, otherwise it is false and contains useless talk. It has also become clear to you that this composition indicates the remainder by assignment (wad‘). It has preceded that the indicated-by-assignment is definitive (qat‘i), and that this indication does not depend on the ruling of the excepted part, as in takhsis. So the ruling of the excepted part does not oppose this ruling on the remainder, as we have presented—so reflect. We also say: The mentioning of ten, then restricting it with what benefits the exclusion of some, then judging on what is true of this composite—this is an indication that the ruling of the excluded is contrary to this ruling. That is, the contrary ruling is understood implicitly, not that it is not intended at all, either primarily or secondarily. So the contrary ruling in the excepted part is established by way of the explicit wording (mantuq), so it is not like the implication of the title (mafhum al-laqab)—so understand. We have elaborated speech in this place, even if it led to repetition, because there had settled in the minds of scholars that the statement of the Hanafis permitting the ta‘lil (reason-giving) of the mukhassis (specificr) but not of the exception, and that the first necessitates probability (zann) while the second does not—this is something fabricated. Until I heard some of those pointed at with fingers saying something unbecoming for one of good character and etiquette among the firmly grounded noble scholars to utter about those who have reached great stations. Allah is the Guide, and in Him is our protection.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh debate about the linguistic status of exception clauses (istithna'), specifically using the example 'ten except three' to mean 'seven'. The author begins by arguing that the definitiveness of a number is not intrinsic to the word but comes from an external factor—here, the exception itself. If the number were considered definitive on its own, then the exception would be the external element clarifying that another meaning (the remainder) is intended. The author then presents the position of al-Qadi, who claimed that the entire composite expression 'ten except three' is a name for the number seven, acting like a compound word. This view was shared by a group of Hanafis, including Sadr al-Shariah. The author raises several objections: (1) This would imply infinite names for numbers since any number can be expressed as a larger number minus an exception. (2) It leads to pronoun reference issues in examples like 'the slave-girl except half of her' if treated as a single word. (3) It would imply the assignment of a ruling through the 'implication of the title' (mafhum al-laqab), which is rejected by the majority. (4) It requires composition from three words, which is rare but not impossible. However, the author notes that the impossibility of the necessary consequences is not absolute, as infinite compound words are possible by type-based assignment (al-wad' al-naw'i). The discussion then moves to the three madhhabs (positions) regarding the meaning of the excepted-from: (1) The excepted-from is used metaphorically for the remainder. (2) The ruling is on some individuals of the excepted-from, with the whole mentioned due to the qarina. (3) The excepted-from is used in its real meaning, and the exception excludes some, so the composite expression as a whole denotes the remainder by type-based assignment. The author definitively rejects the first madhhab and shows that the third is correct. Crucially, this third madhhab has an usul implication: the indication of the composite expression for the remainder is by the original assignment (wad'), which is definitive (qat'i), unlike takhsis which is probabilistic (zanni). The author emphasizes that this does not rely on the ruling of the excepted part, and the contrary ruling for the excepted part is derived from the explicit wording (mantuq), not from the implication of the title. This distinction is important because some scholars mistakenly thought the Hanafis allow ta'lil (giving a reason) for the mukhassis but not for the exception, which the author refutes as fabrication.

Key Terms
المستثنى منه (al-mustathnā minhu): The number (or noun) from which exception is made; e.g., 'ten' in 'ten except three'.
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): The linguistic act of excluding part of the statement using tools like 'except' (illa).
الوضع النوعي (al-wad‘ al-naw‘ī): A type-based linguistic assignment where a pattern or rule applies to a whole category of expressions, such as compound words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third madhhab preferred by the author, how does the phrase 'ten except three' indicate the number seven?
A) The word 'ten' is used metaphorically for seven.
B) The composite phrase as a whole is a compound word placed by type-based assignment to denote seven.
C) The exception merely a qarina indicating that the ruling applies to some individuals of ten.
D) The word 'three' is used metaphorically for seven.

Key Takeaway
Exception clauses (istithna') in the Hanafi school are treated as definitive indicators of the remainder by type-based assignment (al-wad' al-naw'i), making them qat'i in usul.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Condition of Continuity in Exception and Its Proofs
A usul discussion establishing the condition of continuity in exception with Hanafi proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for the validity of an exception (istithna') and identify the proofs used to support the condition of immediate connection.

Core Idea
Exception must be immediately connected — delay destroys certainty in speech and contracts.

This lesson examines a fundamental usul rule: the exception (istithna') must be immediately connected to the general statement. The passage presents the Hanafi position, opposing views from Ibn Abbas and others, and robust proofs including consensus, logical necessity, and hadith evidence. Understanding this condition is key to correctly applying exceptions in oaths, contracts, and legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its condition
The condition for exception is continuity (ittisal), i.e., immediate connection to the beginning of speech. Customary interruptions (coughing) do not break continuity; only embarking on other speech does.
2
Examine the opposing views
Review the reports from Ibn Abbas allowing delay up to a month/year/lifetime, the opinion allowing delay only in the Quran, and the view allowing delay within the same session (Hasan al-Basri, Tawus).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The text offers two main proofs: (1) Consensus of literary scholars that delayed exception is nonsense; (2) Without continuity, certainty in speech and contracts becomes impossible. Additional proofs from the story of Prophet Ayyub and the hadith on oaths.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If delay were allowed, contracts like pledges of allegiance would not be binding; the Hanafi position ensures legal certainty.

ولو قرر الدليل من بدء الأمر بأنه لو صح التأخير في الاستثناء لما علم يمين يكون نقضه واجباً مع الكفارة وقت رؤية غير المحلوف عليه خيراً، والتالي باطل أما الملازمة فلاحتمال إلحاق الاستثناء، وأما بطلان التالي فلأنه لا يبقى شيء فلا لا يبعد
The third article: Linguistic principles. Issue: The condition of exception is continuity, i.e., its connection to the beginning of the speech, even if the connection is customary, such that it is considered connected in custom. So interruption by a cough, for example, or other excuses does not harm the exception, but interruption by embarking on another speech harms it, because that is considered abandonment and turning away customarily. And it is narrated from Ibn Abbas about his disagreement multiple reports: in one report, delay is valid up to a month; in another, up to a year; in another, up to a lifetime. This is in the Hashiyah. And due to its extreme improbability, or the innocence of someone like Ibn Abbas from uttering such an improbable statement, let alone adopting it as a madhhab, what is narrated from him is interpreted as Imam Ahmad said: delay with intention is valid by analogy to other specificrs. This analogy is only valid for those who allow delay of the specifier. And it may be established by analogy to other connected things, and this is even more excessive. If you say: then it should be valid to delay the condition by intention as well. I say: it is not refuted by the condition, as in al-Minhaj, because they say the condition is delayed just as the exception, so there is no agreement, so no compulsion. And it was said: separation is valid in exception in the Quran specifically, not elsewhere, due to what is narrated in the verse: {Not equal are those believers who sit [at home] except the disabled and those who strive in the cause of Allah} [4:95]. And the phrase 'except the disabled' was not revealed initially; it was revealed after a period, and the complaint of Abdullah ibn Umm Maktum and others. And it can be refuted that the intended meaning of 'those who sit' is those who are obligated to jihad, and that is known from the necessity of religion, because what is immediately understood from 'sitting' is sitting from performing an obligation, and it is not said customarily of a bankrupt that he sat from Hajj and Zakat. So His statement {except the disabled} is not a specifier nor an exception, but rather an explanatory statement that it is permissible to occur as an emphatic state, and its separation is agreed upon, so it is not part of our discussion. The author said: It appears that it is like the statement of al-Abbas: 'except al-Idhkhir' when the Messenger of Allah forbade cutting the trees and plants of Mecca, for his statement is connected to an omitted part. And it should not escape you that then the meaning is: 'Not equal are the believers who sit absolutely except the disabled', so it becomes an exclusion from a general ruling, and that can only be by abrogation, which is invalid because it is a report. Also, the ruling of jihad was not general for the disabled, unless it is said that the first ruling was specific, then this ruling came with the exception as an affirmation. So understand. And it was said: delay is valid as long as the session continues, which is the saying of the crown of the saints al-Hasan al-Basri and Tawus, as in al-Tahrir. Our proof: First, the consensus of literary scholars on the necessity of connection between the exception and the main statement. For this reason, if someone says: 'I owe ten', then after a month adds 'except three', it is considered nonsense customarily by consensus, so it cannot be connected to what preceded. Second, if connection were not necessary, one could not be certain of truth or falsehood in any report due to the possibility of exception. For if the generality was in fact true, the possibility of falsehood remains due to the exception; otherwise, the possibility of truth remains. And in contracts and dissolutions, such as sale and divorce, one could not be certain of binding due to the possibility of a modifying exception. It is reported that Imam Abu Hanifa repelled the reproach of al-Mansur al-Dawaniqi, the second of the Abbasid caliphs, regarding his opposition to his grandfather Ibn Abbas in this issue. For Ibn Abbas allowed delaying the exception while the Imam prevented it, based on the non-binding nature of the pledge of allegiance—the people's pledge to accept his leadership. This story indicates that Ibn Abbas's madhhab was well-known among the people. And in al-Taysir, al-Mansur's reproach was due to the slander of Muhammad ibn Ishaq, the author of al-Maghazi. This is far from someone like him, and even if the slander attribution were true, he is one whose narration is absolutely not accepted, as some have held regarding his lack of reliability, for slandering to a tyrant is a great sin, especially the slandering of such an Imam regarding a fatwa that was true and in the cause of Allah. And he argued for the chosen position: First, if delay were allowed, Allah would not have prescribed for the oath of Ayyub (peace be upon him and our Prophet) regarding his striking his wife—Hasanah bint Yusuf or Rahmah bint Ibrahim ibn Yusuf—when she delayed his need, one hundred lashes after recovery, to take a bundle of sticks. The object of 'did not prescribe' is 'taking the bundle', meaning if delay were allowed, it would not be necessary for fulfilling the oath to take a bundle containing more than one hundred sticks and strike with it; rather, the exception would be better because the oath would be invalid, so he would not need to fulfill it. Second, if delay were allowed, the Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and companions) would not have said: 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees something better than it, let him expiate his oath and do that other thing,' narrated by Muslim from Abu Hurayrah. Rather, he would have given a choice between exception and expiation, and the former is better because it is easier, and his noble practice was to choose the easier for the community. And the intended meaning in the argument is absolutely, i.e., if delay were allowed, he would not have absolutely prescribed expiation; rather, exception would be valid in some cases by intention, and expiation prescribed in others. So what was said that this proof does not stand against those who allow delay with intention is refuted. Then this response does not apply if raised against the first proof: for the obligation of taking the bundle and striking with it for the oath in a specific incident where intention was not mentioned; so it is possible that his prescription was due to the lack of intention, so it is not valid against those who allow delay with intention. And one could say: this is refuted by the case of connected exception, for if it were allowed, expiation would not be specified, rather connected exception would be valid in what allows exception, and expiation in what does not. The solution is that the oath to which exception is attached, whether connected or delayed, is not an actual oath that includes the exempted item, because he spoke after the exception, so then the exception is not valid, and the oath is binding on the exempted item. As for what the oath is binding on, expiation is prescribed. The argument from the hadith for the impermissibility of delay: so reflect. And the strength of this speech is not hidden. But it can be answered that there is a difference between delayed and connected exception, because in the former the oath is apparently binding, unlike the latter, and the context of the hadith is the oath that is apparently binding; otherwise, seeing something better would not necessitate breaking the oath and expiation, for the oath only becomes binding if there was no exception, and that is hidden because he might intend the exception. So if the intended meaning in the hadith is the apparently binding oath, then the argument stands: if delay were allowed, the apparent oath would not necessarily require breaking and expiation; rather, exception would also be valid, and it is easier. And if we concede, we say: the hadith is specific to cases where exception is not connected, due to consensus on its validity, so there is no specification to the restricted case that delayed exception is valid due to lack of consensus there. And if the argument were set from the beginning that if delay in exception were valid, no oath would be known to require breaking and expiation when seeing something better than what was sworn, and the consequent is false. As for the entailment, it is due to the possibility of attaching the exception. As for the falsity of the consequent, because nothing remains. So it is not far.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the condition of continuity (al-ittisal) in the exception (al-istithna'), presenting the Hanafi position, opposing views, and proofs. The text begins by stating the rule: exception must be immediately connected to the beginning of the speech. Customary breaks like coughing do not break continuity, but any interruption with other speech nullifies the exception. It then reports various views attributed to Ibn Abbas allowing delay up to a month, a year, or a lifetime. The author dismisses these as improbable or misattributed, suggesting they be interpreted as delay by intention (niyya) following Imam Ahmad's qiyas. Another opinion allows separation only in the Quran, citing the verse on the disabled and jihad. The author refutes this by explaining the verse is not a true exception but an explanatory state. A third opinion, from Hasan al-Basri and Tawus, allows delay within the same session. The Hanafi proof begins with two arguments: first, the consensus of literary scholars that interruption breaks the connection; second, that without continuity, one cannot be certain of truth or falsehood in reports or the binding nature of contracts. The story of Imam Abu Hanifa with Caliph al-Mansur illustrates the practical implication: if delay were allowed, pledges of allegiance would not be binding. The final proof uses two evidences: (1) the story of Prophet Ayyub, where Allah prescribed taking a bundle of sticks to fulfill his oath, showing that exception must be connected because otherwise the oath would be invalid and he would not need that method; (2) the hadith commanding expiation for oaths, implying that exception cannot be delayed because otherwise expiation would not be necessary. The discussion then addresses objections, particularly the distinction between connected and delayed exception in oaths, concluding that the hadith applies only to apparently binding oaths where no exception is made. Overall, the text establishes the Hanafi rule that exception must be immediately connected to be valid.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): A linguistic exception that excludes some individuals from the general statement, e.g., 'except three' after 'I owe ten'.
الاتصال (al-ittiṣāl): The immediate connection of the exception to the beginning of the speech without interruption by foreign speech.
الاستثناء المؤخر (al-istithnā' al-mu'akhkhar): An exception that is made after a gap or after other speech, considered invalid in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for the validity of an exception (istithna') according to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage?
A) It must be immediately after the general statement without interruption by other speech.
B) It can be delayed up to one year if intended.
C) It must be made within the same sitting.
D) It is only valid in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires exception to be immediately connected to the general statement; otherwise, it is invalid.
